THE RED PAGE
HORI GREY
WORTHY of its great subject is the
biography that Geo. 0. Henderson,
Professor of History at Adelaide
University, has written under the title of
/Sir George Greg, Pioneer of Empire in
southern Lands (Dent, London). No other
man in history rendered the Empire such
varied services, and his was the great privi-
lege to lay firm and broad the foundations of
nationhood in two continents. And though
“shunted” by Downing Street, and, as
Henderson justly points out, for good
(official) reasons, Grey had the satisfaction
of an honored old age of retirement. Grey
was the ideal Crown Colony governor, and
his waned with the waxing of the
representative .government idea ; though,
paradoxically, it was he. who made that
ideal of State nationhood possible. Rebel,
autocrat, socialist, diplomat, writer, soldier’
explorer, governor, premier, federationist’
Privy Councillor, he had all the elements of a
great man, and his greatness is confirmed by
the fact that the ideals hq so strenuously
championed have but grown with his death.
Henderson s work is to be commended for
his insistence upon the value and strength
of Grey’s influence. The biographer lias
well indicated how ideal forces operate in
the field of statesmanship in building up new
nations. And Henderson’s claim that Grey
was the founder of far-reaching policies in
South Africa, Australia and Maoriland,
stamped with the impress of his personality,
is more than just.
Grey was a child of the nineteenth century
renaissance. And nothing impresses the studen ;
of his career more than his unbounded faith in
the possibilities of human nature, his deep and
lasting sympathy for the masses of the people, and
his splendid devotion to the welfare of the native
races in the Southern Hemisphere.
It is necessary only to summarise his
efforts for the upbuilding of a sturdy nation-
alism to show chat we are still but following
in the tracks he blazed. Closer settlement,
one man one vote, the education of the
ma'sses —these he put in his Maoriland pro-
gramme as far back as 1876. In 1843 he
was opposed to loans in South Australia. In
his first Administration in M.L. he fought
the big landowners, and the warfare cul-
minated in his policy of “ bursting up the
big estates,” and the Compulsory Land Pur-
chase Bill of his enthusiastic disciple, Sed-
don. Most of the things he fought for we
have already got, or are in process of getting.
But there are several big ideals that were
checked by the inertness of Downing Street.
The Federation o'f the Pacific he advocated
at a time when the Pacific was to let;
and he had it in his power, had he been un-
trammelled, to federate South Africa. He was
the first statesman to foresee the necessity
for that confederation. He advocated it as
early as 1858. Henderson points out that
had Grey’s scheme not been blocked by
Downing Street there would have been no
Boer war. But Britain deliberately refused
the Orange River’s petition to return'to
British sovereignty. Grey argued that the
best way to foster Imperial unity was to
encourage self-government in its completest
form, even to the election by the States of
their own governors. His programme of
Imperial federation also took in the neces-
sity for Irish Home Rule. The principle of
colonial nationalism is now practically ad-
mitted by Britain. And Grey’s continual
insistence upon the necessity for the educa-
tion of the people has not been lost sight of
by us. It is a great heritage that Grey has
left.
He was an autocrat by descent: he became
a radical by experience. On military ser-
vice in Ireland he deeply felt the miseries
entailed by large estates and the absence of
opportunities for the peasants. Against
these two intertwined evils his political life
was one persistent and successful struggle.
His life this side of the line began with his
explorations in the North and West of Aus-
tralia ; and his character was early recog-
nised by the British Government. He was
the strong man they could call upon at all
times when there were great difficulties to
face. He raised South Australia from bank-
ruptcy to prosperity. As High Commis-
sioner for South Africa he averted a native
war ; his strong hand was twice successfully
requisitioned in Maoriland. But he
needed freedom from interference, and
the conditions favored him. For in
those days the Governor was a despot.
It took from twelve to eighteen months to
get a reply to a despatch, and'Grey took full
advantage of the respite, and when he dis-
approved of the Home Government’s positive
orders, would craftily write for fuller instruc-
tions and meantime go, chuckling, on his
autocratic way. Absolutely without fear, he
never failed to set his own convictions against
the policy of the British Cabinet. He
hung up ” for five years the constitution
sent by Earl Grey to Maori land, on the
ground that the time was not opportune ;
but in that constitution he tried to make the
Upper House elective, and won from the
British Cabinet a great extension of the
franchise. His “ high acts of disobedience ”
to the Home Government were indeed suf-
ficiently explicit and sufficiently frequent to
make him an awkward servant. He publicly
encouraged the Orange River petitioners
while the stupid British Colonial policy did not
want them ; against positive orders, he
arranged with a private German firm for the
immigration of 4000 German settlers to South
Africa, and expended £107,000 over the
amount allowed him for that experiment in
colonisation. He ignored important des-
patches, or gained time by equivocal replies.
But his point of view was that of a statesman
and a prophet. In his despatches he wrote:
The British Empire is so vast, and so unwieldy,
that it is all-important that the whole world
should see it has not overgrown its strength ; but
that it possesses quite as much energy and power
at its extremities as at its centre ; and that if any
vital portion of it is seriously endangered, all parts
of it can, without communicating with the centre,
simultaneously stir themselves to meet the emer-
gency as if each part were the head and centre of
action for the whole body.
That was Grey’s defence for his splendid
act of disobedience by which he contributed
largely to the saving of India for the Empire.
At the outbreak of the Mutiny, not only did
he despatch to India every available soldier
and horse within Cape Colony, but, upon his
own authority, diverted to India a contingent
of British troops that had happened to
touch at the Cape under orders to proceed to
China. The importance of this, the most
valuable and also the most dramatic incident
in Grey’s career, is, it seems to us, not suf-
ficiently emphasised in Henderson’s able
work.
The difficult situation that can be created
by the presence of over-sea soldiers in a
colony is evident in Henderson’s account of
the muddle of the Maori campaign. Though
Grey was General Cameron’s superior officer,
the British officer evidently regarded the
War Office as his supreme authority. The
General, who had 10,0(X) regular soldiers
under his command, declined to pursue the
enemy far into the interior ; the M.L.
Government believed it was the only possible
way to exhaust the Maories and end the war.
From the slowness of his marches and his
reluctance to leave the coast the Maories
called him “the lame sea-gull.” Yet
Cameron was a brave man ; but
troops trained for European war are
not fitted for warfare of this kind. That
fundamental fact was proved in the Trans-
vaal ; it would be confirmed if ever Aus-
tralia had to depend upor Imperial troops
for its defence. The capture of Wereroa pa,
upon the Wanganui, furnishes a striking
example of the value of local knowledge and
colonial initiative. General Cameron, on
being requested by Grey to capture this pa,
stated that he would require at least 2000
more men. Grey quietly got to work, and
with a small force of 473 men took the pa,
capturing 50 natives without the loss of a
man.
Such was Hori Grey, friend of the natives
and of the people.
The key to Grey’s character is not to be found
in a love of power; but rather in a passion for
public service.
And an understanding of Grey’s later days,
when he stepped down from the Governor-
ship of Maoriland to champion the cause of
the people in Parliament, when he became
Premier, and founded the still growing
Liberal party, when at last he quarrelled
with all his confreres, and found himself a
solitary leader without a party, is to be found
in this pronouncement by Henderson :
The “vicious mole” of Grey’s nature was an
official intractability that broke down the pales
and forts of reason, undermined his self-control,
and left him the prey of arrogance, vanity, and
personal resentment,
Always he was a rebel, a splendid rebel.
But to rebels all the progress and uplifting
of this world is due ; and Australasia has
most cause to be thankful for the long
rebellion that was his life. Hori Grey died
worth only £5OOO, leaving libraries of in-
estimable value to the people whom he
loved, in whom he fiercely believed.

A good idea of the magnitude of Australia’s
resources can begot from The Golden West (Is.),
the third issue of an annual from Perth. This
illustrated compilation deals chiefly 'with the
little-known North West corner of the continent.
It comprises a history of explorations, and
descriptions of the great pearling, mining, and
pastoral industries of the hitherto hardly
developed North West.

U'OJI.I ,Y
By the authority of her son, a selection from
the correspondence of Queen Victoria, ending
with the death of her husband, has been edited
by Arthur C. Benson and a prefixed person named
Esher, and published, in sumptuous style, by
Murray (three vols. £3 35.). These letters afford
many interesting side-lights upon British and
European politics for a period of 25 years ; but, to
the general reader at this side of the globe,
they are more interesting for the view they ex-
hibit of the private life of an ordinary woman
occupying, through no fault of her own, a difficult
position. Even so simple a business as courtship
was, for her, an awkward matter. Two of her
German cousins, Ernest and Albert, were sent
over to England on appro., and though
Ernest must have felt the importance of
being eldest, Victoria preferred the younger
brother. Of “ dear Albert,” her much-underlined
“ earthly angel,” she gives this first impression :
“ He possesses every quality that could be desired
to make me perfectly happy. . . . He has, besides,
the most pleasing and delightful exterior and
appearance you can possibly see.” And she con-
veys the news of her engagement to her uncle, the
King of the Belgians, thus :
My mind is quite made up—and I told Albert
this morning of it; the warm affection he showed
me on learning this gave me great pleasure. . . .
I love him more than I can say, and I shall do
everything in my power to render the sacrifice he
has made (for a sacrifice in my opinion it
is) as small as I can. He seems to have a
very great tact—a very necessary thing in
his position. These last few days have passed like
a dream to me, and I am so much bewildered
by it all that I hardly know how to write; but I
feel very, very happy.” All much underlined —
exactly the sort of letter Maud would dash off
on her knee to her uncle Bill when Charles set her
mind at rest by asking her to name the date. And
all through Victoria’s correspondence she exhibits
the same sane conventional femininv point of view.
She was, above all things, domestic, and, mated
with a tame and matter-of-fact German husband,
she was perfectly happy in her hqme with her
ever-increasing family. But she was always the
Queen, and even before her marriage she had
some trouble in making Albert understand that
she, and not he, meant to ohoose his household
for him. Albert didn’t like this, but at length
reluctantly assented to her appointment even of
his secretary. One cannot help feeling sorry for
Albert.
Victoria’s relations to her Prime Ministers are
full of interest. Lord Palmerston began by
treating her as a woman who would naturally not
want to be bothered by too much business; but
she sharply reminded him that she must see all
his important despatches. But the feminine note
pei’sisted. Her interest in her Prime Ministers
had always something of sentiment; she felt their
resignation like a personal loss. She was always
characteristically conservative, hating changes,
even of government. Interesting, too, are the
glimpses this correspondence gives of the opinion
of monarchs of the existence of that troublesome
fact, the People. These royal correspondents
regard things like the People and the Parliament
rather as an estimable maiden lady might look on
a big house-dog that occasionally growled at her.
It was a useful thing to have in the house, but
some day it might bite. But Victoria’s concep-
tion of loyalty is charmingly naive. She gossips
to another monarch: “'The feeling of loyalty in
this country is happily very strong, and wherever
we show ourselves we are most heartily and
warmly received, and the civilities arid respect
shown to us by those we visit is most satisfactory.
I mention merely a trifling instance to show how
respectful they are—the Duke of Buckingham,
who is immensely proud, bringing the cup of
coffee after dinner on a waiter to Albert himself.”
You see, a queen in her quest for loyalty cannot
get any further down than a duke who acts as a
waiter.
The personal intercourse of monarchs is very
like the personal intercourse of blokes. Thus the
Queen of the Belgians writes to the Queen of
England when the King of the French, who was
the father of the Queen of the Belgians, is visiting
the Queen of England, requesting, the Queen of
England not to ask the King of the French to
breakfast. (That sentence, for a democratic paper
like this, seems rather overcrowded with monarchs.)
“It would disturb him in all his habits, and be
bad for him, as he would certainly eat, a thing he
is not used to do in the morning. . . . It would be
much better for hini if he only appeared to
luncheon and dinner, and if you kindly dispensed
him altogether of the breakfast. ... If I must tell
you all the truth, my mother is afraid also he will
eat too much. ... If by chance Lord Hardwicke
was in waiting during my father’s stay, you must
kindly put my father in mind to thank him for
the famous cheese, which arrived safely, and was
found very good.” Breakfasts and cheeses —thus
these sovereigns scribble to each other. And how
do subjects write to monarchs ? Let us take, as a
specimen of the courtier-touch, Lord Melbourne
to Victoria on the occasion of her sending him the
pictures of two dead dogs. ‘ ‘ Lord Melbourne has
never yet thanked your Majesty for the pretty
etchings of poor Islay and Eos, which your Majesty
sent to Lord Melbourne when he was last at
Windsor. Lord Melbourne has ordered them
both to be framed, and will hang them up in his
room here. They will afford Lord Melbourne
most agreeable and pleasing souvenirs of the
happiest period of his life.” One can picture
Lord Melbourne gazing with ecstatic loyalty at
the portraits of those two very dead dogs.
As an indication of the utter unimportance of
this country during the quarter of a century
covered by this correspondence, it suffices to
note that Australia is mentioned only once—
when a South Australian wine-grower sent a
present of his first vintage to the Queen, though
‘ ‘ Lord Stanley will not venture to answer for the
quality of the vintage.”
As letters, the collection is not as interesting as
the Letters of the Brownings, and scarcely as
excising as the alleged love letters that Laurence
Housman pretended were those of an English-
woman.

Some yells from “Janor” in rebuttal of the
article, “What the Australian Worker Reads”
(22/8/’07): “This township is a typical Gipps-
land mining centre (there are dozens such),
remote, difficult of access, with small postal faci-
lities, and it is the distributing point for the most
poverty-stricken of all the goldfields of the State.
The adult male population served by this town-
ship barely exceeds 200 souls, all of whom, ex-
cepting half-a-dozen business folk, are engaged in
gold-mining—there is no other industry—and this
is their printing bill: Metropolitan dailies, 50
subs. : ditto, weeklies, 90 subs. ; country news-
papers, 125 subs.; Australian and foreign maga’s
and scrap monthlies, 50 subs. There is also a large
postal delivery of papers and journals from all parts
of the earth. Also the local miners, by co-opera-
tion, have purchased 250 well-selected volumes,
and laid the foundation of a circulating library,
which is to be added to as funds accrue. These
books are housed in a public hall, built and owned
by the diggers. Amusements are plenty—for a
miners’ lawn tennis club flourishes, the rifle club
is virile, cricket and football abound, while the
gold-hunting members of the brass band can hold
their end of the trombone up with any combina-
tion of sound in the State. As for the tent, it has
vanished. During the recent flutter at Poseidon,
a few men have lived under calico, but outside of
that quite unusual happening, not one per cent,
of present-day goldminers dwell in tents. Numbers
of miners in my district have been speculating as to
which goldfield Dr. Schachner struck during his
three months’ incognito, and reckon it could only
be Poseidon, for on older fields the same condi-
tions prevail as I have described above. After
forty odd years of gold-hunting, I can affirm that
no class of Australian workers read as much as
miners, and a large percentage of them are edu-
cated or well-read men, and democrats all the
time. To the gold miner himself there is no
‘ magic ’ or ‘ romance ’ about his occupation, save
the glamor of the great forests, the wide open
spaces, the daily living close to nature ; these are
the things that hold and enthrall—not the yellow
dross. Three months generally suffices to knock
the romance off mining work ; though it is all too
short for anyone, however keen an observer, to
arrive at accurate judgment of the workers in the
four big industries of the Commonwealth over
the vast area of the three Eastern States. Hence
these yells.”
Longstaff’s big picture for the Melbourne
National Gallery, commemorating the end of
Burke’s vain attempt to cross Australia, is well
spoken of by London Athenceum. The moment
chosen by the artist shows Burke and his two
surviving companions arriving, starving, in the
camp at Cooper’s Creek, to find that the rest of
the expedition had, after waiting for them for
eighteen weeks, abandoned it on the morning of
that day. The critic gives Longstaff praise for
his success upon a colossal scale of what must
practically remain only an exact historical illus-
tration. This success is due mainly to the sobriety
of his color-scheme, and to the impressive stand-
ing figure of Burke—“a powerful presentation of
utter despair, still rebellious against the Fates.”
But in parts the composition is weak, and so large
a canvas is said to require a “tighter handling.”
Apparently Longstaff has executed his difficult
task worthily.
“Jaybell” speculates: “That ‘Red Page’
notice of Percival Lowell’s book on Mars has set
me thinking. What’s wrong with my theory
that that planet may be Tophet or Gehenna or
Purgatory or Limbo? Its vegetable production
appears to be rather too limited to suggest
Arcadia or Paradise, or good pine-apple territory.
I haven’t any fault to find on the score of its
crusty old age, but it seems to me there’* a flavor of
addled pints and crawfish in the scarlet folio’s
idea of a Martian population of highly civilised
nosegays. To my mind, thei’e is every indication
that the multitude up in the sister planet consists
of brawny Chows who got tired of remarking
‘ Whaffor?’ here below when the lettuce wouldn’t
sprout, and who missed the Chinaman’s pro-
verbial luck when they were wafted to the other
sphere. But, Chow-like, they assimilated the
desperate situation at a glance, and there-
upon bucked in and perfected a reticulation
scheme that is now the marvel of any enthusiast
who cares to look at it through a lens
till his eye bulges in the middle and sags at the
sides. It fairly catches my breath, and makes
me thirsty, contemplating the magnitude of the
wet task undertaken by the Martian Chows to
bring the cabbagee to perfection and the callot to
a full sense of its own crisp importance. And all
that effort for a few thread-like drills of garden
stuff! By the noeent cucumber (and that is a
deep-green vegetable oath), .1 am on the brink of
a conclusion that Mars is the real, material,
genuine Hell, and Johnny Wun Lung is slowly,
but surely, reclaiming its "desolate vastness. The
usually accepted idea of a warm perdition is likely
to be exploded just as soon as Mars gives up its
secrets to scientific inquiry. And it won’t come
as a shock to me to learn that earth’s contribution
of lost souls is freezing considerably in the
Martian backblocks, and buying John’s forced
vegetables at famine prices. Give a Chow a lease,
a kerosene tin or two, and a hoe, and he can turn
any Hell’s blazes of a wilderness into a plotch of
greens. He can convert it quicker than he
throws off the perfume of China from his
domestic arrangements. If it were only possible
at this stage to dissect the smell of Mars, I think
my Chow theory would resolve itself into an
actual fact.”

HAIR DRILL
OUR READERS INVITED TO CARRY OUT
A WEEK’S FREE TEST.
Remarkable Results Promised.
Have you ever heard of “ hair drill ” ? No.
Well, every hair of your head is a little soldier,
The duty it shares with its more or less numer-
ous fellows is that of maintaining the tempera-
ture of your brain at normal. Without a well
trained head of hair your brain must become too
hot in summer and too cold in winter, and you
have headaches when the sun shines and colds
in the head when the sun doesn’t shine. _ This is
considering the subject of your hair quite apart
from the aspect of mere appearance, important
as this is in itself.
"How can you drill vour hair?”
Well, are you willing to try ? Are you willing
to test the drilling of your hair for one week, if it
entails no more expense than the trivial amount
of 3d. ? “Yes,” you say
All right; now, will you just note that the
present condition of your hair is either one or
more of the following:
1. Dry,
2. Brittle,
3. Lustreless,
4. Losing: Colour,
5. Falling:,
6. Scurfy,
7. Greasy,
8. Slightly Odorous,
or
9. Thin or Patchy,
If you can candidly admit that your hair does
come under one or more of the foregoing descrip-
tions, you certainly ought not to let slip this
opportunity of so easily and so cheaply finding
out what the right kind of care for your hair wd
do for you —you certainly ought to try one week’s
free test drill of your hair. Do you not think so ?
The week’s test will produce results that will
open your eyes to the possibilities of proper
hair drill.
The trial, short as it is, will yet show you— ■
That your hair can be drilled into fine
condition.
That your hair need not be too brittle or
too limp.
That your hair need not be too lustreless
or too shiny.
That it need not be too highly coloured or
too colourless.
That it need not fall out when brushed or
combed.
That it certainly need not be scurfy,
greasy or odorous.
The one week's trial, for which the enterpris-
ing proprietors of the famous preparation
Edwards’ “ Harlene” are willing to send a suffi-
cient supply to every reader of this paper, will
also show another remarkable effect. It will
show how important to the welfare of your brain
is the condition of your hair, and before the end
of the week you will revel in a sense of mental
lightness and alertness. Your test will not be
speculative in the slightest degree. The special
free bottle of “ Harlene ” placed at your disposal
for the purpose of the test is used by most of
those members of Royalty and Society who are
noted for beautiful hair.
The week’s test is open to both sexes and all
ages.
Why not send to-day, and thus place yourself
in a position to commence the week’s trial at once.
A strong word of caution should be given
against taking internal remedies, which will ruin
the constitution, and cheap, worthless remedies
which will ruin the hair.
HARLENE is stocked by all Chemists and Stores.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oh, Come Ye Back to Arcady!
The burden of my songs shall be,
“ Oh, come ye back to Arcady ! ”
And I will call you, in my lays,
To sweeter and to simpler ways.
For, ever in sequestered dells
The godlike soul of Shakspeare dwells,;
And there the sordid world forgets
By banks of thyme and violets.
And still, for ever sweet and young,
The pensive songs that Milton sung
By copses where the hawthorn blows,
With eglantine and climbing rose.
And Virgil here came home at last
To sing his Georgies ere he passed ;
Wherein the ancient perfume clings—
The attar of the odes he sings.
And Sidney, quaint in lore and loves,
Shall ever haunt Arcadian groves ;
And Wordsworth wanders in the woods
In search of rustic solitudes.
And here, in withes of woodbine bound,
Shall old John liuskin’s ghost be found —
A kindly ghost, deploring yet
Man’s growing strife, and needs, and fret.
Bucolic peace and sylvan shade,
With country dance of youth and maid ;
When Phyllis trips along the mead,
And Strephon trills his ribbon’d reed.
What time Diana, swift of feet,
Goes flashing through the woodlands sweet;
And wild god Pan his music shrills
To wake young Echo in the hills !
What time the farms are wrapt in sleep,
With yarded kine and folded sheep;
In lonely brakes the plovers call—
Is this not best, the best of all ?
Therefore I call you, in my lays,
To sweeter find to simpler ways ;
And my song’s burden still shall be :
“ Oh, come ye back to Arcady !”
VIC.
C. G. A. COLLES.
Childless.
They haunt me through the sleepless hours, the
sons I dreamed to have,
The race that might have sprung from him to sing
and love and fight;
And when at times I weep because my hopes have
come to naught
I seem to hear their voices grieving with me in the
night.
And yet if I go laughterless, sad-eyed and slow of
step,
Some mothers wonder why I grieve, their sons
have gone astray ;
Their daughters known disgrace and shame;
“ How happy you must be
Who have no child to break your heart ! ” so,
enviously, they say.
Even if my tree of love had borne only a blighted
thing,
A canker-eaten, scentless rose, a frail, unlovely
flower,
’Twere sweet to know that one through me had
felt earth’s lust of life,
And lying on my breast had known at least one
golden hour.
N.S.W.
R.B.
Winifred Brown.
I once was light-hearted, good-tempered, and gay,
Not a trouble or care to oppress me,
And I cantered through life in a rollicking way,
Thinking nought could disturb or distress me.
The world was a circus, and I was a clown,
Till Fate introduced me to Winifred Brown.
In our journey through life there are byways
unseen,
That will lead us to good or to evil;
One will lead to the right, into pathways serene,
While another will lead to the Devil.
I had choice of two tracks when I rode into town.
I turned to the left—and met Winifred Brown.
She was close to the sliprail that led to the farm
When I first saw her merry eyes glancing,
And my soul became captive at once to the charm
Of her beauty, so sweet and entrancing.
The flag of my freedom at once fluttered down ;
My heart was surrendered to Winifred Brown.
Oh ! Those halcyon days ! Like a poet’s sweet
dream—
Sweeter far than was ever invented.
Then I told her my love ; and my joy was supreme
When she bashfully blushed, and consented.
I was prouder of her than a king of his crown,
For the world was my own, with sweet Winifred
Brown.
: . , . '■ / A
All the guests were assembled. The parson was
there.
To unite us for worse or for better,
When I got a surprise, for a girl with red hair
Came and curtseyed, and gave me a letter.
It was short, it said brieflly, “ I’m not coming
down,
Will you kindly apologise ? Winifred Brown.”
Then a postscript said, “ Charley, I hope you
won’t mind,
But, I’ve met my old sweetheart, Pat Hogan.
We have made up our quarrel; he’s awfully kind,
So I’m going with him out to the Bogan.
We were married this morning. I hope you won’t
frown,
Yours, W. Hogan (nee Winifred Brown).”
N.S.W.

How to Address Betters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin ivould oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager, 'l'o send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy.” Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
ahvays is.

Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be refused.

lishers. The right to reproduce or translate any part of
the contents of this number is hereby expressly reserved by
the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where practicable,
acknowledge in the “ Correspondence" column all contri-
butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or Sketches
(those dealing with Australian subjects, and not exceeding
two columns in length, or, say, 3000 ‘words, are specially
acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed But no
MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these conditions
are observed. When MS. is fonvarded ivithout stamps
for return it ‘will be understood that the copy is to be
destroyed if not acceptable. Personal applications for
return of MS. cannot receive attention. The Bulletin
desires, further, those sending MS. to distinctly under-
stand that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

THURSDAY, DECEMBER 26, 1907.
OUR MILITARY DEFENCES.
So far, the two most conspicuous opponents of the
Deakin Government’s proposed system of com-
pulsory military training seem to be the Sydney
Telegraph and Keir Hardie, British M.P.
This ill-assorted pair represents the two chief
arguments, if they are worth calling such, against
the defence of Australia. The Telegraph is, of
course, the same old Telegraph. It talks vaguely
about “conscription,” though nothing dimly re-
sembling that has been suggested ; it might just
as appropriately speak with severity about the
worship of Baal. It weeps about “militarism,”
which is also quite remote from the case. It
assures the community that compulsory military
or naval or any other training is quite contrary to
the spirit and traditions of the British race, in
which matter it shows a lamentable ignorance
of British history. It holds forth at some
length about the advantages of a hireling
or mercenary army over a national force
armed in defence of its own hearths and homes,
It assures the community at large that Australia
won’t stand —won’t dream of standing—any pro-
posal for compulsory training, in which case it is
difficult to see why the paper should worry so
bitterly ; if the nation doesn’t like the idea, then the
idea won’t come to pass, and the matter is ended.
The Telegraph, in fact, takes the Phoenician or
Carthagenian view of the matter. It believes in
buying and selling; in being a race of fat im-
porters, dealers, traders, bottle-ohs, and so on ; in
having a little hired force to defend us while we
live at ease. And, on the other hand, Keir
Hardie babbles about arbitration, peace, Social-
ism, the millennium, the free admission of all races
to our country, and the Brotherhood of Man
generally. Between the two the case against
defence is fairly well summed up, and it is a
poor case all told.
* * * *
To take, first, the familiar theory that there is
something inherently “un-British” in compul-
sory military training. Even if this were true,
it would have no bearing whatever on the case.
Britain is not situated as we are. Britain has
44,000,000 people from among whom to raise a
voluntary or hired or mercenary army, and
44,000,000 people to pay for that army, and, so
far as concerns its own islands, which are its
main consideration, it has only 121,000 square
miles of country to defend. Australia has only
4,000,000 people, while it has 3,000,000 square
miles of country to defend. Moreover, conquest
in the case of Great Britain would only mean
absorption by another civilised white race of the
same family, the same literature, the same habits
of thought, and practically the same religion and
mode of government, and even if the compulsory
partnership lasted for centuries or for ever, it
would only mean, at the worst, a consolidation of
white races. If Britain likes to fool with
that risk it has a perfect right to do
so. But the conquest of Australia would
probably mean the loss of our white status,
the subjection of our women to a people with
whom woman is a slave and polygamy a habit,
and the replacement of Christianity by Joss or
Buddha. If Britain were extinguished by Ger-
many it would merely become a powerful member
—probably the most powerful member—of a great
white civilised empire, with the chance that
some day its subsidiary sovereign might become
the head of that empire. It doesn’t take the risk,
as we do, of having its daughters sold in the
Chinese slave markets or disposed of to the
licensed Japanese brothels. Even if the system
of depending upon a feeble little army of
mercenaries is good enough for Britain —which is
more than doubtful—it doesn’t follow that it is
good enough for Australia, any more than the fact
that the rabbit is carefully preserved and
encouraged in England proves that the multi-
tudinous bunny should be preserved and treated
as a friend and brother in Australia. Our present
rabbit policy is quite as un-British as—in fact is
far more un-British than —our proposed defence
policy, and the rabbit is quite as good an argu-
ment as the soldier.
* * * *
But as a matter of fact Britain for centuries
depended on compulsory military and naval
service. It was compulsory service that won its
land battles from Ethandune, over 1000 years ago,
down to the days of Marlborough, and even
later. And it was compulsory service that won
its naval battles until the days of Trafalgar.
Indeed, compulsory service for defence purposes
is still the law in Britain, though the law isn’t
enforced these days. The British people only
abandoned compulsory military service even in
foreign wars about a century ago, and substituted
for it the dubious habit of subsidising foreign
armies, of depending on foreign alliances, or on
hiring foreign mercenaries—the last-named prac-
tice was followed as recently as the time of the
Crimean war. But however “British” these
methods may be, they are of no possible use to
Australia. We cannot make foreign alliances for
offence and defence; the power to do so is want-
ing. Even if we could, our 4,000,000 people can’t
put up the money for subsidies to foreign allies
on the lavish scale that Britain used to adopt.
And we can’t hire a German Legion at
a few days’ notice as Britain did in the
Crimean days. A serviceable Chilian Legion
might perhaps be obtained for cash, but that is
exceedingly doubtful, for the old species of mer-
cenary soldier has almost died out; and in any
case, Chili is very far off, and in periods of great
stress the hired legion that arrives late might
almost as well not arrive at; all. Moreover, when
4,000,000 people form a lonesome white man’s out-
post, with 400,000,000 probable enemies at their
very doors, and the remote British suzerain has
very little in the shape of an army, and despair-
ingly admits that the strain of keeping up its
navy to the “ two-power standard,” which is said
to be the irreducible minimum required for safety,
is getting to be past its strength, the case may soon
be beyond being helped by treaties or alleviated by
the aid of dubious mercenaries. It is time to fall
back on the old British practice that lasted for
over 1000 years, instead of on the present British
methods which really only date from somewhere
about 1832. And compulsory personal training is
no more an infringement of liberty than com-
pulsory taxation for the support of a hired army.
Something must be compulsory—either service or
cash. And it is more manly to submit to com-
pulsory training for our own defence than to
follow the present example of Britain, which
nowadays scorns what it calls “conscription,”
and depends in great part for its defence on the
conscript forces of Japan.
» * * *
Then, in addition to the folk who babble about
thingsthat are allegedly “British” and things that
areallegedly “un-British,” there are the cranks like
Keir Sardie and his kind, who talk vaguely
about “peace,’’and blither about “arbitration,”
and hold forth at large about the “ Brotherhood
of Man.” Peace is a beautiful thing, but it cannot
be preserved by having no adequate reserve of
trained citizens to enforce order against the
possible foreign invader, any more than it can be
maintained by having no police to preserve order
against the possible local disturber. If the interests
of peace forbid us to be prepared against the Asiatic
who may break into Australia 500,000 strong, then
they equally forbid us to prepare against the
burglar who may break, one strong, into Keir
Hardie’s private domicile when he pays us a
visit. And arbitration, unfortunately, isn’t likely
to be of any use to us. The one question on which
we are likely to- quarrel with Asia is the question
of the free admission of Asiatics to Australia, and
the swamping of our white status, our civilisation,
our religion, our prospects, and everything that
we prize, by myriads of colored settlers. On
that question we can no more arbitrate than Keir
Hardie could arbitrate on a proposal to sell his
daughter, if he has one, to servitude in the
Japanese Yosliiwarra. As for the Brotherhood
of Man, it is a good phrase, but it really
means no more than would the Brotherhood
of Animal. The Chinaman, the Hindu and the
white Australian are fellow men, all presumably
the work of a common Creator, and consequently
they should, presumably, live in harmony—but
they don’t. The cow, the tiger and the boa-con-
strictor are fellow animals, all presumably the
work of a common Creator, and they should, pre-
sumably, live in harmony—but they don’t. If
Keir Hardie and his kind will try the experi-
ment of introducing the tiger and the boa-
constrictor wholesale into England, and turning
them loose to establish the Brotherhood of Animal
along with the local cow, then the question of
the Brotherhood of Man may perhaps receive
serious consideration in this country. And if
peace and unpreparedness and arbitration do
any good in the first case, then we may
begin to seriously consider them in the second.
But just now we have to do with Things
As They Are, while dreamers like Keir Hardie
talk of Things As They Might Be. And
we are the lonely sentinels of the white race
—the people who will have to stand the brunt of
the trouble when it comes ; while the silly globe-
trotting doctrinaires, who come here for a few
brief weeks, and preach the gospel of non-
resistance, and then bolt away to the comparative
safety of Europe, will be out of harm’s way.
* * * *
Long ago, before the invention of cannon, and
rifles, and gunpowder, and ironclads, when men
wore armoi - , and fought and shouted and labored
in hand-to-hand conflict in the forefront of the
unpleasantness, the European and the Asiatic
met on something like equal terms. Then the
Saracens annihilated the Crusaders ; the Turks
desolated Europe up to the walls of Vienna
and threatened the Holy Father in Rome;
the Arabs swamped Spain; the Tartars
conquered Russia ; and the smoke of the
cities to which the Huns had applied the
torch was visible from the walls of Paris. In
later years, when Europe had developed firearms
and military tactics, and had built great fleets,
and constructed cannon, and forged steel and iron
defences, while Asia still relied on the spear and
the sword, and still labored and shouted and bel-
lowed personally in hand-to-hand disturbance,
Europe acquired a huge contempt for Asia, and
regarded it as a minus quantity or thereabouts.
Then certain European nations—especially Britain
—went mad, and proceeded to teach Asia the
secrets of Europe’s military superiority. Conse-
quently Japan has risen, and China is rising, and
Afghanistan is making its own rifles and its own
cannon ; and the days when the camp-fires of
Asia could be seen from the walls of Paris
may yet return, because history has a habit
of repeating itself which is dreadful to con-
template. And inasmuch as a peaceful atti-
tude was of no account against the Tartars,
and seeing that the Huns took no stock in arbitra-
tion, this lone, solitary outpost of the white man
will do well to prepare for the day of trouble.
There is no idea of “ militarism,” as it is popu-
larly called, in the proposal to train the youth of
Australia to the use of arms. They will not be
available for foreign service, but purely for home
defence. When the men whose votes can make
war are also the men who have to go out, rifle on
shoulder, and conduct the war, it is only in the
last extremity that war is made. The civil popu-
lation is often cheerfully ready to order a hired
army out to needless battle, but it seldom orders
itself out without good reason. And the best
guarantee that other nations will keep the peace
towards us is to show a front which will convince
them that war against us is a dangerous experi-
ment.
* * * *
The weak point in the military scheme of the
DeAkin Government —its naval scheme is full of
weak points—is its inadequacy. It is proposed that
all able-bodied young male Australians shall, for
three years, put in 18 days’ training per annum.
The training is to end at the age of 21, yet the
citizen is to be liable to be called out for the de-
fence of his country till he reaches the age of 40.
At 40, according to the Deakin Government’s
proposal, his only qualification for defending his
country will be that he learned something of
shooting and a little military tactics 19 years
before. This is a most unreliable qualification.
The able-bodied citizen should put in at least a
week’s careful and vigorous training every year
till he reaches 40, and becomes exempt. He
should do this, partly for the good of his country,
and partly for his own good, to keep his muscles
in condition, and to prevent his paunch getting
out of bounds. Switzerland, which has developed
more than any other Power the principle
of defence by a citizen soldiery, trains its
men for a week each year up to the
age of 44, and if Australia did the
same it would be making no needless or excessive
sacrifice in the cause of national safety. No com-
munity can expect to acquire a whole new conti-
nent without being called upon, sooner or later,
to show cause why it should retain possession.
No mere fragment of humanity can constitute
itself the remotest outpost of the white race, and
plant itself in that capacity at the gate of huge,
angry Asia, without being invited, one day or
another, to maintain its position by brute force.
And to subject its young men to 54 days’ military
training in the whole course of their lives—in
other words, to make them camp out and shoot on
their holidays for three years when otherwise they
would probably camp out and fish—isn’t taking a
serious view of the position. The idea is some-
thing much better than has been hitherto
attempted, but even then it doesn’t amount to a
serious attempt to deal with the situation.
NAVAL DEFENCE.
While the Australian Government’s land defence
policy is, up to a certain point, fairly courageous
and enterprising, its naval defence scheme is
timorous and unsatisfactory. The scheme bears
strong internal evidence of being dictated, not by
Australian interests, but by foreign considerations,
and it fails to offer even a tentative solution of
the problem of coast defence, confining its atten-
tion strictly to port defence. Something
much better was hoped for from a Govern-
ment which had shown itself sufficiently in
sympathy with Australian ideals to condemn the
humiliating naval tribute agreement. But it is
evident that the influence of the British Admiralty
and Foreign Office, combined with the disinclina-
tion to suggest to an Australian Parliament the
new expenditure required for the beginning of an
efficient naval defence policy, has been effective
enough to turn “I would” into “I dare not.”
Why the British Admiralty should show an active
hostility to any suggestion for a real Australian
navy it is hard to understand. The British War
Office does not take up the same attitude with
regard to an Australian army. And yet no
argument of any validity can apply against one
more than against another. It is not, presumably,
imagined that the Australian navy will ever set
out to bombard London, any more than it is
thought that the Australian Light Horse will
ravage Hampstead Heath. But, whatever the
reason, or absence of reason, the fact stands that
any proposal from Australia for efficient naval
defence meets with jealous hostility from the
British Admiralty authorities. They are ready
to take our money. They will take our men to be
trained on their ships. They will even let
us provide ships, if they are officered
and controlled by British persons. But an
Australian navy having the same status as the
Australian army, committed first to the defence of
Australia, and, secondly, to the furtherance of
such Imperial interests as the Australian Parlia-
ment may sanction—that, to their mind, is abhor-
rent. Still, there is no legal, or even sentimental,
reason why the prejudices of the British Admiralty
should be considered. It is difficult to understand,
therefore, why an Australian Government, which
in other matters is Australian, should in
this one matter submit to the dictation of a
British department. But that dictation is what
the announced Deakin naval policy clearly shows.
* * * *
There is at the present time particularly little
cause why an Australian Government should con-
sider the unreasonable British jealousy in this
matter. The Australian Parliament has just
approved under the tariff of a grant in aid of
British trade which will represent from £1,000,000
to £1,500,000 a year. That, coming directly out
of our revenue by Customs remissions and pre-
sented to British manufacturers, may be taken as
a fair return for the measure of defence to Aus-
tralia which the Imperial navy represents—a
measure which has been very considerably reduced
since the Anglo-Japanese treaty and the with-
drawal from the Pacific of all first-class British
warships. Australia is no longer liable to the
taunt that it takes something of defence and
gives nothing in return. Begging for the time
the question as to whether—being a new country
with much developmental work yet to be done—
it can reasonably be expected to do more than
undertake to defend its own territory and hold
this lonely outpost for the white race, Australia
has made a contribution, and a very generous con-
tribution, to the treasury of Imperial interests.
It can now go ahead in the matter of its own
defence without the slightest feeling of unfairness
to the Mother Country.
» * * *
The Deakin naval defence policy relates purely
to ports, not coasts. There are to be provided
each year for three years, three submarines and
two torpedo-boat destroyers, not suitable for
ocean work. The total naval defence at the
end of the three-years period would thus be nine
submarines and six torpedo-boat destroyers, and
these would be officered by the British Navy,
and divided among the Australian ports. They
would provide no sea-faring career for Aus-
tralians, no defence for the Australian coast.
Their sole benefit would be to make Australian
ports a little safer and a little better as bases for
another navy. That is why the proposals are
fairly consistent with British Admiralty ideas.
They represent in no real sense an Australian
navy or the beginning of an Australian navy.
The Australian Naval Defence Committee, repre-
senting the best skill Australia could command in
naval matters, proposed, as the beginning of an
Australian navy, four ocean-going destroyers, 16
destroyers of the River class ( not river boats, but
built for rough water and ocean work within a
limited range), and four torpedo boats. That
would have been at least a flotilla which could
have taken to the sea and put up a fight off
Broken Bay or Thursday Island. The Bulletin
did not endorse that programme, which it con-
sidered far too timid. This paper could see no
reason why Australia should not commence at
once with the first vessel of a real fighting type.
For
The torpedo-boat’s a spiteful little vixen ;
She is long and slim, and saucy as a witch ;
She can set the green and slippery sea a-foaming,
And can throw a tube and never drop a stitch ;
Her elder sister’s yonder grim destroyer ;
She can show her heels to all the navy’s bunch ;
But both of them stand back when work is needed—
The battleship’s the lady with the punch.
If Japan can build battleships, why not Australia?
If little South American republics, with their
infinitely inferior resources, can have cruisers,
why not Australia ? But the Deakin Government
goes back on even the proposals of the Naval
Committee and puts forth a half-hearted plan,
not for a navy, but for an improved system of
harbor defence.
* * * *
There are national reasons against this strangling
of Australian naval aspirations. The Common-
wealth should never be satisfied until it is
guardian of its own fate on sea and land;
should never be content whilst the interference
of outsiders is allowed to control its policy in
vital matters. There are strategic reasons of the
weightiest against the shunting of an Australian
navy. It is more and more evident each year that,
in case of a general war, the Pacific will be
stripped of British warships and Australia be left
at the mercy of the Asiatic fleets. In that case, a
flotilla of port submarines and destroyers would
be useful in helping to safeguard the chief ports ;
but they would be useless to prevent or even harass
the landing of a raiding force ; they would be abso-
lutely ineffective, say, against an attack on Broken
Bay designed to cut the greater part of the popu-
lation of Australia off from the northern coal-
fields, and to set up a base for the capture of
Sydney and Newcastle. Australia needs a navy
to patrol its coast, not a superior system of sub-
marine mines to guard one or two ports against a
frontal attack. If we’re not fit to build a navy —
if from complaisance to the British Admiralty,
or through cowardice and incompetence, we can’t
hope to hold our coasts, better not worry about
appointing a High Commissioner to London.
Rather let us send ambassadors to Tokio and
Pekin, and make in advance the best terms
possible with our future enemies.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Voice of Balaam, the Son of Beor.
Sydney Evening News recently published some
articles signed “E.5.P.,” concerning the Port
Arthur battlefields, and thereabouts; and it didn’t
seem to once occur to the News that it was paying
its globe-trotter to cruelly confound the blessed
“ policy of the paper.” For example, take this
(14/12/ 07) about the Tokio Exhibition :
The Japanese have certainly now arrived at the point
of manufacturing all the European articles required for
their home consumption—articles which, until they did
so, had been imported from Europe and America, and sold
to them at a high profit. This will never be again, for
everything, from a toothbrush to a warship, from a
pocket-book to a Maxim gun, from a gentleman’s necktie
to a piano, was there. So the days of imported goods
of a miscellaneous nature, “ Made in Germany,” or in
England or America, are gone for ever, except in the case
of very few articles, which, for some reason or another,
cannot yet be manufactured in Japan. But they will be,
probably, by the time of the proposed International Exhi-
tion of 1912 .... Times have changed within the last
20 years, when I was advised by a friend to bring to Japan
some little crystal articles for presents to the Japanese,
“ for, you know,” said he, “ they have no glass in their
country, and they cannot make it.”
Let’s think. “They have no glass, and they
cannot make it.” Now, where has The Bulletin
heard some expression like that lately ?
. . . The Japanese Government is now paying back
with interest the long years of loss they suffered through
their tariff being dictated to them, in order that the
foreign importer might he the gainer, by making it now
considerably harder on the pockets of Europeans to
purchase their own manufactures. But in this the
foreigners are only being paid back in their own coin.
It is a case of retribution, which the Japanese consider
perfectly justifiable, and, if the foreigners don’t like it,
why, then they can go, and there will not be many tears
shed at their departure.
That is a glimpse of the progress Japan has made
under Protection within a brief 20 years. And
having paid a correspondent to see and be struck
by this progress, the News still maunders its self-
discredited yarn that Protection spells ruin, and
only Freetrade means progress.


Bill Lyne.
The big man of the Federal Session just closed
was Bill Lyne. He overshadowed all others
completely. Taking the Tariff schedule in his
charge “ the rogue elephant plodded ponder-
ously with it through forests of obstruction and
auagmires of talk. What he couldn t smash he
got round. Sometimes he trumpeted with rage.
More often he caressed the Opposition with his
prehensile trunk, and moved that Opposition
gently but firmly aside. Occasionally he dropped
a little bit of his load on the road. But it was
not often. When the end came with the Olinst-
mas adjournment there was, so far as the
Reps, were concerned, a fine high tariff wall
well builded. It had some little gaps ; Australia
will see to those gaps later. But, on the whole,
it is a good wall. And Bill Lyne was builder-in-
chief. There was not much eloquence spent in
the achievement, not much talk of principle.
Lyne took what he could get, and the best he
could get; that was his principle. Lyne was in
the House always, plodding away with facts and
arguments and persuasions, getting through an
amount of work that would have killed a
more imaginative, nervy man ; that was
his method. It was a triumph of business-
like persistence. Bill Lyne moves up by it
a big step in Australian consideration. At
onetime, he floundered hopelessly in the Federal
Legislature. He for long succeeded in doing no
more than hang on to the edge of Circumstance.
He seemed out of touch with the new spirit
of Australian life. At one time, The Bulletin
(which can afford to acknowledge mistakes when
it makes them) said that “ Bill Lyne must
go,” just as it once spoke scornfully of
“ J. Culvert Watson.” Now it is evident Bill
Lyne must stay. He is among the big leaders.

“Where the White Man Can’t Work.”
Up North the Kanaka, said to be the very man
to stand the Queensland climate, is thinning out;
he goes back to his islands, but leaves the bones
of 20 per cent, of him behind to manure the
Queensland canefields. And as Thomas Tanna
departs the white man surges north to take
his place—the white man for whom, accord-
ing to Bruce Smith down south, and Mrs.
Molyneux Parkes down south, and Janet
Mrs. Clarke, also down south, the torrid
north is utterly and hopelessly unfitted. With
his pick and his shovel and his swag and his
other sundries the w'hite man hustles on his way
towards the equator, consequently Queensland,
which in 1861 stood for much less than 3 per cent,
of the population of what is now the Common-
wealth, now contains over 13 per cent.
With this rush of population northward,
and this replacing of the nigger by the
white man in tropical Australia, a curious thing
is happening. The death-rate of Queensland is
reported as showing a very large decrease. In
1906, according to the just-published Victorian
Year Book, Queensland had a smaller death-rate
than Victoria, N.S.W., S.A., Westralia, or Tas-
mania, and a far lower death-rate than any coun-
try in Europe, Asia, Africa, or America; further,
it is represented as running a neck-and-neck race
with Maoriland for the honor of having the very
lowest death-rate in the world. So much better
does the white man thrive in tropical Queens-
land—where blatant and irresponsible fools say
that he can neither live nor work —that the death-
rate there is now not much more than half that of
the United Kingdom, and less than half that of
Germany. The imported nigger population, said
to be “specially adapted to the tropics,” died
like flies in the Queensland canefields, and it was
the huge mortality among these and other colored
races —also alleged to be “ specially adapted to the
tropics”—which once made the Queensland death-
rate fairly high as Australian rates go, though even
at its worst it has, for many years, shown a
better record than the healthiest of European
countries. But as the Kanaka diminishes, and
ceases to adulterate the bills of mortality, Queens-
land begins to do extraordinary things. Here
(from page 336 of the new Victorian Year Book)
are the figures :
deaths per 100,000 inhabitants.

crease.

It is a quaint superstition, and a silly survival of
the belief in the healthful qualities of the fatal
British variety of weather, which causes Tas-
mania, with 1117 deaths per 100,000 people, to be
regarded as the sanatorium of Australia, while
Queensland, with only 956, is spoken of with terror
and alarm, as if it were a species of white man’s
grave. The Bulletin, of course, isn’t sanguine
enough to suppose that the publication of these
official figures will make the slightest difference
to the circulation of the silly Tory lie about the
North being a country where “the white man
can’t live.” That lie will probably last 50 years yet.
Many of the wealthy Tories of Australia are
wilful and perverted liars ; the others are simply
afflicted with the unspeakable illiteracy of the
fashionable classes, and are frankly ignorant
of things which a hod-carrier or cor-
poration laborer would be ashamed not
to know. Therefore, persons like a certain
potentate of the N.S.W. Chamber of Commerce
will probably still go on telling considerable audi-
ences that the country in which the white man
exhibits the smallest death-rate save one on
the face of the earth is quite uninhabitable
except by niggers. It is a curious side issue,
by the way, that the only country which beat
Queensland for healthfulness last year is the one
which the Tory declares has been “ruined by
Socialistic legislation.” Apparently hot weather
and Socialism agree with the white man's consti-
tution, and do a lot to postpone his funeral.

Mostly Concerning one Smithkins.
“ p.” tells a yarn with a moral hanging to its tail:
Every few years a Peace Society comes to the surface,
spouts a little, and then sinks again to darkness and the
depths. One has just begun to spout again ; and I have
been looking for the name of a dear old chap who has
been a prominent office-bearer in several previous
“arrivals,” but I can’t find it. Perhaps, like me, he has
been thinking over something that happened years ago,
and has decided not to spout any more.
The dear old chap—not so old then—was at his desk on
the teacher’s platform. He had “ views” even in those days.
He hadbeen in the Church, and his “ views ” had lifted him
out of it. He hated the idea of punishments—corporal
punishments especially. No cane ever stuck out of his
desk. Now, Smithkins knew that. Smithkins was
a little mosquito of a fellow, full of irritating
buzz and sting, and the dear old chap who wouldn’t
sting back was Smithkins’ victim every day. It went on
for months. Of course the thing had to end some time ;
and it ended all in a vivid moment. Exasperated to the
breaking point, the dear old chap looked round help-
lessly. There was no cane, no strap. There was only an
ink-pot; and as he had to do something, he shied that.
Now, he had had no practice in shying ink-pots. It was
a fool of a weapon, anyway ; for he got the ink down his
own sleeve as he hurled it. It deluged the best hoy in
the class, missed Smithkins, and smashed in the middle of
a map of Europe. It raised a big black volcano in the
heart of Holland, and inaugurated a new European river
system flowing into the Mediterranean. But it only
made Smithkins grin, and he was still grinning when the
poor old inexperienced punisher, horrified at his own
outbreak, rushed out of the room. He left the school
shortly afterwards. He knew that he was a mockery.
But he hung on to his peace notions, and it was he
whose name used to be among the list of chief officers
whenever one of these societies came to the surface to
do another spout, and it is he whose name I can’t find
now. He must have reflected that Smitiikins always
stuck his horrible little sting into him, and into no other
teacher; and he must have remembered how his no-
physical-force ideas encouraged Smithkins to come on
and be annoying and offensive ; and how, when flesh and
blood couldn’t stand it any longer, his unprepared ness
and inexperience made a horrible mess of things, and did
more damage to himself and the best boy than it did to
the grinning Smithkins.
With his helplessness and his futile ink-bottle, he
represented the Australian levies as they will be—if
the Peace Society has its way—when Smithkins in-
vades this country from Asia 100,000 strong, and our
people have to turn out, trained or untrained, and throw
something. And his flight and his resignation represent
what will happen to Australia after the first battle —if
the Peace Society has its way. And the months of tor-
ment which he endured from Smithkins before the col-
lapse represent exactly the time that peaceful, unarmed
Australia will yethave to put up with—supposingthe Peace
Society has its way—before the final trouble, the helpless,
exasperated throwing of the ink-pot at the enemy, and
then the downfall.
The Country Workman.
During the discussion, in Vic. Legislative Coun-
cil, upon the amending Factories Act, the old,
familiar and wearisome yap about the country
laborer was raised for the millionth time. About
20 Rich Landlords rose, in succession, to allege
that, while Wages Boards and such things may be
right enough for the large towns, they are not
needed in the country. Also, for the 20,000 th
time, it was mentioned that Wages Boards would
spread death and destruction amongst the primary
industries, and that an eight-hours day was “ not
practicable ”on farms. Yet, at some later date,
the same heavy imbeciles will arise to complain of
the drift of young men from the country to the
towns, and they will make severe remarks about
the young men of to-day, and compare them scorn-
fully with the early pioneers. Finally, one of the
orators will waddle down to the Argus
office and insert an advertisement for “man
for dairy, wages 17s. 6d. per week and found.”
Just at the time the Legislative Council was cut-
ting the country clauses out of the Factories Act,
the Western District butter factories were issuing
their November payments for milk. Here are
some of them : Colac, £20,942; Camperdown,
£10,210; Glenormiston, £8324; Warrnambool,
£6381; Grasmere, £6616; Farnham, £4711 —a
total for six factories, and for one month only, of
£57,184. At Colac the individual cheques for ’the
month ran from £413, £244, £2lB, £2ll, down-
wards. At Grasmere the largest cheque was £220,
and there were 17 over £IOO. At Glenormiston
the top cheque was £361, and there were 25 over
£IOO, while the average for 110 suppliers was £75.
And this is the industry that would crumble to
pieces if it paid decent wages ! The fact is that
the dairying industry could easily afford to pay
double the present miserable rate of wages, if the
landlord only sat on its chest in moderation. The
talk about an eight-hours day is just so much dust
raised to befog the main issue. The employees
will not trouble much about the hours, so long as
they are decently paid, and decently treated. A
Wages Board, comprised of country residents,
could, in a couple of days, fix up a scheme that
would suit the industry admirably. At present,
dairying is the one industry at which grown men
are unable to earn enough to marry upon ; and, in
an empty country like this, such a state of affairs
—for which, as the figures given show, there is no
excuse—is a scandal.
South Africa and the Unemployed.
A letter from a land which has tried Freetrade,
plenty of cheap imports, the importation of capi-
tal to develop the great natural resources, low-
priced colored labor, and all the things Bruce
Smith admires:—
Presently I, too, shall be among the unemployed, and
my wife and babe may be asking me for the bread I may
not be able to supply. That is the thought which keeps
throbbing in my brain, the ever-present haunting dream
of thousands in golden, sun-tipped South Africa. And
now the thing is done. My dream of permanent employ-
ment in the land of promise is over, and in a few weeks’
time my wife and 1, in company with hundreds of like
unfortunates, will be journeying we scarce know whither,
but away from this country of disenchantment, this
devil’s apple called South Africa. The personal note is
always with us, but to-day I understand, as has never
before been possible, the feelings of that weird and hope-
less assembly of out-of-works which ever grows larger
and larger and more insistent for work and bread. And
to understand is much.
For the past three years, and perhaps ever since
war devastated this land, a deadly inertia has been
gradually creeping over the trade of the country,
seizing upon the vital centres of commerce, stifling
all initiative and enterprise, and dragging down
the colonies, new and old alike, in its relentless and
never-ceasing advance. The causes are not far to seek.
The thousands who flocked hither at the close of the war
were not producers. They came to import, and to buy,
and to sell, and to write, and to do a thousand and one
things which do not matter in a new country. Few came
to work, fewer came to farm, none came to manufacture
and to produce. It was really a rally of the—shall it be
said ?—useless ; a gathering of men whose one aim was to
serve their own ends and to feather their own nests. The
great seaports—Cape Town, Port Elizabeth, East London,
Durban, and even outcast Lourenco Marques, became
crowded with merchants and their clerks. Their harbors
were filled with ocean-tramps and with great liners stuffed
with the merchandise which was not wanted, and never
would be wanted till the true settlers, the workers, en-
tered into the heritage awaiting them. The warehouses
were glutted with goods, the yards were piled high with
timber destined to rot away in the fierce noonday heat,
the railway trucks groaned under their loads of useless
commodities hurriedly sent up country, none much cared
whither so long as they went—but the new life, the new
vigor, the new' strength of the community never arrived.
This is but one cause of the present unparalleled de-
pression. Over-importation has much to answer for, but
the whole burden does not rest upon the shoulders of the
merchants. The mines, those vast pulsating engines for
the destruction of human life and the levelling of human
intellect, did not play their part. They fouled the
national nest. Their owners gambled heavily and lost.
The throw of the dice miscarried. The shares went down,
down, down, and the dividends doled out to the pockets
of their Park-lane owners never reached this country,
but such as they were remained in the coffers of Lombard-
street, or were dissipated in the swirl of London society.
This is the second cause of the depression in South
Africa. And the third follow’s hard upon it. It is the
tinkering with constitutions, the pulling down and
the building up, the incessant question-asking in the
Commons, the constant interference of the Mother
Country in things colonial. Here in South Africa we are
not free men. London and Jew'ry own us. The Parlia-
ment of the Empire and the Under-Colonial Secretary, or
the Under-Colonial Secretary and the Parliament of the
Empire, possess our souls, and the Kaffir, the Hottentot
and the Zulu alone are truly free.
During this month over 2000 men will be thrown upon
the streets by discharge from our railways. The great
businesses and corporations are vomiting forth their sur-
plus. De Beers, the richest company south of the Equator,
is sending forth to poverty 500 men. Large firms are
closing down. Warehouses are shutting. In Durban
alone over 300 shops and warehouses stand empty—and
in a few months time the exodus of British subjects from
South Africa, British born and bred, will assume even
more alarming proportions than it already has done.
Dutchmen will inherit the land. Jews will possess the
mines. And Scots and Chinamen will hold fast to the
warehouses and stores. At least, so say the enemies of
Scotland.
In the meantime, the personal element will ever intrude
itself into a widening circle of unfortunate homes, and
the hopes and aspirations of many will turn from the
flesh pots of the Transvaal to the fertile plains and valleys
of Australia. Sydney Harbor and Table Bay will be
joined by an ever-increasing stream of unfortunates—
passing, however, in the one direction only, since the
lessons of South Africa have now been too well learned.

Some English Railway Facts.
A recent cable announced that the London press
was making merry at the efforts of the Australian
Government to arrange industrial matters on a
basis fair alike to the worker, the employer, and
the public. The London press seemingly cannot
understand a country where no one is allowed to
rob. That seems to them an absolute negation of
the spirit of freedom. It would be much more
sane on the part of the English press to look
to its own land. British railway management,
for instance, is a fearsome thing; but so far
there has been no press campaign against
it. The position taken up by the railway
directors is that English farmers and manufac-
turers must use the railways, and, therefore, are
fair game. The foreign manufacturer and
farmer, on the other hand, will not ship goods
over the English railways if the freight absorbs
all the profit; therefore, the foreigner must be
dealt with tenderly. In The Bulletin, lately,
a writer pointed out how the English hop
industry was steadily declining, in spite of the
fact that it is worked with the cheapest labor
in the world. And what wonder when the English
railways charge 355. per ton on hops from Ashfield
in Kent, while the French grower can ship his
from Baris to London for 17s. 6d. ? The Chamber
of Agriculture complains that the coast towns of
all Britain can be supplied more cheaply with
produce from farms 150 miles away in France than
they can be from Lincolnshire over the English
railways. French eggs are carried from Dover to
London for 3s. 3d. per ton, while English eggs
laid around the same seaport are charged 225. 7d.
per ton, thus giving the foreigner 19s. 4d. prefer-
ence on every ton carried. On cherries, the
foreigner has a preference of 10s. per ton; his
dead poultry is given 14s. per ton advantage over
the local chicken, and the same applies to butter
and cheese. England was once a great silk manu-
facturing nation, but the railways helped to
kill the business by giving the French silks
preference in the matter of freight, and
carrying English goods at owners’ risk while
the foreign goods were carried at companies’
peril. The most startling example of preference
to the foreigner is in the matter of radiators, on
whjch the railways charge 365. Bd. per ton from
Glasgow, where they are made, to Nottingham,
where they are largely used, while it only costs
12s. per ton to bring the same machine from New
York to Nottingham. Fish caught on the
Scottish coas-® by British boats pay £4lss. per ton,
while the foreign boats that bring their surplus
into Aberdeen, and send it by the same train, pay
only £1 per ton. Foreign bar iron from London to
Staffordshire costs from 6s. 4d. on Belgian to 9s. lOd.
on German ; British bar iron from Staffordshire to
London pays 13s. 4d. Foreign wire from London
to Birmingham is charged from Bs. 2d. on Dutch to
11s. 2d. on German; English wire from Birming-
ham to London pays 245. lOd. yer ton. After an
exhaustive analysis and dissection, the English
Chamber of Commerce decided that what would
cost 6s. 3d. freightage in the TJ.S.A. would
amount to 295. 2d. in Britain, and that
for equal service and tonnage the English
merchants and farmers paid £2,000,000 more
than did their German rivals on the State-
owned railways of the Fatherland. And with all
this sort of robbery and national treachery going
on right at its own doors, the London press can
find time and space to throw sarcasms at Austra-
lian efforts to make trade and industry fair and
equitable!

Some of Cohen’s Security.
Cohen has some splendid security on the northern
rivers of N.S.W. Early in the campaign
against .Nature in that part, officialdom
just spent money and “gave employment.’’
At the mouth of every bar-harbor may be
seen cropping up amongst the sandbanks and
siltbeds a disorderly heap of rocks ; these are all
that is left of training-walls, which didn’t train
worth a cent. Afterwards, the imported “ex-
perts” came along. Their plans were in most
cases so absurdly extravagant that even spend-
thrift Governments were not game to go the
whole borrowed hog with them. Despite it all,
the river entrances are not one whit better than
they were. The local people have all along
been less concerned about real harbor im-
provements than about getting money spent
in the district, in the interests of the pub
and grocery trade. When the vote petered
out, the gloom which settled upon the affected
towns was thick enough to trap a, fly. Having
nothing else to do, the residents in such cases
held meetings that bubbled over with indigna-
tion. With a strongly-worded telegram in his
pocket, the local member would next day haunt
the Works Department, or ask questions in
the House, or move the adjournment. Then
there was another aspect of these works
which was winked at by the public,
because any exposure might have meant stoppage.
In one case it was shown clearly that a contractor
was paid for stones of a weight greater than the
lifting capacity of the quarry cranes. More than
once the clay strippings, too, were trucked and
jettisoned, and charged as five-ton stones. The
river and harbor dredging has been very little
better. For the past 30 years or thereabouts
plants have been employed in the Clarence and
other rivers lifting the silt with pump and bucket,
and depositing it a mile or so away on some
shallows, to be washed back again with the first
flooding of the river. If there is a day of reckon-
ing for the N.S.W. Works Department, there
must surely be a scent of brimstone already
hanging around.
