The Queensland Farm Laborer.
The unspeakable Tory press of Brisbane, which
for wilful misrepresentation sprints hard after the
Australian record, is busy trying to rob Kidston
of the support of the “ Black Soil ” members, who
hail from the Darling Downs. The proposal of
Kidston to bring the rural industries within the
scope of the Wages Boards is the stick these
members are being beaten off with. The House
of Fossils refused to accept the clause, “just
because ”; and the Tory papers are urging the
farmers’ representatives to render another Kid-
ston regime impossible, “ just because.” They
ha ve no other argument worth mentioning. “Just
because ” constitutes their hope of another finan-
cial drunk, and more Philp. There is no arguable
reason why rural laborers should not be afforded
some sort of legalised relief, if they can make out a
case. All that Kidston asked isthatthey should be
entitled to be heard. Queensland specially lays
itself out to encourage the “primary industries.”
It employs a staff of experts in the Agricultural
Department; it maintains several State farms as
object lessons; it imports stock and seeds and
trees for distribution; it pays the Orient Co.
£25,000 a year to go up to Brisbane from Sydney
for butter consignments ; it takes charge of the
export meat industry ; it advances money at 5 per
cent, to concerns that handle the farmers’ produce.
Having done all this for the industry, the very
least that the State can do is to see that the
servants of the industry have a chance to remedy
any grievances they believe they suffer from.
Apart from all that, it is supposed to be one of the
great pillars on which British liberty is based that
justice shall not be denied to even the meanest;
yet the Tory party of Queensland, which professes
to regard old British institutions with extra-
special veneration, denies the means of justice to
this farm-laboring class. Why ? Is it fear ? Is
it because their alleged regard for the pillars of
British liberty is a sham, while their love for
cheap-sweated labor is real ?
The Labor Leader from Afar.
“ Two Seas” looks back upon his ill-spent youth:
The English Labor agitator, who blows hitherwards
when the agitating business is slack in his own country,
is surprised and grieved to find that ‘‘the Australian
workman is too fat and contented.” That kind of work-
man has no use for a dude in a velvet coat—a dude whose
mission in life is to stir up the thin and discontented until
they “arise” and clank their fetters, and whirl their
chains around their heads, and, incidentally, provide
funds for a new velvet coat when the old one gets worn at
the seams and greasy around the collar, where the long,
oiled hair of the visitor dangles. But the men he
harangues in Australia can’t understand the language
designed to appeal to the English wage slave.
I recall my first acquaintance with the British work-
man in the days of my youth—not far distant. Most of
the “hands” in a relative’s factory had been working
there over 20 years. They served seven years’ appren-
ticeship at nominal wages, and, when out of their time,
earned from 20s. to 355. per week—a week being 60 hours
of strenuous toil. Some of them worked in cellars, ven-
tilated only through the trap-doons which led into the
workshops above. In these dismal dungeons, lit by
gas flares, and poisoned by the fumes of forges,
lacquering ovens, and sulphuric acid brass-dipping
tanks, men sweated with handkerchiefs tied over
their mouths the whole circle of the clock, with
li hour off for meals. And (ye gods !) such meals, eaten
in an atmosphere like a Presbyterian’s Hell. Most of
them were married men, who had to live near their work,
and a single room, at 6s. per week, in a crowded “ court ”
or alley—where the week’s washing had to be dried on a
line stretched across to the opposite neighbor’s window—
had to do for the whole family.
On Fridays, there was only a copper or two left for
tucker, and the errand boys, who fetched the men’s teas
from the neighboring shops, were clouted if they failed to
return with full value for the money. A tea order was
usually “a farthing’s worth of tea, a farthing’s
worth of sugar, a doorstep [slice of bread .-11111
butter] and a bloater ’’—total, ljd. If the “'doorstep”
or the bloater looked too thin, the urchin was sent back
with orders to change it. “ Cawfee ” was a luxury pur-
chased ready-made, and it was no unusual thing lor a
That Mighty Myth, the Public!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When the daily paper editor is feeling ill at ease,
From malignant Cobdenitis and most hopeless
Bright’s disease,
There is only one reflection his disordered mind
can please—■
That perhaps it’s all the better for The Public !
When a policy propounded is too utterly absurd,
When it’s even more ridiculous than commonly is
heard,
There’s a grain of satisfaction in the editor’s last
word—
That perchance it may be better for The Public !
In the latest Tariff question he has got it on the
brain !
’Tis a daily and an hourly and perpetual refrain
That, although the manufacturer in some respects
may gain—
’Twill be something truly awful for The Public !
It may suit the men of Capital he’ll readily
consent;
It may also suit the workers to perhaps a less
extent;
And the shopkeepers and shopmen will not lose,
that’s evident —
But it’s something wholly frightful for The Public !
Civil servants may not feel it as remarkably
severe !
And with statesmen, lawyers, doctors, it will
scarcely interfere !
But the addle-headed editor to this will still
adhere—
That it’s really very dreadful for The Public !
’Tis a Tariff which, upon the whole, can do but
little harm
To the rich man, or the poor man, or the man who
owns a farm !
But what fills the fatuous editor with furious
alarm
Is the horrible effect upon The Public !
He will deal with every class in turn and show
that each may gain,
That it benefits, or fails to hurt, all classes in the
main ;
But although it may suit everyone his heart is
filled with pain
For the burdens it will place upon The Public !
That’s the curse of Cobdenitis and the bane of
Bright’s disease
To the editor who gets them very badly ; for, he
sees
Not alone the various classes, but, entirely outside
these,
An imaginary body called The Public !
“And suppose there’s more employment,” says
the Daily Heliograph,
Not by way of jocularity or editorial chaff,
But in solemn, sober seriousness that nearly kills
the staff,
“It amounts to subsidising by The Public ! ”
When you’ve counted individuals of every rank
and grade,
All the classes, masses, lasses, asses found in every
trade,
When a count of ALL professions and the idle rich
is made,
Where the Devil is this myth they call The Public ?
N.S.W.
W. T. GOODGE.

when he returned to the factory.
VVe have no boys like these in Australia—standing
around eating-houses with a wolfish glare in their eyes,
always alert and ready to grab food, or to duck from
the blow they are ever expecting. We have no men
like the fathers of these boys, whose bitter poverty
compels them to allow their children to hunt in the
streets with the stray dogs for food. But the little
dude in the velvet coat, having been so successful in
banishing poverty from England—by the simple method
of calling upon the poor to “ Arise 1" and clank their
chains—comes to Australia, and is disgusted to find
that the Australian workers “ are too contented and
fat.” They don’t understand the eloquence that appeals
to British workmen —the men who purchase their
groceries by the farthing’s worth. Many of them have
never even seen that secret of England’s greatness, the
Free trade “ fadge,” the fourth part of a penny, glori-
fied by the image of the King of the richest Empire in
the world.
No wonder the British Labor-leaders get angry when
they come over here to convert us ! “ Why be con-
tented and fat,” they inquire, indignantly, “when by
arising ! and clanking your chains you can be like us ”
—and have farthings to spend?

Again the Affable Alfred.
Last Friday there was published in the Sydney
papers three small-print columns of rather polite
correspondence between Prime Minister Deakin
and State Insect Wade about the Australian
capital. Reading it, one is inclined to gnash his
teeth at the dreadful affability of Alfred. All
through the narrative Wade quite ignored the
fact that the Australian Parliament long ago
selected a site for a capital, and in letter after letter
he implied, with oily insinuations of treachery
and breach of faith, that the Australian Govern-
ment had declined to move in the matter, and
that the Ma State was patiently waiting for its
wrongs to be put right. And never once did
Deakin go after him with an axe and tell
him briefly that the capital has been selected
according to the strict terms of the Constitution,
and that it is the cantankerous, childish obstruct-
iveness of the N.S.W. State Legislature which
alone stands in the way of a settlement. In the
excess of his affability he didn’t once remind
Wade of those plain words in the Constitution
which say “ The seat of Government of the Com-
monwealth shall be determined by the Parlia-
ment,” and tell him that his interference in the
matter was a gross impertinence. And he wasn’t
even roused by this clause in one of Wade’s
earlier letters: —
The Constitution of the Commonwealth has now been
in operation for nearly seven years. Its inauguration was
delayed in the first instance by the objections on the part
of N.S.W. to the provisions of the earlier Bill dealing with
the location of the capital.
That silly old yarn has already been burst a
thousand times. It is all false from beginning to
end. N.S.W. accepted the first Bill, which left
the capital an absolutely open question, by a good
majority, and it was only the disgraceful breach of
faith of the N.S.W. State Parliament in altering
the minimum affirmative vote required to carry
the Bill from 50,000 to 80,000 which nullified the
acceptance. And the only visible reason why the
80,000 affirmative vote wasn’t reached was that the
people of N.S.W. were assured over and over
again that the Br.addon clause was utterly
ruinous ; the “ location of the capital ” • was
hardly mentioned during the campaign. Yet
the mild Deakin didn’t even resent having this
glaring old fable pushed at him as a reason why
Wade should dictate to Australia about the site
of the capital. The prospects of Australia getting
its rights seem very poor unless Alfred can get
rid of nine-tenths of his horrible affability, and
buy a gun with the proceeds.

Japan Draws in its Tail.
According to cables from Tokio to London,
Japan’s recent swelled head is making it feel very
sick in the region of the pocket. When the head
was at its swollenest, vast military and naval
undertakings were entered upon. The Monkey,
having humbugged itself into the belief that it
had given the Bear no end of a beating, insisted
upon pushing its tail in among the great nations,
and decided that it must have an army and navy
to match the size of its afflicted head. It was too
puffed up to remember what sort of a poor upstart
it really was. It forgot for the moment that
millions of its population are so poor that they
can’t even eat good rice—they eat the weevils and
sell the rice. While thehysterics lasted, thecountry
which had got used to making sacrifices—even of
its daughters—pulled through somehow. There
was such a run on anything Japanese among the
fools of the world that Japan was even able to
sell some of the weevils as well as the rice, and
that was a help. But the hysterics soon passed,
and then Japan found that its swelled head was
costing it about a-third of its entire income. A
memorandum written by Wakatsuki, the Im-
perial Japanese Financial Commissioner, was
quoted in The Bulletin a few weeks ago. Some
of the country’s own particular and numerous
Cohens had been making inquiries about the
security; and Waka was turned on to comfort
Cohen. “ Pooh ! ” said Waka, in effect—still in
the best swelled-head manner —“ what is your
little interest bill to a great people like us ! ”
The whole burden of tax, national and local put
together, on the shoulders of the people, is only some-
thing like 30 per cent, of their incomes.
Wakatsuki’s “only” was magnificent. But even
granted that the Jap had been trained to make
sacrifices, the point comes when sacrifice means
starvation; and starvation was never camped
very far away from the hovels of millions of these
people. In 1898 the whole revenue from taxation
was £9,300,000 ; now it is £26,900,000. Income tax,
started as the patriotic contribution of the wealthy,
is now squeezed out of everybody who receives
£3O a year. If Japan decides that it had better
draw in its tail a bit, nobody, then, need be
surprised. But there is not the least sign that it
intends to radically alter its plans. It merely
“ postpones ” expenditure. It might borrow more
money, and go ahead, and probably burst; it
prefers, instead, to go slower, and nurse its credit.
The Japan that does that won’t come pounding at
Australia’s door quite so soon, possibly ; but he
will be a more dangerous Monkey when he does
arrive. The country that goes into a campaign
with its credit good and active is never a pleasant
enemy to blimp against; and Japan is just about
as careful of its credit as Australia has been
prodigal. Still, though Australia may have a
tougher proposition to handle in the end, it may
now probably have a few more years in which to
make its own preparations. And that will be an
advantage, provided those few years are used and
not wasted. But it is no use getting a hundred
yards start in a race if you lean against a post and
wait for the other fellow to catch up.

Mr. W. B. Hill, a Sydney man interested in
Northern Territory tin mines, recently informed
a Brisbane interviewer :
White labor is scarce, and the percentage of Chinese is
too large. A clergyman in the Territory has made
the startling statement that fully 80 per cent, of
deaths up there are due to alcoholic excess, and my own
investigations bear this out. There are between 500 and
<SOO shanties, and only seven hotels. Sly grog-selling is
carried on in a most open and shameless manner, and
boarders at hotels go across the street to shanties to pur-
chase beer. This is done because the shanties supply
cheaper liquor.
In other words, in this “ terrible climate ” where,
according to B. Smith and Mrs. Molyneux
Parkes, the white man can’t live, the real case
is that the white man doesn’t seem able to die to
any extent, in the ordinary way. Nothing short
of poisonous grog is able to finish 80 per
cent, of him—and poisonous grog would
hasten the end of Beelzebub. In the Territory
the bulk of what population there is has concen-
trated itself far north in the least healthy part of
the country. Yet the death-rate among its white
population is below that of England—this despite
the climate and the awful fact that there are
about 600 groggeries for 4000 people. If the
British isles had 6,600,000 drinkeries, what sort of
a death-rate would they have ?

From an ardent Melbourne Labor sympathiser :
It was a bad move when the guardianship of the poli-
tical Labor movement in Victoria was taken out of the
hands of the trades unionists and invested in the Political
Labor Council. True, some good work has been done
under disadvantages by the P.L.O. ; but that body has
been water-logged on almost every occasion when it
should have been going full speed ahead.
Here’s my experience of Leagues in Melbourne. The
branch meetings are held anywhere—in a laundry,
over a milk shop. The attendance varies from seven
to thirteen —mostly contributors to Tom Mann’s salary.
The subjects discussed are varied. The world, from
China to Peru, is surveyed in a gossiping, domestic
fashion, and the nationalisation of monopolies, and the
question of Australian Defence, are incongruously inter-
posed in the narration of a quarrel Brown had with
Smith anent a goat. Most of the speakers practise at
being members of Parliament. They imitate the diction
and style of Tom Mann, and make an awful noise.
Their cliquishness, the Tom MANN-ite element of discon-
tent, and the extravagant ambition of every third mem-
ber to be in Parliament, all conduce to a row sooner or
later between Labor members of Parliament and the re-
spective branches ; and that will, pro tern ., cripple the
progress of Labor principles. A five-pound note, by pro-
viding subscriptions to pack a league, could easily decide
the rejection of almost any Parliamentary candidate.
Labor members know this. They recognise the selfish-
ness, the danger of corruption, these leagues afford. Yet
they do nothing. And so things drift on, and if we are
not careful they will go on drifting till they bump against
destruction.
A large strip of country between Cowra and
Grenfell (N.S.W.), known as the landra district,
enjoys this year what is regarded as an excep-
tionally fine wheat crop. The land is let out on
the shares system, and there are some 60 farmers
struggling on the ground. The land-owner sup-
plies the land, the seed, and one-third of the
manure; the farmer does the rest. Machines,
labor, keep and housing are all in the farmer’s
responsibility. The owner receives one-half the
produce up to 20 bushels per acre. When the
crop exceeds that the excess goes wholly to the
agriculturist. It is the hope of that excess—
which apparently never arrives—that gives rise to
the farmer’s rosy visions. Yet though this is an
exceptionally gorgeous year in landra the crop is
only expected to run 15 bushels to the acre.
If this hope be realised, the owner receives 7\
bushels; if the crop is only 10 bushels, he still
gets one-half, and so bn downward till sharing
becomes a delusion and a snare. Allowing for
interest on the original price paid for the land and
for the cost of the landlord’s one-third of the
manure, the blessed, owner of the soil would be
fairly well paid at one-half of a five-bushel crop,
but the other half wouldn’t support any farmer
who lived on a level higher than that of a rice
coolie. Nevertheless share-farming goes on, and
new men crowd on to shares-lands when the old
ones have been starved off. They do this partly
out of foolishness, but mostly because the Federal
graduated land-tax is still hovering in the dim
remote.
A summary of the Barclay Tableland, Northern
Territory: A tract of land 360 miles by 80.
Magnificent pastoral country, well clothed with
the best Australian grasses ; fairly well watered,
with an average 16in. rainfall; underground sup-
plies of water easily obtainable at 200 ft.; reser-
voirs easily and cheaply constructed ; a good
healthy climate (the thermometer sometimes falls
as low as 32°), in which fever is an unknown ail-
ment. On the rivers and low country coolibah,
gidyea, gutta percha, white wood and Lands-
borough pine are amongst the timbers, and there
are extensive belts of forest consisting of hard
and durable timber, chiefly gum. The principal
rocks are limestone and quartzite. A splendid
sheep region, and, with railway communication,
capable of carrying a large population. This
country is now given up to cattle. McArthur
River station has an area of 15,000 square miles,
Brunette Downs 7000, Alexandria 10,000, Rock-
lands 6000, Lake Nash 3000, Avon Downs 2400
(the only sheep run, and it carries 35,000 jum-
buclcs), Creswell 1000, Afton 1000, Austral 2000
(not stocked, used as a dep6t for Syd Kidman’s
travelling stock). When the Australian Govern-
ment takes over the Territory, and rushes a broad
gauge railway, fit for swift express traffic, through
from Port Darwin to Adelaide, and resumes the
great estates, and starts to hurry desirable white
immigrants into the Territory, it could put a
million settlers on the Barclay tableland, and
then the place wouldn’t be as crowded as the
emptiest country in Europe.
The only Dan O’Connor discoursed at
Manly (N.S.W.) the other night, but the press is
a soulless sort of boiling-down establishment,
which grabs two or three hours of an orator’s best
“iloquince,” treats it by various processes, and
hands out in return about two bald facts. The
account of Dan’s meeting in next morning’s
papers practically amounted to a statement that
Dan had taken certain manuscript to ’Frisco, and
that ’Frisco had fallen on him, and he had to fly
in his socks, and leave the manuscript. In fact,
something like a whole herd of hotels lay in ruins
upon the manuscript, and it was no use waiting
to dig it out.

The “Girls of Gottenberg,” at Her Ma-
jesty’s (Sydney), is the story of the hook that not
only caught the fish, but sold it and had a
rattling good time with the proceeds. Lauri is
the hook. He turns up as the valet and general
beast of burden of Reginald Roberts, who is
Duke of Saxe-Something or Other large enough
to take a photograph of. Just as conductor
Howard Carr gives the final shake to his
stick in the martial overture, Duke
Saxe-Roberts is joining the Blue Hussars
as a lieutenant. But—most horrible discovery on
the threshold of his military career—he finds thatat
Rottenberg, where the Blue Hussars are stationed,
there is only one girl; and even when the one girl
is Pressy Preston, what is a single pair of lips
and cheeks among so many! Simultaneously
comes word that the Red Dragoons are ordered
to Gottenberg and glory that is to say,
Gottenberg and Girl, Gottenberg being full of
what the soldier mostly deserts for. Then
Roberts, the desperate fisherman, uses Lauri
as the hook, dressing him up in gorgeous
colors as one does the snare for the
trustful trout, and sending him out as Envoy
Extraordinary from the Kaiser, with orders to
the effect that at the last moment his
Majesty has changed his racnety mind,
and Reds and Blues are to change places.
Having carried through Duke Saxe-Roberts’s
plan, the Envoy Extraordinary, however, insists
upon continuing an Envoy Extraordinary, and
orders the arrest of anyone who comes between
him and his Imperial lord’s reflected majesty.
•k 4t H? *
So while the valet fills the Royal suite at
the Town Hall, the Colonel sleeps with the
spiders, and Duke Saxe-Roberts is billeted
at the Red Hen, over the river. Yet even
the most uproarious comic opera must marry
the Duke to his blue-blooded cousin, in order to
keep the diseases in the family; so the Red Hen’s
beer-girl has changed places with the rompish
Elsa, daughter of Saxe-Nierstein, and thus every-
things ends all right.
* * * *
In Melbourne, where these newest of
comic opera “ girls ” were tried on “ the wolf ” (in
a Ma State-insect manner of speaking), Florence
Young was the rompish Elsa. But the panto-
mime has called away Florence, whose energy
spreads over all the stage while the audience
catches her good temper, and Olive Godwin
takes her place; and Miss Godwin romps with
difficulty. Yet, once she has used her fine
clear voice and roused the crowded house that
way, she warms up wonderfully. For the rest,
Sydney is most interested in Fanny Dango, a
newcomer whose merit lies nearer her toes than her
tongue. There have been all sorts of song accom-
paniments, but none more picturesque has ever
been designed than the swaying sextette who
make a moving-picture background to her principal
number. Lauri is Lauri in a part that won’t
lessen his popularity, and all the other
people of the Royal Comics have parts
that fit them nicely ; while it is many a
day since the excellent chorus had such
gorgeous clothes to wear or such haunting things
to sing. All round, then, “ The Girls of Gotten-
berg ’ ’ can be marked up as being safe for the
same good time in Sydney that they seem to have
had in Melbourne.
* * * *
The seasoned playgoer and reader knows
two sorts of dream-plays—the sentimental and the
comic. “The Road to Yesterday,” for ex-
ample, was a well-written work, but weepful.
“When Knights Were Bold”—produced at
Sydney Criterion on Saturday night by the
Geach management—is indifferently written,
yet quite funny. Sir Guy de Vere is a
modern baronet, and withal (amazing dramatic
circumstance) temperamentally dull, flat and vice-
less as a total abstainer’s wedding feast. He has
the commonplace viewpoint of a suburban
alderman without the drive and enthusiasm
which so often lands the latter type of
citizen in Parliament or gaol. De Vere
loves his beauteous greenery-yallery cousin—the
Lady Rowana Eggington. But Lady Rowana’s
tastes are mediaeval to the verge of mid-
Victorianism. She despises the worthy baronet’s
unimaginative nature, and contemns him as
a sordid fellow. The consequence is that the first
time he gets drunk he falls to nightmaring in the
wayback realms of Chivalry and old Romaunt.
* * * *
And there he is discovered (Act II.) in even-
ing clothes, and the company of his lady relative,
an Irish adventurer (who is after Rowana himself),
a Jewish financier, and more armored Saracen-
exterminators than enough. Julius Knight
or Cuyler Hastings would be social suc-
cesses in such an environment; Frank Thornton
(who plays Guy) is l a shrinking, self-effacing soul
who, to the roystering quarrelsome aristocracy
of the period, seems altogether loathsome
and unworthy. Much knock-about joy is
extracted from the tribulations of Guy.
Ultimately the Irishman—a most offensive
braggart—and the Hebrew—who, had he really
lived in those wild Yid-baiting days, would
have lost his nose and one or more ears
at short notice—are comprehensively foiled
(Ha! Ha!), and matters end as they should.
Frank Thornton absorbs most of the fat in this
show. There is no niggling reserve about his
treatment of the part of Sir Guy, whose futility is
limned with a whirlwind-like strenuosity. JJiss
Donaldson makes a sufficiently intense, not to say
priggish, Lady Rowana; and Douglas Hamil-
son’s sketch of Isaac Isaacson is good. The minor
parts are well filled, and the piece goes from
start to finish with all the “ snap ” and rush of a
runaway tram.
* * *
“Clause Six,” a 20 minutes’ two-character
curtain raiser, eventuates at 8. The parts are filled
by Mr. Powell and Miss Clare Manifield. The
lady is a very sweet little person, who, after 19£
minutes at cross purposes, consents to marry Mr.
Powell (a mislaid cousin with prospects, a fancy
waistcoat, and other desirable assets), and so
enables the male portion of the audience to go
out and get a well-earned drink.
** * *
“The Scarlet Pimpernel,” which rose
last Saturday night on the horizon of Sydn'ey
Royal, is a sort of reply on the British side to
“ Beaucaire.” Here it is the Englishman who,
masquerading as a fop, is at heart a lion of the
true British breed, fit in brains and thews
for any dozen of the rascally Frenchmen
who, across the Channel, are murdering aristocrats
as a popular diversion, in those days before the
Seven Devils of the Revolution had been curbed
by the great Napoleon. “ The Scarlet Pimpernel”
alias Sir Percy Blakeney, Bart., alias Julius
Knight, has for wife in the story a French
actress, who unwittingly has sent many
aristocrats to Madame Guillotine by be-
traying _ them to the desperadoes of the
Revolution. He, in return, has formed a secret
league to save as many as possible of them from the
clutches of the Paris sails culottes. Therein is
the story, which follows true melodramatic
lines in sensationalism of incident and im-
probability of characterisation. A touch of
humor is given by the foppish pretences of Julius
Knight and the dour philosophy of a British
rustic, who sees in an untimely rainfall more matter
of importance than the murder of a million aristo-
crats. So is made up a quite popular play of the
current taste, with no attempt at real dramatic
art, but plenty of obvious situations and good
stirring clap-trap.
* * # *
The cast of “The Scarlet Pimpernel”
gives the piece the best of chances. A stronger
combination has not been seen in Sydney
for some time. Miss Ola Humphrey, as a
lady who was a French actress, and is
the wife of a British hero, has a fine
“fat” part, and from her first appearance
in a cart-wheel hat (which gives the final
“Sweet Nell ” touch to the character), whirls
into popularity. Miss Harvey, a very pretty
Suzanne de Tournai, and Miss Susie Yaughan, a
sonorous and dignified Comtesse de Tournai, are
her chief feminine supporters. Julius Knight, as
the noble hero, inefficiently disguised as a fop, has
a series of magnificent costumes and attitudes,
and carries them all off well. With him are
Harry Plimmer as the Prince of Wales, and
old friend A. Greenway as Lord Dewhurst, and
Leslie Victor as a French spy with a snaky
voice ; and quite a host of others. The programme
calls it a great cast; and it is—worthy of a great
play if such had been available. But that is
another story.
** * *
Though they put all the “show ladies”
on a one-meal-a-dav diet, and knocked corners off
the porcelain ballet, and pruned the demons’
horns, and sawed delicate fronds off Johnny
Sheridan’s bright green brogue, it was no good—
they found at the last moment that “Cinderella”
couldn’t do herself justice at the Sydney Palace
until further alterations had been made to the
stage. So the opening of the show was post-
poned from Saturday until Boxing Day.
** * *
Sydney Tivoli has several aces in its hand
just now, chief of which is Martin Henderson, a
blind musician, who takes the concertina—tribal
instrument of the push—and, by caressing and
squeezing and waving it, makes to rise before you
a cathedral with stained-glass windows complete,
and the organ playing inside. Another artistic item
isLaSylphe, who creates an Egyptian atmosphere
with a weird snaky dance, done to the accompani-
ment of weirder music. Price and Revost are
announced as in their last week, so anyone who
wants to see how many ways it is possible to fall
over furniture and yet avoid fatal injury will
have to hurry. This pair has perfected
the art of falling down humorously, and mankind
asks nothing more of anyone. The four Eugenes
have a crisp, clean acrobatic turn, which includes
some excellent somersault work ; and Tom Daw-
son—the home-grown humorist —succeeds in
making several English comic songs sound funny
—a gift possessed by few. There are other turns,
of course, but space is a sacred thing and very
scarce.

“ 10-2-4 ” does a retrospect :
There aren’t many things more depressing than a retro-
spect of Australian dramatic productions during the past
half-year. About 80 per cent, of the shows staged in
Sydney and Melbourne since (say) July last were melo-
dramas. Not more or less convincing melodramas such
as Pinero writes on occasion, nor yet witty Shawian ones,
but wild, screeching, blood-and-dynamite impossibilities,
inducing in the judicious the same feeling of weary
protest that surges up while observing Tom Mann
advocating Sosh and saying “Arise!” or G. Reid
bellowing Anti-Sosh and also saying “ Arise ! ”
“Human Hearts,” “The Fatal Wedding,” “A Warn-
ing to Women,” “The Beautiful Fiend,” “Her Love
Against the World,” “The Tyrant,” “The Female
Swindler,” “ A Life’s Revenge ” —these are a few of the
many outrages perpetrated in the six months that have
just sped. In one week four of the sort—viz., “ The Way
to Kenmare,” “The Southern Cross,” “The Midnight
Wedding,” and “The Yellow Peril”—raged contempo-
raneously in Sydney. The leaven of more-or-less rational
stuff is thin and elusive. “ Mr. Hopkinson ” was worth
seeing—mainly for the presentment of the name
part by Hugh Ward. Yet there was no tour de force
of acting so far as Ward was concerned; it simply
happened that the actor was curiously well-
suited by mannerism, appearance, and a score of other
natural qualities to play Hopkinson. But theatre-goers
weren’t hypercritical on these or any other grounds.
They eagerly accepted the joyous stuff offered them, and
clamored for more. However, they didn’t get any more,
Ward being “ off” and rather dull in the remaining shows
staged by his management. Latterly, we have had
“Brewster’s Millions” and “John Glayde’s Honor”
enacted by mimes who know their trade. These aren’t
plays of exalted merit, but they easily touch the dramatic
high-water mark of the past year. The imported musical
comedies have been the worst up to date. The essence
of a good variety show is that its average turn should he
fairly good. In recent musical-comedy variety shows the
average stunt has been ; awful, and the best—well, nothing
to write to mother about. During the period under review
play-house frequenters who weren't attending at some of
the above were shuddering before the fliekergraphed
infamies of the Kelly or “ Robbery Under Arms” mis-
creants, or watching faked bloodshed in connection with
the Eureka Stockade massacre. Does this sort of thing
represent the intellectual level of any fair section of
townsfolk ? Pray Heaven not. And yet, if it doesn’t, why
is it so persistently offered to us ?

* * *
At Melbourne Royal the change of bill is
to happen on Saturday, instead of Thursday. On
Thursday, which is Boxing Day aforesaid, “ The
Midnight Wedding ” will be matineed for the last
time, and the evening and the next night will
bring the long run to a close. “Her Love Against
the World ”is the trouble due on the 27th. There
was an intention to sandwich “Camille” between
the melodramas, but the consumptive lady of easy
virtue has been indefinitely postponed. Miss
Mclntosh, Harcourt Beatty, Mervale and the
other people who kept “The Wedding” warm
for more than fifty performances will now devote
their energies to “ Her Love,” etc. The name of
the drama is bound to be shortened if it catches
on. Popularity breeds abbreviation in such
matters.
** * *
Judged on its first-night appearance, and
credited in advance with the cuts and condensa-
tions inevitable to such bulky matters, the new
panto at Her Majesty’s, Melbourne, is a fairly
brilliant attempt to repeat the “Mother Goose”
success. There are; snatches of humorous
fooling in “ Humpty Dumpty,” and the
parts that savor most strongly of musi-
cal comedy are not wholly unconnected with
the name part in a harmless necessary story in-
tended to appeal to the young. In short,
“ Humpty Dumpty ” is sufficient of a panto, as
times go, and quite enough of a gorgeous en-
tertainment for anybody. It has rather the
effect of an ordinary Christmas annual in
which some of the latter scenes have trans-
ferred to the first act, and some of the early ex-
travagance has been held back for the finish.
These departures from old precedent give an un-
warranted suspicion of novelty to the show.
Humpty, it seems, is a malignant person, con-
trolled by a demon king and a good fairy—a sort
of demon understudy—with power to ao every-
thing but marry the Princess. He creates a
hard-faced mother for himself, and causes the
local dyspeptic monarch, who is Bert Gilbert, to
marry the mother aforesaid, who is Harry
Phydora. And thereby Humpty becomes a super-
natural heir to the tin crown of his stepfather.
But he fails to marry the Princess. Finally he
turns magnanimous, and presents the young lady
to the principal boy—an excellent arrangement,
seeing that Florence Young plays principal boy,
and her hearty, healthy way of acting and singing
is calculated to rouse Princess Maude Thorne
to energy.
* * * *
Miss Thorne is merely a negative subject for
praise. She is prettier and more gracefully
feeble than Olive Morrell, the Princess of last
year. Another pair of lovers are the shapely
Mabelle Morgan and little Irene Dillon. The
latter has one song and dance, and would be an
excellent soubrette if she were to hold herself
more upright. Of the others in minor characters,
Harry Shine has hardly enough to do, whereas
the animal impersonators do a little too much.
Their best animal is a baby elephant that goes
mad fore and aft. Bert Barton is a serious,
well-spoken Humpty Dumpty, and the come-
dians who raise a generous crop of laughs
from the barren panto, soil are Bert Gilbert and
Phydora. The Gilbert of to-day is the slim, dry
quaint joker of ten years ago, very little altered
in method. He has a melodious voice for
catchy songs, and his ballad of love, with an
accompaniment of four-and-twenty girls, is a
notable hit. He is humorous by himself, and
even funnier in association with Phydora, who
presents a middle-aged female in a number of
different wigs and garments, and is a highly
respectable joke all the time. As far as a
panto, is based on scenic effect, this one
is built upon a rock. The ballet at the
end of the first act is an undeniable
“boshter ”in all respects. The blending of color
in scenery and dresses is as pretty as the dancing
and the girls. The last scene, more daring than
the rest in the color scheme, is another fine
gathering of young females, but these special
attractions for bald-headed man are naughty com-
pared with the intermezzo spectacle, which goesone
better than the swing scene of last year. This
time it is a revolving wheel, glittering with elec-
tric light and radiant with girls. Every spoke of
the wheel is alive with girls. They com? down on
the near side like a cataract—an arithmetical
stream of girl emblematic of the Waters of Life
which make thirsty where they most do satisfy.

** * *
On Boxing Night the second of Melba’s
concerts at the Exhibition Building will help to
relieve the streets of the metropolis of their
holiday foot traffic. Early last week the question
was debated as to whether the Melba rush would
be greater for the first of the Exhibition concerts
or the second, but it is now generally understood
that the persons who hesitated were lost on
Saturday night last. The booking for both con-
certs was gigantic.
** * *
The Wirths are mourning the loss of two
notable performers of the gee-gee species. The
circus train ran off the line, and in the caps;ze-
ment the educated pony, Fairy, was killed.
Fairy used to do a “turn ” in conjunction with a
big horse, and he left no understudy to take his
place. The same accident put an end to the
jumping career of Playmate, who was a record-
breaker at the business. If the game little animal
recovers from his injury, he will be only a pen-
sioner of the circus—useless for show purposes.
** * *
West, of the wonderful picturegraphs,
opened a big season before a fair audience at
Wirths’ Olympia, in Melbourne, on Saturday
night. That commodious edifice has been
floored throughout, and gives seating ac-
commodation for 4000. Most of them
were there, and they seemed really happy,
despite the fact that a torrid afternoon had re-
duced most people to a sort of viscous condition
not conducive to calm and patience, However,
while the big brass band discourses beauteous
music a number of young Swedes come hurling
through space on ski, and you almost feel the cool
wind of their passage. Then a dog rescues bank-
notes from a burglarious assassin, and King Ned,
looking surprisingly well and stout, commences to
lift his tile to the multitude. Altogether this
picture show is fine ; also it is cool.
** * *
J. T. West, who is one of the world’s old
masters among the photographers and showmen
of animated pictures, has a fixed policy in cater-
ing for the masses. He holds that his public
should be treated as persons of some intelligence.
In so far as their taste may incline to blood-and-
thunder melodrama and cheap sensationalism,
they will get what they want at the theatre, and
from the newspapers. They go to the cinemato-
graph show in a general way for entertainment,
educational or oddly amusing, the “comic” pic-
tures being addressed to juveniles to the same
extent that a panto or circus is. And the busi-
ness of West’s Pictures, says the experienced
dealer, is to cultivate more and more the public
taste for subjects of intellectual interest rather than
to enlarge Deadwood Hick’s sphere of influence.
Ten years ago the phonograph records were
mainly restricted to jingling music and American
jokes. There has been a remarkable advance in
the average quality of the subjects, as well as in
the production of them. The ordinary unculti-
vated person has just as much capacity for artistic
feeling as the somewhat cultivated ordinary per-
son, who poses in admiration of an inscrutable
“Whistler.” But you can’t develop his capacity
by giving him gory melodrama in dumb show,
says West.
* # * -*
The company of amateurs that presented
“The Tyranny of Tears ” in style at Her Majesty’s
(Melb.) two or three months back, gave another
performance at P’ran New Town Hall last Wednes-
day night before a supposedly distinguished audi-
ence. The actual performance didn’t transpire till
8.20 p.m., a mandolin orchestra and some attractive
nurses (vending lollies and things) getting in more
or less deadly work meanwhile. However, the
show was, on the whole, very good, even allowing
for the dreadful acoustic properties of the hall.
Mr. D. M. Beere has improved on his Parbury to
some appreciable extent. His work, notably in
the third act, was excellent, and so superior to
that of the others as to make the contrast a little
ridiculous. Fred Newman, as the Colonel, is
still prone to accentuate the tremendous im-
portance of an apologetic old joker who is
stuck for a word; while the cynical bachelor
(Mr. Emanuel), though quite tolerable at times,
was often a vexatious person, with a rever-
berating voice. The Misses Pinschof were, as
usual, dressily magnificent, but, artistically, a
little absurd.

Nobody of consequence who wasn’t there
last week will drop into the Christmas bill at
Melbourne Opera House prior to Saturday, any-
how. Seeley and West, who wear respectively a
plum-colored suit and a black face, and are
musically disposed, also Rhodesia and the other
people, will greet the glad New Year in Mel-
bourne. If they are called away to another State
the notice of removal will appear in next Bulle-
tin. Meanwhile, it is not too late to remark that
Hayman and Franklin should discover a new
duet if they are to remain worthy of larger type
than Terry and Bentley. The “ Hebrew Imperson-
ators ” are practically a one-man turn, whereas
Terry and Bentley are a double event, and the
lady half of the firm fills her place in a graceful
style of her own. Also, she has a refined speaking
voice, which has been reckoned an excellent thing
in woman ever since the days of Holy Writ.
** * *
Adelaide Tivoli has just completed one
unbroken year of variety show. Bert Sayers and
Brandon Cremer, Rickards’ under-lessees, have
made a success of low prices, with a show always
adequate and occasionally striking. Recent
features have been Antonio’s performing dogs,
with a picture show of William Squires in
America receiving that all-too-sudden Punch that
took the Purse. Pictures are everywhere, and so
are concerts —sometimes the two together.
Wirth’s circus is raging furiously (“seven
shows for one ticket”), and is rivalled by
Skuthorp’s stockyard display, at which outlaws
and bullocks buck all comers to blazes by moon-
light. Andrew Mack has moved on towards
Perth, closing with the old “ Arfah-na-Pogue,”
which was the more attractive by reason of not
having the insistent, unmistakable built-in-
America brand of all Mack’s other plays. The
’Varsity students, having given bond to behave
reasonably at Commemoration, took the Royal
for one night of entertaining foolishness, and had
a packed and high-priced house. Meanwhile,
Manager Pollock, of the Royal, has been out fish-
ing for plays, and now promises that in 1908
Adelaide shall have absolutely everything that
Adelaide can ask for.
A tale of the sea, with an unsatisfactory
ending, came up in Sydney last week. Early in
December the British ship Ancaios, with a crew
made up from many nations, left Newcastle
heavily burdened with coal. As soon as the tug
cast off the crew broke into open mutiny, and
declined to work the ship. For 24 hours the
vessel wandered round the compass without
steerage-way, and with no one at the wheel or on
the lookout. The captain, the mates, and the
ship’s cook tried vainly to get enough sail on the
vessel to carry it off the land, when it|would have
been blown out into the wastes of ocean, and the
men, it wasthought, would haveeithergone to work
for their own safety, or else thrown their officers
overboard. In the end the Ancaios was picked
up by a tug and towed to Sydney. The crew’s
tale as told in court was a lurid account of the
captain’s conduct—so lurid as to cast a doubt on
its accuracy in some minds, for even a very
“tough” skipper seldom begins throwing hard
substances till he is out of sight of land. The
P.M. struck a compromise; he decided that the
men had a distinct grievance, and, all the same,
sentenced them to seven days. This result
has spread alarm among shipmasters. If the men
were justified in their mutiny, they should have
been discharged. If they weren’t, seven days
seems a feeble jest, seeing that the loss already
runs to over £5OO, and the deserted windjammer
is laid up in Sydney with a reputation which
makes the getting of a new crew a matter of vast
difficulty.
AT POVERTY POINT.
Quite the most absurd of the rumors now current in
connection with the opera company which Melba talks of
bringing here next year, is that which credits her with
saying that Jean de Reszke will probably come as tenor.
Jean de Reszke has retired for ever from the operatic
stage. He teaches selected pupils in Paris now at an
enormous fee per 20 minutes lesson. Last year, and the
year before, Hammerstein of N. York offered jhirn the
unprecedented fee of £lO3O a night for a season of 40
nights ; but de Reszke refused, vowing that money could
not buy him into grand opera again. This forbearance
is not due only to the fact that he is an immensely rich
man. The singer is said to have a morbid horror of
sounding one note after his amazing voice shall have
begun to fail.
“ Melb ”: Our half million has thronged into a colossal
amphitheatre, stalls, and dress circle to judge the duel
between Ruby Bona Wallace and Frank Gavan Duffy.
Just the kind of thing Charles Wyndham delights to stage
in London. Titheradge and Mrs. Brough have done it
here. Janet Achurch and Herbert Flemming showed it
in “ Forget-Me-Not,” as likewise did Genevieve Ward and
W. H. Vernon. On the first day Ruby was in black, with
copious white ostrich feathering round her pompadour.
Purves was keel-hauling Charles Wallace, not by any
means in a brutal fashion. Superficial people said he
ought to have been on the other side. Not at all. His>
style, his rasping “ Now, Madam ” would have been preju-
dicial. People said, too, that Duffy ought to have been
Ruby’s champion. No, again. A bigger and more indig-
nant-looking man was wanted as the lady’s champion.
For her great effort Ruby turned up as the Woman in
White, plump as a partridge and mild as a dove. Pallid,
but not exactly nervous. Somewhat languid. Never
fiery. No indignation. Just quiet “Noes.” Ought
not the jury to have been half women ? To this
complexion must we come, with women as voters.
Madden refused to turn them out, when cunning Duffy
suggested that it was not good enough for them. Ruby
ought to have been indulged with a Chair. In general it
was a finely-staged performance. Madden, as a stage
manager, recalls Dot Boucicault. But Dot Boucicault
would certainly have allowed the lady a chair.
The Ruby Adams voice which trilled forth publicly for
the first time last Wednesday, at Sydney Town Hall, is of
a marvellous range. The singer reached D in alt.
during one item. Needless to say (as the junior reporter
would write) Miss Adams wants another bunch of years’
training.
The dragging tail-end of a crop of anecdotes re the'
recent German Opera visitors still leaves a furrow in the
sand of history. For instance, there is a hotel in Maori-
land which will remember little Carl Foller, an excitable
tenor. One day, finding that there were no matches in
his room, he rang the bell but received no answer. He
hung on to the bell like a lost soul hanging to a straw,
but only met the same callous disregard. Becoming
excited, he rushed into the passage in slight undress, and,
calling to his aid a limited repertoire of the British
speech, he greeted the housemaid toiling up the stair’
with the cry of “ Fire ! ” When the girl’s senseless form
had been attended to and the local resources brought into
requisition, it was discovered that Mr. Foller had merely
desired to intimate to the girl that he wanted his gas lit.
The other tenor, Massimio Massimi, made it his unvarying
custom to get sick at sea even in a dead calm. He even
got sick on land when he read a shipping ad.vt. One'
night he fled in disorder from the saloon, leaving his
baggage and artillery behind him. A couple of girls-
followed to find him leaning in a graceful attitude over
the rail. In answer to an innocent query as to whether
the moon was yet up, the unhappy tenor said, sadly, “ It
is, if I’ve eaten it.”
Young Leslie introduced a little gun juggling into the
grand march in “The Girls of Gottenberg ” at Melbourne
Majesty’s; and one night, towards the close of the
season, his musket came down on the head of the prettiest
of the chorus girls, and wiped her right out of the opera
for the time being. She was carried off, and for the next
five minutes the performers were directing such anxious•
looks towards the wings that the audience suspected the
worst. However as nothing has been heard of a burial it
is assumed that Leslie’s gunnery was not fatal. After
that the girls gave the gun-spinner a wide berth.
“8.P.”: The ordinary mummer’s conception of a'
bushman’s customs and “ clobber ” passeth all under-
standing ! Take the biograph picture of “ Robbery Under
Arms,” as faked by Meynell and Gunn. On the screen-
the bushrangers appear armed with the latest, up-to-date
rifles and revolvers, instead of the old cannon-bore Snider,
in use at the time of the story. The next absurdity one
notices is the costumes of the ladies who were mixed up-
with the fortunes of the gang. They are dressed in the
very latest skirts, blouses, and millinery. The mummer
who impersonates George Storefield forgot to remove his
fashionable, three-inch, turned-down collar before posing
for the machine. Then my conception of Warrigul would
be a slim, active, always-alert nigger—not the 11-stone
specimen of the typical “ you-gib-it-t’chickapence-boss,”
public-house nig depicted on the screen. Again, I don’t
think that the ’rangers were in the habit of turning out
dressed in immaculate white riding pants and shirts and
highly-polished top-boots ; and the Australian bushman
does not mount his horse backwards (standing behind the
saddle, with his face towards the horse’s head), though
that’s the way they invariably mount, according to the
biograph. All these things make me say—well, “Drat it.”
“ Theorbo ” : Just after my recommendation, in these’
pages, for an Australian grand opera co., tumbles in the
news that Melba is on the job. That was the business
end of her jaunt. Of course Musgrove will be in it, with
all his stock of scenery, costumes, and scores. It may be
assumed that Melba’s constant friend, Madame Calvb,.
will come out. Melba sings Michaela to her Carmen, and
has also obliged by taking the small part of Queen Mar-
guerite in “ The Huguenots.” I think Melba was nervous
about facing the London season. A cruel and false rumor
was circulated in America last season that she had broken
down, but she never sang better. Yet the spell
was advisable. She is going ,to give Australia all the
Wagner operas. I fancy they will be in Italian, from the
manner in which she sketches out her arrangements for
Italian and French artists.

A bounding wire-walker now on exhibition in Australia
is jbilled as the best on earth, and the most astonishing
skipper on a taut string that ever amazed mortal man.
But, good as this handsome miracle is, he cannotmaintain
all these claims while little Oaicedo still lives. Oaicedo
is the slim, limber, Venezuelan, who came out to orna-
ment a Williamsonian pantomime some years ago, and
imparted so many thrills with his sensational work that
a nervous manager could not bear to see him revolve,
and used to hide his head in the office so as not to witness
the seemingly inevitable tragedy. Oaicedo still goes on
hitting tbe wire with two infallible feet, and is as
clever and daring as ever, although now the grandfather
of three children, and the father of fourteen.

Mrs. Blanche Arral—just now appearing in Melbourne
under the direction of John Wren—has been allegedly
identified by an ex-attache of the Austrian Legation at
Paris, and this mysterious informant has “ let on ” to a
Melbourne Herald reporter. Mrs. Blanche, he says, is a
Princess on her first husband’s side —Princess Sergius
Nicholas de Peschlcoff. Also her age is 33, and she “ was
celebrated all through Europe ” as Claire de Hardinois
before the Prince met her “ in the flush of her great
success” and married her. Then she went to St. Peters-
burg with his Highness, her husband, and sat
on his family seat for two years. But there
was her own personal family to be considered,
“her father having been a count.” So she went to
Brussels to revisit her noble ancestry, and when she re-
turned to St. Petersburg she found that the Prince was
dead, and his ruthless Russian relatives had seized his
real and personal estate, “ together with a large sum of
money belonging to herself.” Ever since then she has
been endeavoring to recover her property and the third
of her husband’s estate. The earnestness of her endea-
vor, by the way, is evident in the fact—vide Melbourne
Herald— that she resumed singing under her old name,
and went to Java and Tonkin, and finally came to
Australia disguised as Blanche Arral —the first Prin-
cess on record who ever disguised herself for
professional singing purposes. Whilst she was thus
endeavoring to recover her property in Russia she
met Mr. Bassett in America, and married him.
He had previously fought for his country (and his strong
Yankee accent) “ with the rank of captain in the First
•Ohio Infantry,” but he cared ho more for rank than his
wife cared. He came to Australia as Mr. Bassett—plain
“ mister”—in spite of his being a captain wedded to a
Princess. This is an amazing narrative, all things con-
sidered, yet it stops short of the whole truth. The
attache of the Austrian Legation at Paris, yielding to
the solicitations of John Wren and Barney Reynolds,
must have decided to draw it mild. Blanche Arral, as
far as The Bulletin knows to the contrary, is really the
rightful Queen of Barataria. She was stolen from her
•cradle by the Shah of Persia, who was walking in his
•sleep at the time. Fortunately she escaped to Paris in a
balloon, and was received with unbounded enthusiasm,
•especially in comic opera. Some time after her arrival
in Australia her birdlike vocalism attracted the attention
of Mr. Wren, who is a bit of a bird himself, and Mr. W.
•offered to back his new' attraction to beat Melba in a 20ft.
•sing, under Marchesi rules.
Mrs. Melba’s booking for her two “popular” concerts
•at Melbourne Exhibition Building wipes out every indi-
vidual record made in Australia, and will probably repre-
sent the diva’s best score for two performances :
Old Santa Olaus came down the flue,
And stood by Melba’s cot;
Her modest stocking hung in view—
He looked at what he’d got.
Then took the Earth, and crammed it in,
“ Here, Nellie,” growled the mannikin,
“Just take the bloomin’ lot! ”
“ Harriso* O.” : For the last few years I have made a
careful study of the methods resorted to by actresses,
singers, and others for the purpose of gaining advertise-
ment. There are at least four persons who may be said
to have brought the art (for it is an art) of self-advertise-
ment almost to perfection. They are King O’Malley
(picturesque), Judkins (grotesque), Tom Bent (burlesque),
and Miss Clara Butt (materfamiliesque). The first is a has-
been, the second never was, and the third soon will be ;
but the Lady w»ith the Voice is still fairlyNup to date,
thanks to the help of Puppa, Buba, Joy and others.
Then take another instance—Tittell Brune. Owing to
absence of kids this lady had to fall back upon the
“ dear-r gur-rls ” dodge, combined with scrupulously
regular visits to kirk, but this worked only moderately
well. The domesticated and pious systems are generally
•successful, though Peggy Pryde, when high-kicking her
way to fame, adopted somewhat different tactics.
“ Ruru ” (Maoriland): Your London correspondent
(Poverty Point, 28/11/’O7) remarks that AddieLorking, the
Maoriland child actress, would make an ideal Peter Pan,
“ if she is still the Addie that she was.” On that point there
is no doubt. She has grown beyond the infant pheno-
menon stage (not that she ever was an I.P. in the oppro-
brious or Crummies, sense of the term), and is now a tall
•slip of a girl of fifteen or so, and pretty as a picture.
:Saw her recently playing the leading part in a Japanese
play staged here in aid of a local charity, and for charm
■of manner she would be hard to beat, while her acting is
•striking for its humor and brightness.
THE GODS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Come, fill a golden cruse, and pour
Black rams’ blood on the sods,
With meal, and milk, and honey, for
Oblation to the gods.
We’ll crown great bowls of ruby wine,
Hymn Dionysius’ praise;
And round dull prophets’ brows entwine
Garlands of singing days.
We’ll drink the sun to rest; that done,
There’ll be no sad goodbye,
Till stars are paling, and the sun
Once more relights the sky.
Upon the beach of Night are strewn
Pale singing shells called stars—
Fair Aphrodite’s scented zone,
Pan’s pipes, the sword of Mars.
The gods still live ; a lucky chance
Has marked them in the skies ;
Chant “ Evoe ! ” beat the drum, and dance
In joyful sacrifice.
In every cup a reveller quaffs;
A satyr, too, takes part;
A nymph of Aphrodite laughs
In sighing maiden’s heart.
Where sullen Saturn wheels and turns,
And mystic evening gleams
With phantasy, our incense burns
To Hermes, god of dreams.
Then, fill a golden cruse, and pour
Black rams’ blood on the sods,
With pious Pagan rapture for
Oblation to the gods.
VIC.
LOUIS ESSON.
A NAME.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
TIC.
“ What’s in a name ? ” the poet queries ;
Such foolishness my spirit wearies ;
There is no question as to which’ll
Draw bigger crowds—“ Melba ” or “ Mitchell."

PENEOLD'S
WINES
“Alba,” from England: In four days I
h ive looked upon four kings—and the blight con-
tinues. Haakon, of Norway, handsomest of all,
pissed yesterday with Maud' of England, and his
jangling blue and gold postillions. Alfonso, of
the horse face and the underlip, is a dreadful
example of high breeding and low thinking.
While in London he ran amok in his motor car, a
fact carefully suppressed by the great slobber
dailies. While in France, Alfonso, of the moke’s
profile, twice lost his baby, the dear infant
Asturias and heir-presumptive. Nobody prose-
cuted him for attempted child-desertion. But
what a man is this German Kaiser, with the
catarrh-inflamed eyes and the uplifted mo !
The recent Moltke-Harden revelations have
shaken him to his roots. He is like one who has
been allowed a peep into that glittering and
wonderful machine the German army—only to
find it corroded with the acid of Gomorrah. One
cannot help a touch of pity for Wilhelm 11., for
he was the man and his the army that should have
shaken the throat of Japan. To-day Europe
stands aghast at this dry rot, this shadow of
Sodom which threatens Germany’s white legions.
Wilhelm has come to England to cover his head.
And Edward VII.. who never got up at 3 a.m. to
whoop his army out of bed at dawn, met him at
Windsor station with a big cigar. In these days
of leper armies and Congo horrors, it is a relief to
meet a king who confines himself to his big cigars.
GAY DOGS.
SYDNEY : “Oh , George, I do feel wicked!"
GEORGE : “So do I, Sydney; what shall we do? ’
SYDNEY: “I have it! Let’s ride home in a
smoking compartment."
Tait has introduced to the Victorian
railways another Yankee railway notion —the
“Stop, look, and listen” notice at cross-
ings. There is a legend that some American
cos. years ago hired an expensive lawyer to
frame a notice that would block legal proceedings
in case of accidents at crossings, and the bill for
these four words, which the expensive lawyer
produced, ran to £5OO.
Melbourne and district are a trifle con-
cerned about the apparent ease with which
criminal lunatics evaporate from Yarra Bend
these times, and go into general circulation again.
James Ryan, a homicidal gentleman whose
specialty is train-wrecking, walked out of the
asylum a few weeks ago, or jumped out, or fell
out, or flew—anyhow, he got out. So railway
travellers in the vicinity of the metropolis
gather a little additional uneasiness from
the possibility of Mr. Ryan being engaged
building a cairn of rocks or running up a log
barricade ahead of the locomotive. Since then
there have been three other escapes, and in each
case the runaway is a man who is apt to pat his
fellow creatures with an axe. The asylum
authorities have been unable to discover the leak,
and the police cannot find theleakers. What with a
lunatic asylum with a hole in it, and the likelihood
of Bent breaking into song at any moment, the
disadvantages of living in Melbourne are obvious,
and people are crying for the breaking up of big
estates so that they may go on the land.
The duties of the present-day State school
teacher are many, and, as a rule, they are per-
formed cheerfully and well. In the old times it
was considered sufficient if the kid-whacker
slammed into the infant a knowledge of the three
R’s, but the palmy days of teaching are gone, and
it is just as important now, from a departmental
point of view, anyhow, that the teacher should
have a knowledge of the bad habits of
the native bear, and the domestic arrangements
of the wagtail, as he has of grammar. A species
of carpentering has before now been indulged in,
but the lords of the Education Department have
not, as yet. taken any official notice of pick and
shovel work. Until they do , one teacher, at least,
is not going to include it in the day’s work. She
is a lady stationed in the Wimmera (Vic.), and has
charge of a school which is built in the vicinity of
a water channel belonging to the Western Wim-
mera Water Trust. The channel—like most
things pertaining to a Trust —is badly in need of
repair. Every now and then it goes on the burst,
and the lady teacher adjourns the lecture on the
politics of the boiled owl, and scampers forth at
the head of the scholars to effect repairs, and pre-
vent the school being washed away. This per-
formance has been going on for years, and the
lady has now written to the Trust, saying that
she’s sick of it, and that as the whole thing could
be repaired properly for about 405., she will be
obliged if the Trust will send down a real man to
do the job. Evidently, the Trust feels a little
ashamed of itself, for it has written in haste to
the teacher, informing her that the plumber will
call.
• * * *
Thus the late “Medi-Cuss” in his last
letter from the North
Up to a few weeks since I had marked down in
memory’s tablets Hungerford, Euriowie and Milparinka as
the three most God-forgotten spots on the footstool —this
after more than 20 years’ battling about Australasia.
But that was before I visited in Southern Queensland, and
made acquaintance with Biranbrandi (locally known as
beer and brandy), Hebei and Tego.
At the first-named Eden there is a post office, an aban-
doned school, a store, and the most ramshackle pub in
the universe, and how the people exist bangs Banagher.
Yet the place must be fairly healthy, and the publican a
straight-goer, for it was told, as an absolute fact, that in
the 35 years the present owner had been in the premises
there had never been a death in the place.
However, we got there a few days too soon, for shortly
after leaving Mrs. Landlady passed out, and the eternal
monotony of things was still further broken by the wild
and woolly myalls of the district, a tribe consisting of two
or three aged bucks and a toothless gin, who held up the
pub while the funeral party was absent, and comman-
deered the entire liquid stock-in-trade.
En route to Hebei, at a noon camp, we pulled up to
replace with wire a lost hub band, splice a broken spring-,
and replace some missing bolts. There battlers along
that fine and large sample of local government roads may
behold, printed on a board affixed to one of the tall gate-
posts on the rabbit-proof fence, the following chaste and
truthful legend :
“ This trail is not quite endless, let the pilgrim mark it
well,
Ten miles north is Biranbrandi, while forty south is
Hell.”
As monotony-breakers our party certainly shone, for
while at Hebei the language used over the poor prospects
of business so worked up the nervous system of the soli-
tary trooper’s moke that he bolted five miles, and the
bobby came near having to arrest himself for endeavoring
to cross the N.S. W. border without a tick inspection
permit. •
At Tego, certainly the tail-end of all creation, while
buying some tobacco at the little shanty called a store,
the parson’s son, who was playing David to my Jonathan,
and, incidentally, trying to drive the soul-cases out of a
pair of good horses, remarked to the forlorn-looking
woman who was serving us, “Great Heaven! is there
anything to be hoped for in a miserable spot like this?”
“ Yes,” came the quick reply, “death.”
And but a few days later that hoped-for event arrived
for that poor little human waif, per medium of a bullet
from a Winchester rifle, fired into her back while she was
out looking for horses.
Not far from Tego, on the N.S.W. side of the border, at
still another blot on the landscape, we came across a bush
school in charge of a young and charming girl, who re-
ceives from this Deform Government the colossal sum of
£26 per year—a living example of the true cussedness of
that department that is a disgrace to the State, and
which J. Pannikin, Esq., speaks of as being “ wise and
generous.”
Asked how she managed to put in existence, the young
lady said that it kept her so busy figuring how she was to
spend her bloated income that she had no time for con-
sidering her miserable surroundings.
My opinion of the worse than criminal meanness of the
heads of a department that so damnably sweats women
would, if expressed in print, procure me three years free
board and lodging ; but as I hope to ere long see the day
when an outraged public opinion will rise and hang some
of the callous, bell-toppered gang responsible for a state
of thing’s that would disgrace a Sydney rag shop, I refrain
for the present, as I want to help pull on the rope when
the joyful time arrives.
LIFE.—(FROM THE BEETLE'S POINT OF VIEW.)
That Sydney divorce-court story of the
two wealthy spinster sisters who implored a male
person to marry one or other of them is somewhat
remindful of the private and confidential narrative
of Mr. Gee, long deceased, whose name survives
in the English Turf records. But the Gee story
was sweetly sentimental in its way, and the hero
was a rich and joyful widower ere it became well
known. He had been butler in the service of two
prim maiden ladies, who paid him so handsomely
that in due course his British butler’s thoughts
turned towards a pub. He intended to retire from
livery and take his ease at his own country inn,
like ten thousand Jeameses before him. When he
gave notice, the two gracious sisters were
simply appalled at the prospect of a new man
in the old place. Life without their accus-
tomed Gee seemed not worth living. They
begged him to reconsider his resolve, but he
was adamant. Then they waited upon him
in a deputation to suggest a compromise.
Would he remain as the husband of either
of them, and manage the household as
before ? Whichever one he cared to
select for a wife, the other would be a
contented sister-in-law. So Gee bowed
his buttling head respectfully, and
replied that—if it made no difference
■ —he would prefer Miss Lucretia,
who was a little younger than Miss
Tabitha. Whereupon they thanked
him, and named the happy day.
When the carriage arrived to
take the bridal party of three to
the village church, Gee, in plain
clothes, obsequiously held the
door of the kerridge open whilst
the ladies took their seats. He
refused their pressing invitation
to come inside and be the bride-
groom-elect. He felt that he had
not ceased to buttle. If it made no
difference, he said, he would prefer
to ride on the box beside the coach-
man going to the church, but would
assume his position of lawful husband
for the return journey. So Gee and
Lucretia were married, and lived
happily ever after, and the fortune of
Tabitha, who predeceased her sister, melted into
the family estate. Anon, when his good straight-
laced wife was laid under the turf, the bereaved
Gee went on it and broke into a gentle canter, so
to speak.
* * * *
A Port Melbourne wife had a sweet re-
venge the other evening. The old man had just
given the father of a hiding to the lady’s own
particular “ Bob,” whereupon the pair of males
wen run in for riotous behavior. Then the sweet
dame turned up at the lock-up, bailed out the
tertium quid, and let the old man cool his eye in
the cell all night. But it was his turn next day ;
pleading the “ unwritten law” in certain events,
the beaks discharged him.
The Congregational Union of Victoria
demanded, the other day, that the State should
abolish “marriage scandals” by suppressing
matrimonial agencies. While Bent was consider-
ing how to say something indefinite, Government
Statist Drake chipped in with a memorandum
stating that one Congregational parson regularly
advertised his willingness to marry the whole
world at cut rates, while the largest agency in the
city relied on the services of another Congrega-
tional minister ; yet, though the Union had been
asked if it approved of these capers, it had appar-
ently not only said nothing to the parsons
referred to, but hadn’t even the manners
to reply to the query. Presumably the
Union hasn’t got any control over these
parsons; yet, as long as they can get half a
dozen or so people to pretend to be their “con-
gregations,” they can hang on to their marrying
licenses indefinitely. The sect’s confession that
it can’t regulate the business is the best
evidence that the State should—by appointing its
own servants to do the job. Then, if the public
wanted frills hung round the business, the parson
could come in—just, by the way, as the “dis-
senting ” parson comes in in dear old Hingland.

Following, from current London Sporting
Times, is the class of reminiscence that is read
with avid interest by a frequent type of Britisher :
t remember I was once standing with the old Duke of
Cambridge and the late Duke of Teck at a theatre when
an ignorant attendant addressed the latter as “Your
Royal Highness,” and the Commander-in-Chief as “ Your
Grace.” Neither moved a muscle, but both were inwardly
amused, and though they did not utter a word, their eyes
said unspeakable things. It was highly amusing.
Now, in the name of Sheol, where is the joke ?

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Last evening-, a man was found lying in Commercial-
r >ad, South Melbourne, in an unconscious condition. He
was taken to the hospital, and the doctors discovered
concussion of the brain, and other serious injuries. The
man’s name is unknown. There is, as yet, no means of
ascertaining how the damage was inflicted.— Melb. Aon.
Dear Bulletin,—Hush, whisper low,
And to the doors on tip-toe go.
Is no one lurking ?
A grim confession I would make,
With hair a-stir and lenses a-quake,
And eyeballs jerking.
It is my work ! He met me there.
The sun was high, the day was fair,
The breeze beguiling.
He seized me by the left lapel,
As one who has a tale to teli.
His face was smiling.
He held me gaily to the spot.
I lingered, little dreaming what
Mad maggot bit him.
He said : “ I want to ask you if
You know why Mrs. Wallace ” Biff !
’Twas then I hit him.

A PRAYER TO THE ARCHBISHOP.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
If Canon Nash
Has done a “ mash ”
With maid or widdy,
Why keep it dark,
Archbishop Clark ?
Is he a “ spark 1 ”
A shepherd giddy?
Your gaitered Grace
Should state a case
To give us solace,
When comes to pass
The end, alas !
Of “ Mrs. Wallace.’*
If Canon Nash
Was gay or rash,|
O, tell the story !
AVe want, forsooth,
The blessed truth,
The words, the acts ;
O, state the facts
For Heaven’s glory ! !
Plank down the Proof,
Don’t stand aloof
And look so haughty
Prelate, speak out,
Dispel the doubt —
Say something naughty.

309=311 GEORGE STREET, SYDNEY.
Now it is Sydney Municipal Council that
is up against the Asiatic problem. At Bel more
produce markets there are several Chow stall-
holders ; also, just across the street there is a long
string of shops, wherein some more Chows vend
the fruits of the earth to unpatriotic Australians.
Market stall-holders are charged per load ot pro-
duce taken into the building. Stall John takes in
his little load in the morning, and sometimes that
little load lasts a long time. Seemingly Stall John
is satisfied to have the stock on his hands. He will
supply the purchaser of a small lot, but the
customer requiring something a little larger than
a small lot, Stall John suavely conducts to his
pal, Shop John, across the way. Generally the'
customer is flattered by Stall John’s courtesy, and
goes over to Shop John. So Stall John is satisfied
(he keeps his barrow supplied, and saves further
market dues); Shop John is satisfied, and the
customer is satisfied. The only people who are
not satisfied are the white stall-holders, who
haven’t any adjacent shop-confederates, and have
to pay further dues every time they want more
supplies; and the City Council, which objects to
its markets being made a mere touting place for
Shop John. Also naturally the Council objects to
the loss of the revenue that would accrue to it if
Stall John used the markets for the purpose for
which he is allegedly there. So the Council is
profoundly considering what to do about Stall
-John.
Hood, of the Victorian Supreme Court,
t’other day slid back into the age when every per-
son whose mind happened to be temporarily or
permanently clouded was chained up to the
nearest weighty log and left there. He had be-
fore him a man guilty of an aimless attempt to
murder Samuel Jones, General Superintendent
of Transportation on the Victorian Railways.
Jones’ official title, nailed on to him by Tait from
Canada, appears to be the only excuse which the
accused man had for shooting at him. It was
shown in the defence that the prisoner had pre-
viously been confined in a lunatic asylum, and
that he had had frequent fits. In sentencing the
man to four years’ imprisonment, with hard labor,
Hood declared that “even if the prisoner suffered
from attacks of lunacy, he would be better in gaol
than wandering about taking fits and shooting
people.” Perhaps by this time Hood realises the
sheer brutality of that remark.

“ D.’s” plaint: —
Have been for years an earnest student of the eterna
type of feminine who goes in for street collecting. She
is mostly, in private life, of a Vere de Vere pride. There
are but two occasions on which she will suffer the
advances of the strange male, viz. ; (1) When she has
been run over by a tram ; (2) when she adheres to a
wooden collecting-box. So far as looks are concerned,
she is, as a genus or clan, well worth any ordinary mate’s
advances. In fact, she is for the most part prepossessing,
not to say alluring. Taken at the correct moment (viz., im-
mediately after a tram has passed over her, or a collection
box been dealt her, as the case may be) she will be found
to be far from unfriendly. Again she is mainly Hebrew.
