In the Wallace case Barrister Duffy has
been stringing up witness after witness on the
spikes of the bunyip’s older brother, the dilemma.
“Sosso” Rogers met the trouble early in the
proceedings. Asked “Supposing a woman dis-
honored herself with you, would you admit it, or
would you lie?’ he hesitated for a long while,
and finally petitioned for more time to consider
the matter. After lunch the cruel, persistent
Duffy asked him if he was ready now, but had to
be satisfied with the non-committal reply
that “he couldn’t answer thequestion.” “Sosso’s”
trouble, of course, was, that if he said he would lie
about the matter, the jury would be invited to re-
gard Sos. as a person who owned up to a capacity
for perjury; while if Sos. said he would tell the
truth, the jury would ask itself what it thought of
a person who would put the lady away. Nobody
has ever been known to put a brand on a dilem-
ma, but it’s a well-known and horrible beast all
the same—especially this sort of dilemma. Yet
after all, “Sosso” had a short and simple way
out. “Haven’t the faintest idea; which would
you advise me to do ? ” would have met the case.
Or he might simply have said that if such a case
arose he would take legal advice before acting.
“ S.” : The Burke and Wills picture by
Longstaff is now suspended in the Victorian
National Gallery, and the verdict is that it was
“ worth waiting for.” Many efforts were made
to rush the artist, and it must be Admitted that
seven years is a long hesitation. From this point
of view Longstaffs painting is a work of time, if
not of eternity. Better, however, wait seven
years for a good picture than seven months for a
had one, and this painting is worthy of the subject.
In dimensions it is only inferior to the drop curtain
“ Moses ” and the back-cloth “Brigands,’’ bvtitis
miles ahead of both as a work of art The
figures are life-size, the men, the atmosphere, the
“feel” are all true, and the story of the lost ex-
plorers is more pathetic and convincing to me on
this canvas than it has been in any other form.
The work is highly dramatic, of course ; it is a
dramatic theme—a tableau—but there is nothing
theatrical, nothing over-emphasised. The draw-
ing is admirable and the paint is good—it breathes.
By far the best Australian historical painting to
date.
The other day the Law in Victoria was
asked to presume the death of Francis Evans —he
left Melbourne for Fiji in 1876 and had never re-
appeared—so that his children might get the
remains of his estate before they died of extreme
old age. Evans was understood to have died in
Fiji shortly after arriving there, his mate
(Moroney) having written his mother to that
effect, and sent his personal effects, along with
some £7 in cash. Unfortunately the 30-year-old
letter had been lost, and Moroney had been lost
also. The Law put on its wisest look and inquired
if there was any record of Evans’ death. There was
not, registrars being less numerous than cannibals
in the South Seas at that time. Then the Law said
the certificate of his birth might be of some use,
though it didn’t specify in what way. Counsel
replied that, though Francis had been born in
Victoria in the early ’fifties, there was no record
of his birth either. Here the law put an ertcra-
profound look on its countenance an£ ooserved
that so far as the evidence there was no
Francis Evans whatsoever and never had been ;
in fact, he had never existed, and the whole
thing was merr, imagination, like Anti-Sosh or
Reid’s FreeVade policy. Counsel observed that
the estate wasn’t imagination, anyhow, there
being a .ew hundreds left to Francis or his
children by a brother, who died a while back.
Having pondered deeply for a space and con-
sidered the smallness of the estate and the large-
ness of Costs, the Law said it might be as well to
declare Francis dead, and serve out the estate.
But it did so with hesitation and grave doubts,
because if Francis did hereafter transpire he
would be a mere corpse, a late lamented, and his
claim bn the estate would be gone, and his name
Mud. Francis will, therefore, please accept this
intimation that he is expected to remain dead.
THE FUNNY MAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. Keir Hardie’s reply was mainly in humorous strain.
—Argus.
The sweated mortals of the slums
Gry out in pain. Who will assist
The Cause ? Their saviour-hero comes
Along—a blessed humorist !
Hark to the sounds of starving scum—
The noise of feeble curses hissed
From filthy darkness ! Brass and drum
. Bellow —here comes the humorist !
Grey hands of hate wave in the air,
The slop-wright’s claw, the navvy’s fist,
They beckon and implore—and there
He grins, the English humorist !
The gloomy toilers of the Pit
The joys of life have mostly missed.
“Ah, yes, indeed—l’ve mentioned it
In speeches ! ” brays the humorist.
A piebald brotherhood —a throng
Of mongrel men—is on his list.
A White Australia? Hear the long,
Wild laughter of the humorist!
VIC.
FRANK HENTY.
AUSTRALIA’S STOMACH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There is one urgent circumstance, however, about
which no one seems to agitate with much enthusiasm—
namely that of Australia’s stomach.—Bulletin, 5/12/’O7.
Sing a song of Tummy,
Listen to my plea !
Leave off learning Sanskrit
At the ’Varsity.
Never mind your ’ologies
Stupid LL.D. ;
Learn about your “ innards,”
Give up drinking tea.
You have learnt “ inhaling
Through the nose ”? ’Tis well ;
Also, some Theology
Teaching Death and Hell!
Oh ! These silly isms
Taught by silly fools
Knowing naught of Tummy,
, Tummy or his rules !
I, enthusiastic,
For the outcast pray,
And I sadly warn you
Turn will have his day !
Oh, that poor old Tummy
Day and night you cram
With all sorts of horrors—
Pickles, mustard, jam !
Mute and unprotesting
Tummy is, until
He goes out of business
Having had his “ fill ”!
Now my song is finished.
Off to Paris House
Gaily goes dear Tummy
, Quiet as a mouse !
N.S.W.
HELEN JEROME.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The Wade and Waddell loan estimates, which
tore through the N.S.W. Parliament the other evening as
though they were a motor-car, furnish some more
evidence that the Oarruthers Public Works Fund is the
fraud The Bulletin always said it was. Oarruthers, bent
on getting a surplus somehow, dropped his revenue
into his right-hand trousers pocket. Then he looked at
the bills, and paid the washerwoman her sevenpence, and
tuppence for a bootlace, and found he had 15 bills left
and a handful of silver to pay them with. But instead
of paying the 15 bills out of what was left in the right-
hand pocket, Oarruthers’idea was to call it “surplus,”
and shift it over to the other pocket, and then pay the
bills out of that pocket. Nobody was any better off
in the end, because Oarruthers would have had to pay
the 15 bills anyhow, and he didn’t have any more to pay
them with than if he hadn’t done any juggling. But the
poor little trick allowed Oarruthers to pose as a great
financier who produced surpluses, and spent them on
“permanent and reproductive” undertakings hitherto
paid for out of loan money.
Yet, in spite of that Fund, the Wade and
Waddle Ministry now turns up with a demand for
£2,470,981 loan money, also £1,500,000 for the renewal
of an old lOU ; and included in this £2,470,981, for which
Oohen is to be tapped as soon as possible if he isn’t
tapped already, provision is made for such things as “17
engines reconstructed and reboilered, £28,017 ” ; “ duty on
40 engines imported, £14,247”; a quantity of wire-
netting, additions to rolling stock, the making
of new roads, additions to various buildings, etc.
Such a fraud is the N.S.W. Public Works Fund that even
the repairing of engines is charged to loans ; in other
words, works that were paid for with borrowed money in
the first place wear out, and the patch that is put on the
hole is paid for with more borrowed money, so that a
patched engine has to stagger along under- two loads of
debt, and has to struggle to earn two lots of interest; and
if it breaks down in the process, then the shortage has to
be made up out of more taxation. Instead, in fact, of the
Public Works Fund being used to supply permanent and
reproductive works out of revenue, it is obvious that it
isn’t even paying for repairs and renewals, not to men-
tion such unproductive things as new streets, and such
perishable goods as wire-netting.
Some day all Australian banks will be required
to balance on the same day, and then their figures will be
worth more for comparative purposes. Also there will
be other advantages. Some banks are very friendly
towards some others ; when they don’t balance on the
same day, one bank can help another, and one lot of
liquid assets can give a fine, substantial look to two
balance sheets. The Eternal Bank balances on Septem-
ber 30 ; the Everlasting Bank on December 31. On Sep-
tember 29 the Everlasting Bank suddenly finds that it
has more coin than it w r ants, so it lends the Eternal
Bank £lOO,OOO, and then the Eternal Bank’s holding of
coin and other liquid assets is very impressive when
the balance sheet comes out. Next month the
Eternal Bank hands back the £lOO,OOO, and in a
few weeks it passes on another £lOO,OOO to the
Everlasting Bank, so that the latter’s holding of
liquid assets shows up fine and large on December 31. So
the window-dressing goes on, and a little stock is made to
go a long way. By and bye there is trouble, and a smash,
and the public says it is so surprised, because the figures
of the Eternal and the Everlasting always looked so good.
If all banks had to make up their accounts on the same
day twice a year—March 31 and September 30 would pro-
bably be the best—this gentle art would be wiped out.

In this last comparison the £40,000 paid as interest to the
State by the Bank of New Zealand is treated as dividend
and capital. The most notable feature, apart from the
large increase in profits disclosed, is the jump in the
amount put to reserves. This, however, is very largely
due to the fact that the Bank of New Zealand
finished its job of filling: up the hole which
was left by the old Private Enterprise regime;
and the profits which for 12 years had gone
that way now go to formal reserves. The (Queensland
National Bank’s figures are under some suspicion. First,
it has a large amount of old deposits on which it is pay-
ing very little; then, under the last arrangement with
the Government, 75 per cent of the net profits have to go
towards repaying old private depositors ; and as this part
of the old private deposits carries no interest, the bank
hasn’t much inducement to magnify the net profits,
especially when it can find plenty of use for them in
writing down old over-valued securities.
• • • • •
Every bank has its own way of doing things :
no two general managers are hatched out of the same
egg. One bank piles up figures : it shows the biggest
profits it can, and the biggest reserves it can. Another
general manager, in charge of the same bank, would use
part of the profits to write down assets. That part of
the profits would in that case not be shown. The Bank
of Australasia, for instance, has taken large sums out of
profits to write its premises down, until they now stand
at £251,836, while the premises account of the A.J.S.
stands away up at £366,742. If the Bank of Australasia’s
premises are worth more than £251,836, then the excess
represents an internal, hidden reserve ; and the amount
used to write the asset down below the real value has
been profit, though it has not been shown. Banks, like
others, have to take the lean with the fat. Some of the
profits they make in the fat years have to be regarded
merely as provision to meet the losses that are inevitable
in lean years. Some banks make that provision without
showing it; in other words, wffen losses do arrive they
won’t have to write down their reserves to meet the
losses. Others don’t. But, as far as they are set out, the
earnings of the various banks, on the basis of capital and
reserves, were as the next table shows. These and the
dividend figures are for the last term for which accounts
have been issued in each year

No comparison of any value can be made between the
Bank of New Zealand’s earnings for the past two years,
calculated on the foregoing basis, because of its altered
financial arrangements ; the State now holds £1,000,000
worth of preference shares, and to that extent has re-
duced the old debt. The Bank of N. S. Wales increased
its capital during the last half-year, issuing new shares at
par : this was equivalent to an increase in the rate of
dividend. The Bank of Adelaide put up its dividend to
9 per cent, in the second half of the year.
When the Bank of New Zealand got into
trouble 14 years ago the most hopeless-looking of the
assets were handed over to a Board for realisation. The
bank helped this Board by handing - over its net profits ;
and so unexpectedly large was this help that 12 months
ago the Board was able to say that it had practically
finished the undertaking business, and was ready for a
quiet funeral in a plain coffin itself. What was left of the
old trouble was then handed back to the bank. The
unrealised assets stood in the hooks at £461,047. They
have since been rushed out at such a rate that on Decem-
ber Ist only £128,401 remained. Thus £332,646 (book
value) of these old assets have been got rid of in I, Tie 12
months. Meantime advances and other assets have in-
creased by £85,754; so the latter is apparently the
amount realised from the sale of old assets which'stood
in the books at £332,646. Figures like that show the size
of the disaster which would have hit Maoriland if the
Government hadn’t gone to the rescue in 1894, and put
the bank in the way of earning big profits to fill the vast
hole which gaped in the accounts.
The Bank of New Zealand disappointed some
people by announcing a div. at the rate of 10 per cent,
per annum at the interim meeting this month. When
ordinaries get 10 per cent., preference shares must get 7£
per cent. ; but preference shares (held by the State) can’t
get beyond 10 per cent., and when they reach that point
ordinaries will be entitled to 15 per cent. The 10
and 15 per cent, dividend would absorb £125,000,
while the annual profits for three years past have gone
beyond £300,000. It is clear that some, anyhow, of the
directors look for a pretty sudden spring in the dividend
to 15 per cent, for ordinaries ; and Martin Kennedy, one of
the oldest of the directors—for a while past associated
with the London Board—made a long speech at the half-
yearly meeting, paving the way for the rise. Unless
something hard and unexpected hits Maoriland during
the next few months, an announcement will very likely
be made at the end of the bank’s current year (March 31).
*■){■**
A few years ago, when the N.B.W. friendly
societies were overhauled by actuarial experts for the
first time, a state of pretty general rottenness was
revealed. In numerous cases “ lodges " were already
unable to pay the promised benefits, and most others
were drifting that way. The Acts of 1899 and 1902, how-
ever, put matters on a scientific basis, and the money
placed to reserve for future claims during the past four
years, viz., £273,029, is more than one,-third of the amount
saved in all the previous 29 years. The “ funeral fund ”
practically the life-insurance branch of the business—
which had been reduced to positively grotesque pro-
portions as the claims naturally increased, has been
augmented by 52 per cent, in four years. It had been the
old business over again : because most of the members
were young, and death claims were few, funds mounted
up ; and, as friendly society men are not as a rule used
to big amounts, the size of the accumulations led them
to either cut down contributions or increase benefits;
and the newer societies adopted the low-contribution
scale. There was general smash looming ahead, when the
State took a hand, and contributions and benefits were
settled on a proper basis. At the end of 1906 there were
107,723 members, with £1,057,496 of funds; the
expenditure for the year was £299,199, of which
cost of management amounted to a moderate 10 - 7
per cent, of the total income, and the year’s lay-by
was £96,628. For many years these societies, or most
of them, were objects of mirth, mainly; but, nowadays,
since the State has interfered and practically provided
them with an actuary, they are taking a place as sound
and desirable business propositions; and when they have
moulted the rest of their flummery, and dropped the habit
of calling one another “Noble Grand,” and “Most Wor-
shipful and Prodigious Buffalo,” they will be worth set-
ting up alongside, say, the A.M.P.

The Wellington (M.L.) Municipal Tramways
accounts for the second year to March 31, 1907, have now
been published. The undertaking began with a deficit of
£32,930, represented by cost of loans, interest during
construction, and expense of altering telegraph lines, etc.
This amount was reduced to £32,113 by a set-off of in-
terest earned on deposits. The position in the two years
has changed in this way :
Deficit.
Preliminary expenses .. .. £32,113
Written Sinking
March, Profit. Interest. Off. Fund.
1906 .. £23,960 £12,098 £7,436 £3064 27,687
1907 .. 34,518 15,778 10,641 4071 19,588
The sinking fund is included among the reserves, which
have gone towards reducing the deficit; but the depreci-
ation fund is not included. After paying interest and
providing for depreciation, the profit was £4426 in
1905-6 and £8099 in 1906-7. The city council includes the
preliminary expenses as an asset, but is writing them off
at the laggard rate of 4 per cent, per annum. In the
foregoing table, of course, they are not reckoned as an
asset.
The total amount of capital raised by way of
loans is £452,423. Of that £409,170 is represented by per-
manent way, rolling stock, and other fixed investments.
(There is a paper item among the fixed investments,
“ Patent rights, trolley-head, £700,” acquired during the
year, which possibly ought to be added to the deficit
above ; but it can pass—it is only a “ small one,” anyway.)
Depreciation is being provided for at the rate of 2f per
cent. This is really the fund out of which replacements
must be made ; and though it is all right at present, it
will nob be any too much when the renewals start to
come in. There is always the danger, when a community
sees that a few thousands of profit are being made by a
new undertaking, that concessions will be demanded;
and in view of the fact that hardly anything is going out
yet to pay for repairs and maintenance, the profits in
this case are by no means large enough to stand any
cutting into.
The sinking fund is not a very vigorous one—
-1 per cent.—but any sort of a sinking fund at all is a
blessed relief from the common drift of Maoriland public
finance. The money is invested partly in bank deposits,
partly in the city’s own debentures ; and as to the latter
there are some misgivings. The object of the sinking
fund is to store up enough money to presently pay off
Oohen, so that the city will really own the tramways, and
will be able to instal low fares and divert profits to city
improvement generally ; but if the money is invested in
the city’s own debentures, the chances are that when the
time comes for the city to pay over the money, it will
have to borrow to do it; and Wellington in that case
won’t be much “ forrarder.”

In 1897 this society took over the Mutual of Vic-
toria, and since then it has doubled its income,
with a little bit left over for the past year.
A great deal of the new business has come from
S’Africa, where, down to a couple of years ago at
any rate, new business was easily got, and in big amounts.
Partly for that reason it was also got at comparatively
low cost. The real value of that business has yet to b,e
proved. When a place is booming, and people look at
things through a wine-glass, the tendency is to scorn the
modest insurance policy, and talk in thousands instead of
hundreds. Later on, where the policy for hundreds could
be kept going, the policy for thousands is abandoned.
Up to now', it is true, the National Mutual’s recent record
in the way of lapses and surrenders has apparently not
been at all bad ; but something more definite may be said
about that after the issue of the results of the triennial
investigation, now just about completed. For this isn’t
one of the offices that follow the excellent plan of the
A.M.P., and give ,every year particulars, not only of the
new policies, but also of the policies discontinued through
death, maturity, surrender, and forfeiture.
The growth of the business is certainly very
striking, and reflects the energy and enterprise of the
office. For the past year 11,909 new policies were issued,
assuring £3,080,128 ; for 1906 the great A.M.P. issued
17,122 policies, assuring- £4,585,436. Going back to 1897 it
w'ill be found that the A.M.P. issued 11,625 policies for
£3,085,388, while the National Mutual issued 4523 policies
for £1,108,430. Thus the A.M.P. issueds497 more policies
than it did 10 years ago, while the National Mutual issued
7386 more. But, as mentioned just now, the value of
much of this new business in the National Mutual has yet
to be proved. The A.M.P. kept hitherto to a w'ell-tried
field ; the other broke new and uncertain ground.
The expense rate is still high, especially for an
office of this size:—
Expenses Compared with Total Income.

1899.. 18 4> 5 „ 1904.. 18 1 5 „

But that test is not enough. It is necessary also
to know the proportion of new business that has been
got for the money. The renewal premiums (old business)
amounted to £§32,740. Assume that this costs 7 per cent,
to work = £37,292. Add 3 per cent, for single-premium
and annuity business = £1442. Total, £38,734. The ex-
penses amounted altogether to £157,512. The difference
between these two—£llB,77B—will be the cost of the new
business. The new-business premiums amounted to
£104,230, so that this new business cost 113’9 per cent, of
the first year’s premiums. On the same basis the
A.M.P.’s new business last year cost 84T per cent. The
Citizens’ and one or two other offices have got well down
towards the same figure, helped mainly by the Industrial
business. The National Mutual runs no Industrial branch,
but it appears to do a considerable amount of halfway-
house business. The medical expenses point to that. In
the A.M.P. “medical fees and expenses” absorbed 15-5
per cent, of the new-business premiums last year ; and in
the Widows’ Fund they called for 19 - 9 per cent. In the
National Mutual they ran to only 7 - 3 per cent. It looks
highly probable that this means a good deal of without-
medical-inspection business ; and the chances are that
what is saved in expenses will presently be absorbed in
larger claims. The a’lleged saving must, anyhow, be kept
in mind when comparing expense rates.
Interest earnings are moderate :

This office, however, adopts the practice of deducting
rates and taxes from interest earnings. Thus the interest
earnings are reduced, but the expense rate is reduced
also. The least satisfactory feature in connection with
the assets is the poor holding of gilt-edged securities ;
7-5 per cent, of all assets represents public (including
municipal) securities. On the other hand, no less than
49-8 per cent, of the assets are in mortgages. Consider-
ing the risk that this disposition of the assets involves,
the interest earnings are distinctly moderate. Fortu-
nately the society has something substantial in the way
of an investment fluctuation reserve; it amounts to
£84,414, £1993 having been written off it during the year.
In computing earnings this reserve is, of course, included
with the Bunds. Among the assets the objectionable
“ Deferred instalments of annual premiums ” still appears-
—an unreliable paper item which now stands for £93,001.
The Nation al Mutual persists in including this as an asset,
though every other Australian first or second or even
third-rank office has abandoned it.

The Maddens /all have more than a streak of humor; and
Walter, of th at ilk, being managing-director, of thisf'co.,
turns to account even such unpromising material as
a balance-sheet. It is quite a big balance-sheet; but
it mostly consists of entries like these: “ Debts
due on judgment, nil" ; “ debts due on simple contracts,
nil"; “moneys at without security, ml.” Then, after
noting this nice regard for infinite detail, you turn tc
find out the things you want to know—the things that
matter—and you don’t find them. The co. got into the
habit of paying an 8 per cent, div., and an 8 per cent. div.
required £2400 ; so, year by year, the profit and loss
account showed £2402, £2403, £2406, £2407, £2408. The
profit and loss account states the gross profit, “after pro-
viding for contingencies ” —and that is where the
humorous managing director chuckles ; for everything
depends upon what provision is made for contingencies,
and the accounts don’t breathe a word of what the
accumulated provision for contingencies amounts to. No
doubt trustee cos.’ accounts are awkward things to
handle. There are commissions on the corpus of the
estate and commissions on the revenue, and some of the
former ought to be reserved to help pay the expenses of
management. But it is pretty obvious that this co.
carries the matter to such a length that, for all practical
purposes, its published accounts are almost valueless.
In 1898 salaries and other expenses were £3892 ; last year
they were £6293. That ought to mean much bigger
business and larger profits ; yet, as a matter of fact, the
disclosed profits have only moved up from £2390 to £2714.

The co. has been gradually getting out of city property,
and evidently acquiring country property for subdivision ;
and these subdivisional sales are no doubt mostly on
terms. The latter transactions are covered by “ advances,
etc.” It is generally supposed that great profits have been
made out of this subdivision business during the last few
years, not so much by this co. as by private specu-
lators. No doubt in a good many cases profits have been
counted before they were properly hatched ; for it is one
thing to sell land at high prices on terms and another to
get paid for it. Against that contingency this co. may
have made provision which isn’t shown ; if it hasn’t, the
profits have been very small, and what are shown as
profits may not be profits in the end. Anyway, the fact
that £lO,OOO has been written off estates isn’t too com-
forting. It is a speculative business, the.prospects of which
can’t be summed up with any certainty from the pub-
lished accounts.

* * * *
Every third man or thereabouts nowadays is
waiting till Broken Hill Souths touch 405., and then he is
going to buy. Another third is waiting to accommodate
him, and meantime is encouraging him not to wait for
405., but to snap them up at say 455. The one-third that
are bears masquerading as bulls remember that, no later
than the year 1904, paid Souths sold at 235, and con-
tributing Souths at 15s. 9d. “Ah !” says the optimist;
“ but look at the enormous ore developments in the last
couple of years.” Quite true, there have been develop-
ments such as no other Barrier mine has had. But mere
size of ore body isn’t of much consequence in a
low metal Blanket. In the first half of 1903 the
net profit was £21,117, and it was then clear
enough that the ore bodies below 800 ft. very large.
Above the 800 ft., the report said at that date, the reserves
were 1,100,000 tons, while below' the 800 ft. “ the possi-
bilities are very great, as the extent of the ore bodies is
proving' greater at the lower levels than in those above.
So there w’as no question about ore reserves even at that
date. Yet Souths (paid) went down to 19s. 6d., and the
others to 11s. In 1902,' things w’ere even worse ; m the
latter part of that year profits got dowm below half-a-
crown a ton. And they wouldn’t have been a farthing
more if the ore reserves had been 20,000,000 tons, unless
ore could have been picked out of a higher grade. Ore
reserves ought to be a most important factor in settling
prices of shares ; but the man who depends upon them as
the most important factor will get left. There was Block
14 tor instance ; prices soared up lately in spite of the
fact that the mine had wretched little reserves. About
all the public considered was that the mine was at the
moment making a big profit. And the chances are that
the public will pay just as little attention to the South s
big reserves during a slump as it lately did to Block 14’s
miserable reserves during a boom.
$ * * *
For 18 months, while silver-lead shares were
gradually creeping up to heights that turned old heads
dizzv The Bulletin made itself, and a lot of other people,
tired by persistently wanting to know what all the fuss
was about. At the start, the boom was worked.on the
zinc racket. When it was clear that nothing new had
been discovered in connection with zinc treatment, the
“ improved statistical position of lead ” w'as trotted out
by papers that ought to have known enough about metal
brokers’ “ lead statistics ”to scoff at them. The history
of the lead market was the one guide worth anything,
and the man who reckoned that £2l, £lB, or even £l5 for
lead was a permanent thing, could not have known what
that history was. Only twice between 1881 and 1905 did
the year’s average reach £l5. Ten times it was under £ll.
For two years, the papers, with very few exceptions,
allowed themselves to be made use of by the bulls ; now
they as meeklv allow themselves to be used for bearing
purposes. They are very likely right, though, in sug-
gesting that most shares will go . lower. There is not
much hope of a quick change in the American position,
and until that happens neither metal nor stock markets
are likely to get any better. People who sell out silver-
lead shares now, therefore, have a very good chance of
being able to get in later on at a lower price. But while
these shares may go lower, nothing is much more certain
than that, sooner or later, they will again go higher.
The lead market swings to and fro. It was down to £lo£
in 1885 and up to £ls£ in 1888 ; back to £9 in 1894 and up
to £l7i in 1899 ; back to £lo£ in 1902 and up to £2l in 1907.
And shares rock backwards and forwards with the metals.
Nothing much is to be gained in most cases by telling the
man who was fool enough to buy in at the top, how low
his stocks have been ; having made a fool of himself, his
best course now is to hang on if he can, and wait lor the
recovery that is pretty certain to come. If he could be
trusted not to make a mess of things again, the strictly

Two patent portable traction scoop engines
are being built to the order of Messrs. McLelland and
Cooper for cutting the main irrigation channels in the
'yaranga Basin District of Victoria. The engines will be
placed on opposite sides of the channels and will pull and
tip the scoops in their own direction, and so make the
channel and form the embankment. There are two
engines of the vertical compound type, and, along with
winding drums, friction clutches, worm gearing, and
other appliances, are mounted on a mild steel framework
carriage which projects above and forward over a portion
of the locomotive boiler. The boiler is mounted on
driving and steering wheels. The main haulage engine
is 160 I.H.P. and the smaller one 35 I.H.P. The Austral
Otis Engineering Company Ltd., South Melbourne, are
the manufacturers.* [d.]

* * * *
The people who are going to be wise this time
and buy silver and copper stocks when the markets are
low, and put them away and become rich and bloated
when next the market booms, have a thing or two to
remember. It isn’t every mine or mining co. that can
last through a slump. Most of them, struggling to keep
going as long as they can, peck at the good ore, and then
in the end find themselves up against faces of poor stuff,
and compelled to spend large amounts (which they
haven’t got) in order to get to new ore bodies. In most
cases that means reconstruction. Oos. that run against
that snag have to buy money, and the old shareholders
have to make the sacrifice. The co. that faces a slump
with little or nothing in the till is a co. to be avoided.
The slump in zinc (spelter) doesn’t get much
mention, but there has been one, nevertheless. Present
price is about £2l ;12 months ago it was £27 15s. At
£23 per ton the B. H. Prop, was lately making 225. per
ton of zinc concentrates, equal to about 4s. 9d. per ton of
tailings (116,240 tons of tailings last half-year gave 25,353
tons concentrates). The concentrates give about 45 per
cent, zinc ; so, at £23 per ton for zinc, there was a profit
at the rate of about 48s. 6d. per ton of zinc. With zinc
down 40s. below £23, it is obvious that the profit must
be only the odd shillings, unless richer tailings can be
treated or working costs reduced.
The premature closihg-down of the Australian
Smelting Co.’s works at Port Kembla suggests one or two
things concerning the failure of so many Australian cus-
toms smelting works, from Maryborough (Queensland)
round to Premantle (W.A.). The failure of these, or
many of them, has not been due to want of enterprise so
much as to excessof enterprise, plus the usual bit of
roguery. Aldershot (Queensland) began in a land job
pure and simple, and, except for one or two little bursts
when Australian brains looked like pulling the English
people out of the mud, the works near Maryborough have
never presented symptoms of sound industry. Works in
the wrong place, no big mines behind them, and bad
English control have got Aldershot so deep into the
mud that it will probably never get out. It would be
madness to spend more money on the spot.
The Port Kembla works appear in more
respects than one to be a reincarnation of
the Dapto scheme; and, although it is hinted that
these works will presently re-open and astonish the
natives, it is very doubtful whether they can be carried
to success on the present lines. The Sulphide Corpora-
tion all along has had the Central mine, Broken Hill,
behind it, and that mine has been big and rich enough to
pay for many mistakes and miscalculations. All the
same it is a good thing for Australia that Cockle Creek
has kept going: the Commonwealth could ill afford
to see the last of its lead customs works give
way to the German metal buyers. No doubt Aus-
tralia has got excellent prices from those German buyers.
The fact is, they must have the lead concentrates for
their European works, so in effect a premium has been
paid for the Broken Hill stuff. This premium is not
always available, but for about two years in five it pre-
vails, and then returning charges come down, and the
Barrier cos. smile with dividends. It is during those two
years that the Australian lead customs works are
smothered under the fierce foreign competition. In some
cases, instead of shutting down, they have rushed in and
bought supplies at the enhanced figure—and collapsed
in consequence.
Concerning the Fremantle smelter, lead sup-
plies (when gold material was, plentiful) and gold supplies
(when lead was available) appear to have been its double-
barrelled difficulty. To send gold ores to the smelter is a
costly game, and, anyway, there are no big available
deposits of refractory auriferous material in Australia ;
certainly not enough to keep a smelter busj r . Concen-
trates carrying the bulk of the gold not amenable to
ordinary amalgamation or cyanide are all right for the
smelter ; but, then, gold concentrates at present in Aus-
tralia do not mean big tonnage, so a gold smelter cannot
hope to keep its head up. Aldershot (Maryborough, Q.)
started out to be a gold smelter, and it stopped. It had
no lead supplies, and in the end finished without gold
supplies, either in its bins or its cash box. Failure of the
concentrating plant at the Pinnacles will not cheer the
London people who are landed with Aldershot stock.
Fremantle also started out as a gold smelter with results
already known.
Hitherto lead and refractory gold ores have
found their way to Australian smelters ; but the lead sup-
plies are now almost all collared by Europe, and the only
thing open to Australian smelters is to use copper as a
vehicle for collecting the gold and silver. The strong
point here is that Australia has now assured to it an
electrolytic copper refihery, where the precious metals
can be separated and the collector (the copper) refined ;
and in future we may look for the development of re-
fractory gold bodies in conjunction with copper, and
copper smelting. For some time past, Australia has been
beaten out of the game so far as lead-smelting is con-
cerned, but in copper it is doubtful if we can ever be
beaten. In vieV of this, it is up to N.S. W. to place every
facility in the way of the Mt. Morgan people in their
efforts to get the electrolytic refinery established at Port
Kembla as soon as possible. That refinery will have to
compete strongly against the rest of the world, and Aus-
tralia cannot afford to see its material passed on to the
American and German to finish for us. They have the
volume of trade now, and Australia has sufficient volume
available if it has the sense to take it. Volume is all that
is wanted.
* * * •
The recent fire at Block 14, Broken Hill, has
aroused managements to the fact that there is an ever-
present menace to surface works in the steel stacks with
which all the 1 mines along the line are equipped. At one
of the biggest mines the sparks belched out from a
steel smoke stack recently ignited the poppet-legs three
times., Only the other day a blaze was put out at the
South mill. As a consequence several managers are in-
stalling pumps that will throw jets of water over their
poppet-heads.
• * * *
Some devoted optimists are still exploring the
eastern ridge of the White Leads flat, outside Broken Hill,
and, in their enthusiasm, regard a speck of galena or a
handful of rotten quartz with the veneration an anti-
quarian shows for a relic. The Silver City Blocks and
the Horseshoe Blocks cover most of the ground to the
east of the Rising Sun and other White Leads mines, and
on both these properties pot-holes and a shallow shaft or
two have been sunk. All of any value that has been got
out would g'o in the silk hat of the hoped-for English
investor.
* » * *
The proposed new Cockburn to Broken Hill
syndicate railway led to a fine little gamble in Adelaide.
At first there were 30 shares of £25 each ; then buvers
were talked up to £7O; then the syndicate watered'the
stock into 1500 shares of £5 each, and got the first read-
ing of the Bill (purely formal, of course). But the gamble
was about played out by that time, and shares began to
sag down to below par. The Bill has precious little hope,
anyhow.

An odd copy or two of Burbank’s Birthday
Co.’s report for 1906 found their way out to the West by
a recent English mail. Amongst other things, this
belated document mentions in a casual way that the
accounts will be submitted at a meeting of shareholders
in Westralia, but where, and on what date, this meeting
is to take place is not stated. Directors mildly exult over
the fact that the tributors are still working at a profit to
the co. ; and they assert that “ the late rich find in Bay-
ley’s mine—in the near vicinity—will undoubtedly give
the tributing parties further encouragement.” But as
the two properties are over six miles apart, and are situ-
ated on different belts of country, this assertion is repre-
hensible and impudent. The only similarity about
Bayley’s and Burbank’s is that values become poor at a
comparatively shallow depth, and that for company pur-
poses the doom of both is sealed. The co. has about
£9OOO in hand, and the money now derived from public
crushing and tributors’ royalties just about pulls the two
ends together.

Lake View Consols (W. A.) has decided to instal
a vacuum filter plant to re-treat a big pile of mill residues,
300,000 tons or so. A profit of between 3s. and 4s. per
ton is expected ; and as provision is being made to treat
400 tons daily, the co.’s receipts should presently spring
to the extent of about £2OOO a month.
* * * *
Speaking at a recent mining-men’s dinner, at
Kalg'oorlie, Haig, of the Golden Link, cheerfully pre-
dieted that 20 years hence “ six or seven of the Golden
Mile mines would still be producing-.” Yes, but how
much ? The Boulder, Ivanhoe, and Horseshoe, which
contain ore that is less refractory and more persistent
than the neighboring properties, may be earning good
profits twenty years hence ; but before ten years hence
some of the others will have a struggle to keep going.
Mines whose ore has been ripped out at the Kalgoorlie
rate can’t have the life of the leisurely-worked Bendigo
mines, for instance.
* * * *
The Ore developed at the Great Fingall (W.A.)
during the past year was the lowest-grade yet exposed in
quantity ; so the yarn, lately circulated, that the mine
was “looking better than ever,” may be put aside and
forgotten.
* * * *
It looks as if the Cosmopolitan Proprietary
(W.A.) were about to throw seven. Recent developments at
depth have been so disappointing that even 8., M. and Co.,
who boss the show, have lost heart. Meantime, the mill
is fast eating up the reserves, and the exhaustion of these
in a few months will probably marie the end of profitable
mining in this property, except, perhaps, for tributing
parties.
* * * *
There seems to be something wrong about the
valuation of the ore in the Gwalia Consolidated (W.A.).
For a long time past boom assays have been regularly
cabled to London, yet the returns from the mill only
average about £1 per ton. Either the plant is a rank
failure as a g'old saver or the estimates of ore averages are
unwarranted ; and, anyhow, it is the gold that is saved
that counts, not the gold that is estimated.
* ♦ * •
The Lord Bobs Syndicate, Coolgardie (W.A.),
is still in clover. The last retui-p ran out at 4190 z. from
273 tons crushed, and a dividend of £BO per share has
been declared. This makes a total distribution of £865 per
share since the property was re-pegged in the middle of
1906. Some years ago the Lord Bobs bloomed for a time,
but the reef vanished from sight after several good
crushings had been obtained, and the lease was thrown
up. The present owners then came along, and have done
well ever since.
Windanya Consols (W.A.) was sold up the
other day, when the plant went for a song, and the pro-
perty found a buyer at next to nothing-. It is not so long
since there was an “ official opening ” at this mine, and as
late as a few weeks ago the local papers printed the usual
whoops. A “local’’paper may at times tell the truth
about a bad man, but never about a bad mine.
* * * *
Another “find” has been made in the nor’-
west of Westralia—wolfram this time, worth £l2O per
ton, and there are also tin, copper, silver-lead, etc., in the
same locality. Unfortunately, this part of the West has
fallen into the hands of boomsters, who have smudged
what little reputation it ever had.
* * * *
Mt. Remarkable (W.A.) is being given another
trial. Some years back this district was alleged to have
a future ; but as one co. which tried its luck found that
payable values snuffed out at a shallow depth, the field
was abandoned in a hurry.
* * * *
Those two London promoting and gambling
concerns, the Australian Commonwealth Trust and the
Consolidated Deep Leads present figures which show how
completely and utterly “busted” they are. Their pur-
pose was to plant Victorian deep leads high in J. Bull’s
mining firmament. Judging by the holdings, the directors
did one of three things—(l) Believed in the deep leads,
and loaded up to the Plimsoll mark with shares ; (2) tried
to force the market, and got “ left ” with a swag of scrip
by profit-takers; or (3) failed to unload. , According to
circulars now issued dealing with the proposed recon-
structions, the Commonwealth Trust holds 79,465 Con-
solidated Deep Leads shares (45.), 59,412 Berry Deep Leads
(£1), 70,879 Victorian Deep Leads (some £1 paid, the
others 155.), 46,380 Great Berry Consolidated, and others.
The Consolidated Deep Leads concern holds 63,863 Lod-
don Valley Goldfields (£1), over 90,000 Moolorts, 40,200
Commonwealth Trust shares, and others. So each
is a heavy holder of the other’s shares, which
is a case of the very drunk trying to prop
up the immoderately unsober. This looks as if
the public didn’t come in too well even at the start. A
lot of cash has been forthcoming, but it would be a dis-
tressing problem to try to figure out how it was got.
London financial press, with a crusade of lying, made
some sort of a market till Loddon Valleys shot the final
bolt. Since then it has been poverty and reconstruction,
first by the mining cos., then by the promoting cos. Now
the two promoting cos. are to be fused into one, with a
capital of £68,600.

From Manager Whitfeld : Touching par (B.
21/11/’O7) on Oroya-Black Range and Sandstone Develop-
ment Co., the “Man on the Spot” may be “uninter-
ested ” (I might enlighten you on that point if I knew
who he was), but he certainly has not inspected both
properties, and is grossly ignorant of their relative work-
ings and positions. He says “ the shaft where the strike
was made (i.e. Juno ‘C’shaft) is several hundred feet
north of the Oroya-Black Range workings.” Doubly
wrong. The 0.8. R. No. 1 level (110 ft. vertical depth) has
now been driven oh pay ore over 1200 ft. north (of No. 1
shaft), and they are stoping about 1000 ft. north, near
Leach’s old workings, which yielded some of the richest
of the prospectors’ crushings. The Juno “C” shaft
(S. D. Co.) is 1000 ft. north (from same datum), and
therefore, approximately due west of some of the richest
workings on the line of reef. As to the alleged
southerly dip of the shoots, this is based on the
assertion of the same' or some other “uninter-
ested” individual (B. 20/6/’O7). After years’ inti-
mate acquaintance with the Sandstone reef, theS.D. Co.’s
management reckons that the gold is dipping due west,
i.e., with the reef, and in view of the recent strike of rich
ore in Juno “ O ” shaft aforesaid, this contention should
take a lot of disproving. Anyhow, as the reef itself is
dipping west, and theS.D. Co. extends along the whole
western boundary of the 0.8. R., it is difficult to see how
“ values will pass once more into the Black Range
ground.” The S.D. Co. is cheerfully prepared to prove its
assertions, and only asks that its critics should have some
first-hand knowledge of the property. It cordially in-
vites any mining engineer to come along and get that
knowledge.
« * « *
Ballarat is doing some work now that it is down
on its mining marrow-bones. Thus 6018 tons from the New
South Imperial gave a profit, though it was only 3dwt.
stuff. Mining was done from 430 ft., so that the work
was a fair test. New Imperial, next door, did better,
for, by putting through 10,800 tons, worth 15s. (nearly
4dwt.), it paid one div., and worked up a slight cash
balance. Both these cos. would have done well if they
could have realised that the one way in which to make
low-grade stone pay, is to treat it in bulk and with heavy
stamps. That means opening up the mine ahead, and,
consequent calls; but, under that system, the ratio of
expenses is subsequently so reduced that the calls soon
come back. This is. what Ballarat must do if it is to
revive.
* * * *
The tin-boom was worth not far short of
£70,000 this yearlto' the Briseis (Tas.). In 1904, the last
year when quotations were fairly normal, the output was
worth £39,529 gross. ‘ This year, to the end of September,
the profit was £95,000. Even between August and
November, when the market was well on the way down-
hill, the average price realised for metallic tin was £165,
according to a circular issued in London. On the day
the circular reached Melbourne the quotation was £ll9,
or £46 less. Lumping the output from Briseis and
Brothers No. 1, the output from January to the end of
September was 1069 tons ; and an average of £6O was
netted over and above what may be fairly considered the
normal price. No doubt it is hard to say what is a nor-
mal price for tin ; but, anyhow, prior to 1905 the highest
year’s average'for quarter of a century was £133 11s. 6d.,
and in 16 years out of the 25 the average had been under
£lOO. In 1896, the average was £59 9s. lid.— a price low
enough to make everybody with any interest in tin
shiver. Certainly there was a .steady improvement after
1896, and conditions are still favorable for good prices-
that is to say, .something over £loo. But it is a tricky
market at the best of times.
* - - * * f - *
New Ruby Flat is one of the few Tasmanian
tin shows that have stood the test of time ; but the
directors do not show up too well. They hugged to their
bosom about 13 tons of the total output of 581 tons of
tin, and have had the ,popr satisfaction of seeing metal
values fade while they were still holding on. Therefore
the cash return was 0n1y'£4939, whereas it should have
been £l2OO more. The co. hi the half-year treated 85 487
cubic yards, averaging tin ; and as costs are low it
pays dividends when other mines starve.
Gladstone (Tas.), which came along with the
small comfort of a 2s. div. in the past half-year, shows
nothing- in its accounts to be at all joyful about. The
div. only totalled £250, yet, in order to pay it, the profit
and loss balance was reduced from £3130 to £2983 during
the half-year. The profit was thus only £lO3 ; and even
then, though of course maintenance has been paid for,
nothing has been written off for the heavy wear and tear.
And this, too, in a booming tin market. The profit
amounted to less than £4 per ton of black tin, and the
fall in price has been nearer £4O. It is said that there is
better ground ahead ; but it will need to be so much
better that it really was a shame to revive hope ever so
little by means of the recent dividend.
* * * *
“M.C.” : Mount Lyell comes out of this de-
pression better than most. The November yield was
680 tons fine copper, 71,3470 z. silver, and 15320 z. gold.
Taking copper at £5O per ton, silver at 25., and gold at £4
per oz., the month’s production is worth £47,262, or,
roughly, 30s. a ton of ore ; and a co. that can do its
mining, smelting, and converting for about 15s. 6'd. a
ton, and yet run out nearly 30s. worth of metals per ton
of ore, has a decided pull over most other copper shows.
But the foreigner takes more than is generally
supposed for freight, refining and commission, and the
directors would clear up a mystery if they disclosed the
over-sea charges that have to be deducted from the in-
trinsic value of blister copper.

“Platman ” : A par a few weeks ago mentioned
the “ guileless ” working men of Irvinebank. Yes ; jesso.
During the past few months the following cats have curled
their tails for ever : —Dead Finish, which on an expendi-
ture of over £2OOO produced £l2OO worth of tin ; Rose of
Tralee, floated in £l5 shares, and in debt the first half-
year ; Dorothy, an alluvial proposition, floated about a
year ago, now in process of reconstruction ; Woolaman
Creek, twin sister of the Dorothy, floated for £3600, pro-
duced £505 worth of tin, whereupon it was reported as
worked out.” There is still a wriggle in the Woolaman
tail, as they have a lease on the basalt. A tunnel has
been driven across the bed of an old creek a distance of
172 ft. ; it produced 60 tons of wash, worth on samples
71b. to the lode. The tunnel is Oft. x 4ft., so that
roughly 1537 yd. of dirt were shifted for a return of 4201 b.
of tin. Truly a guileless crowd whom outsiders should
think it a shame to take down.
* * * *
Queensland gold returns show no sign of atoning
for the fall in the value of the base-metal output. For
November the gold yield was down to £154,201, which is
only about 75 per cent, of the output for November, 1906,
and that was talked of at the time as rock-bottom.
Dividends now barely counterbalance calls, leaving Mt.
Morgan out of the reckoning.
* * * *
November output from Mills United, Charters
Towers, was the largest since the opening of the eastern
shoot of stone. It included £12,619 from the plates, and
totalled £28,217. About £20,000 of this was profit.
This year, £75,000 has been paid in dividends, and there
is a balance of over £40,000 to start 1908 with. Though
the shoot is being depleted at the rate of 50,000 tons a
year, there is a good reserve of stone, but only the
stampers can guarantee its quality, and they don’t give
any guarantees in advance. The deepest level (No. 13) is
out 415 ft. from the main underlay shaft, and has 3ft. of
no better than light mineral stone in the face ; still, levels
have previously been extended a long distance before
meeting the rich eastern shoot.
* * * *
Ruby P.C., Charters Towers, pays a sixpenny
div. : 395 tons were crushed for 8290 z., which is an im-
provement on the previous lot of 370 tons for 6400 z.,
worth £3 15s. 6d. per oz. The underlay has been sunk
from No. 5, and a new level (No. 6) has been driven about
30ft. east and west of the shaft; prospects so far are from
9in. to Ift. of good stone. The Ruby is some distance
outside of the‘’classical ” neighborhood favored by the
Brilliant and Day Dawn reefs. It has a narrow, patchy
reef, expensive to work, and that sort has to be ab-
normally rich to catch the popular fancy.
Pfeiffer’s Day Dawn, adjoining Mills United
on the west, has crushed 700 tons for £3OlB. It should
he able to pay a sixpenny dividend (£5000). The output
is now up to between 600 and 700 tons a month. This is
one of the shows that may give the Charters Towers
mining market the badly-needed lift.
* * * *
Carrington United, the outside-Charters Towers
gold-copper mine, has now got two furnaces at work, a
third is ready, a start has been made on a fourth, and a
fifth has been decided upon. A recent development
occurred at a depth of 365 ft., just away from No. 2 shaft,
a good copper lode, about sft. wide, being struck. This
is a big body, as lodes go in the Carrington.
* * * »
The break-up of the tin gamble, hurried along
by the depression in America, has pulled shares of over-
capitalised tin mines down with a run. Stannary Hills
(Q.) let its chance slip ; but it evidently thinks that
matters may yet grow worse, for, in spite of the set-back,
it proceeds to issue more 6 per cent, stock to pay off a
loan carrying high interest. Meantime, a large number
of hammer and drill men have been paid off, and the
plant is to get overhauled. The present manager has
shown the way in the matter of development work, but
it has not resulted in any very important discovery.
Some profit is made out of the railway, now that it is
connected with Irvinebank ; but this railway depends
upon mines which won’t be able to do much work if the
markets go much lower.
* * * «
Wolfram is practically unsaleable at present.
This is partly due to the fact that during the recent boom
tons of inferior rubbish, such as specular iron and iron
pyrites, were rung in bv the vendors to the buyers. An
identical result followed the boom of 1904, when the
same practice was followed.
* * * *
About six or eight months ago the S. Australian
Mines Department advertised for an inspector of mines,
at the princely screw of £4 per week—but he had to be
an expert, of course. Up to then Captain Matthews had
been the . solitary inspector of mines and chief
warden as well. During the first few months of
the new man’s appointment he had a hustling
time among the swarms of old and new copper
shows up north, so he didn’t have time to inspect any of
the old shows about Callington, in the south-eastern
hills. Yet that district looks as though it would be all
the better for a visit. Lately, a chain was being lowered,
hung on to the rope, at one mine. It caught in the
shaft, and, when freed, fell about 200 ft., and killed a
man. The jury said “Accidental death ” ; but, from all
appearances, no inspector of mines visited the scene of
the accident, and there was no one there to question
whether that was a safe way to lower a chain. S. Aus-
tralia is a big place, and its mines are scattered and
mostly poor, wherefore methods are often bad, and yet
inspection amounts to practically nothing. On the whole,
therefore, there are plenty of safer jobs than being a
miner in S. Australia.
* * * *
The West Mutooroo (S.A.) has come to a full
stop. The mine has simply lived on calls since it was re-
vived, and shareholders have at last got tired. Also, the
Mutooroo Consols has put up the shutters, and why they
were ever down remains a mystery. Then the Mutooroo
proper is in a bad way ; after twelve months’ work with-
out any production w’orth. mentioning, the capital has
gone, and only sufficient men are now employed to hold
the leases.
* * * *
The “ National Share Exchange ” begins to
blossom into big. advts. in Melbourne. People are ex-
horted to “improve their position by the judicious
employment of their spare capital.” The “judicious
employment ” is the old “margin” or bucket-shop
system, by which, you pay a deposit on a parcel of shares.
If the shares go up, you win ; if they go down, you lose.
It is one of the most insidious forms of gambling ; and the
new Victorian Anti-Gambling Act .should be wide enough
to close down upon this folly, which ropes in pious old
ladies and others who would faint at the sight of a race-
course.

“Ossian” : Had “ Mularula” (B. 14/11/07)
been to Iluka, be would proclaim Grong-Grong
(N.S.W.) a cheery place by comparison. Iluka is
on the northern entrance of the Clarence River.
Long ago, the North Coast boats called there, but
they don’t any longer, for two breakwaters have
been constructed, completely separating the town
from the main river. To-day, one is only able to
reach the place by balloon or row-boat. The in-
habitants number about 40. There is one pub, one
store, no telegraph nor money-order office, no
police, and finally no Liquor Act. Iluka is cos-
mopolitan, derelicts from most every country
having anchored there, and they are the saddest
lot of derelicts I ever saw. When the utterly
mournful quality of its inhabitants is duly con-
sidered, Iluka must win in the Sad Town Stakes.
* * * *
“Flinderino”: Mackay, the coastal town
in Queensland, is the home of wandering cattle,
loose dogs, stray goats, and other noxious weeds.
Horses wander along the footpaths and flatten
their noses on the lolly-shop windows, cows lie
down under the verandahs and chew the cud of
contentment, and goats of all nationalities and
miscellaneous colors and odors meander about
the streets, eating the road-metal and dangling
their beards over the fences. Every house in
Mackay possesses a fierce dog, and for some unex-
plained reason, the dog is turned out every even-
ing and locked out all night. Consequently, it
is not safe for visitors to take a stroll after dark
unless accompanied by a big stick and a pocketful
of stones. Even then one trips over a sleeping
horse, or falls on a cow, or else gets tangled up
with a setting of goats. The peaceful inhabitants of
the sugar-town seem quite content with this state
of affairs, so the four-legged nuisances will con-
tinue to flourish and obstruct.
“ Mohatra ”: Be sleepiest town. I beg
to nominate Bogantungun, in Central Queensland.
It is probably the deadest aggregation of timber,
iron and sundries in the world. From a place of
sixteen pub-power it has fallen and fallen, till now
it boasts but one solitary lone old drinkery, the
which is bossed by an unashamed Dago. Indi-
cate me your pubs and I will tell you of what
sort your town is, every time. On pub-form
Bogantungun clamors for early interment.
* * * *
“ Pica ” on native names :—•
Have discovered, during a quest for native folk-lore,
etc., that Kurnell, Cook’s landing-place at Botany, is a
corrupted name. The native name is “ Kundull.” Coogee
should be “ Koojah ” (meaning “ bad smell,” the decom-
posed sea-weed being responsible for the odor). Wagga
Wagga is a corruption of “ Worgan Worgan,” and
“ worgan ” was the word for crow. The early bullockies
used to call the place “ Worga Worga.” Woolloomooloo
was aboriginally “ Wulla Mulla” (burial place), and
Wollongong was “ Wolyungah ” (denoting that fish were
to be caught by hook and line). Kiama was “ Kiari Mai”
(fertile district), and Ulladulla should be “ Wooloodorr ”
(harbor of refuge). Illawarra is derived from “ Elouera ”
(or fine country). The bay so well known to fishermen,
south of Coogee, was originally known as “ Maroobera,”
which was the name of the chief of the black clan out
that way. Parramatta means “ the place for eels ” (Parra,
“eel,” and “ Malta,” “this place”), Brewarrina also
means “ a fishery.” Writer has seen one of these Bat-
won “ fisheries ” near Walgett,—a complication of stone
“yards” built in the bed of the river, forming an intri-
cate maze from which no fish, once in, would ever escape.
Bullah Delah means “swinging rock,” Tooal, on the
Paterson, means “plenty,” having refert nee to the
number of ducks once found there. North Shore was
known as “ Caiumeray,” Pinehgnt was “ Mattinwunye,”
and Middle Head, “ Gubba Cubba.” The natives called
Sydney Cove “ Warrane.” “Memel” stood for Goat
Island, and “ Cannae ” for Manly Beach.
* # * *
“ : In the course of my experience
as manager of a rather large North Queensland
plantation, running to nearly 900 acres of cane
for the mill, I came to the fixed conclusion not
only that the Kanaka cannot understand the first
thing about machinery, but also that he is even
less to be trusted with horses. That is why, so
long as this type of labor is employed, only the
most primitive appliances are in vogue. In my
first year it cost a good deal more than the dif-
ference between white and black wages to replace
the horses the Kanakas ruined. This accounts,
too, for the prevalence of the mule in the Southern
U.S. and West Indies. No mule will allow
himself to he overdriven; the more generous
horse can be driven to death. In the same way
the Kanaka proved himself a bad ploughman
through impatience with his horses ; he could not
run a straight and even furrow. A white man
would do far more work in a day with less distress
to his team. As for machinery, a sugar mill uses
a great deal of steam apart from its engines, and
it must have several boilers ; but no Kanaka has
ever yet been made to understand and remember
the workings of a boiler gauge. The race might
possibly be educated up to the requisite sense of
responsibility in a dozen generations, but it will
probably be extinct first.
AN ABORIGINALITY.
“ Back in the big drought, my old dog fell down
a crack. There was cracks everywhere, like
as if the earth was gaspin' for breath. I
giv’ him up for lost, an ’ walked three miles
on an ’ camped. Thet night I was half asleep
when I heerd him bark ’way in the distance.
I sat up and whistled, and the bark came
closer an ’ closer, muffled like. I put my ear
to the ground. Yes, the poor unfortunate
creature had followed the cracks along after
me, and was way down underneath, barking
in the bowels of the earth."
There is a dog in Melbourne, a thorough-
bred collie, who knows his way about. He is as
well known as any fashionable Block habitue.
He is (or was) a waif ; he belongs to nobody, yet
he leads a languidly petted and fashionable life.
He does the round of the swagger tea rooms ;
haunts the Vienna and the Paris cafe for a week
or two, then roams off to another food haunt. If
one place more than another can be called his
home, it is the Mia Mia, with its daily garniture
of blue-gum leaves (he is quite an Australian,
despite his Scottish ancestry): he is great pals
with all the maidens there. If any of them
encounter him casually in his languid saunter
down Collins-street, they always receive a gracious
wag of the tail. He is a courteous gentleman.

“ Two Seas ” : The latest fashion in rugs
appears to be dyed rabbit skins, with a tiny baby
bear skin let into the centre, and I wonder what
manner of brute it is who provides the little bears.
In my opinion, he’s a thing to be avoided by all
men with a spark of compassion in their natures.
I’m no city-bred sentimentalist, but one who has
roamed through the bush with a gun ever since he
could carry one; but I could never harden my
heart sufficiently to take pot shots at a native
bear—a creature that looks down confidingly
at the man with a gun without offering to
move an inch out of range. I once came
across a belated bear sitting on the fence by
the roadside in the early morn. Knowing
what would happen to it when the boys
came along to school (there was a heap of
road-metal handy) I tried to shoo it away,
but it wasn’t the least bit afraid of my panto-
mime. Then I took a light fence rail and
tried to prise it off its perch ; but as soon as
I had got its hinder end adrift after some
heavy straining, it tightened its grip for’ard,
and before I could get my lever to work
there it was all fast astern again. I got it
upside down, endways and all ways ; but,
in any position, there was still some part
of it fast to the rail. The brute never
lost its temper—it left that to me—but
its expression was one of intense sur-
prise at my energy. Finally, when
I had almost decided to leave it to
its fate, 1 was struck with the bright
idea of taking the rail it was on out of
the fence. So I carried it on the rail to
the tallest tree in the paddock, and set
it down against the same. It seemed
to recognise that, and crawled slowly up
the tree, and satin the first fork, where it
remained looking down at me with an air
of placid security. I thought then, and still
think,that the “sportsman” who would put
shot after shot into such a harmless creature,
until it falls to the ground sobbing like a child,
would shoot a baby in arms for its skin if it
were not for fear of the law.
* * * *
“ A. Zelwood ” : An Abo. writer says the
cocoon of the fin. green caterpillar (cup-moth
larva) is made of bark. But he is altogether at
sea, as the material is only the web and sticky
exudation from the caterpillar itself. The process
can easily be observed at this time of the year by
keeping a few caterpillars and feeding them on
gum leaves. The cocoon is made in one night.
The moths emerge during the following autumn.
« * * *
“J.G.”: Here, in some of the rich dis-
tricts round about Bendigo (Vic.), it is the usual
thing to see farm-houses in -all their nakedness
stuck in some corner of the crop paddocks, or
else out in the middle of the grass paddocks.
And not even a tree or shrub is planted round the
humpies to shelter them from sun and wind.
Even if the houses were originally built in a
timber patch, the cookies never rested till they
chopped down or killed every blessed tree about
the place. If anyone were to remonstrate with
them for this practice, the cookies would tell you
that trees take up too much room. As for grow-
ing fruit or vegetables, the cocky would think you
a madman to suggest such a thing, ’cause he has
no time, and can buy them off the Chinamen. If
any white man started a garden, the average
cocky wouldn’t purchase his fruit or vegetables,
but would sooner buy inferior stuff from the
Qhow—stuff that had been rejected from the
Bendigo and Melbourne markets. I have seen
European gardeners with first-class greengrocery
not sell a penn’orth at some farms, while next
half-hour a Chow came along and got rid of
about a pound’s-worth of doubtful-looking verdure.
These anti-Australian cockies follow the Argus,
and vote Anti-Sosh at election times, and are
advocates of cheap black and yellow labor.
“ Camp-oven ” : Be bush - hospitality
(“ F.H.R.,” B. 31/10/’O7). You cannot safely
measure the warmth of a traveller’s reception
by the number of horses he drives. “F.H.R.”
states that a man with four horses can dine with
the bank manager. Now, nearly every team of
four found in the bush tows the van of the
Assyrian hawker, at the sight of whom every cur
on the selection is loosed. Also “F.H.R.” can
hardly base his assertion, that hospitality was
buried in the last drought, on the fact that his
shearer friend was inhospitably treated. Who has
not heard of the epicurean tastes of the average
shearer ? He is more particular about his grub
than the big-gun squatter. As the cocky does not
believe in ham and eggs and cocoa, we can hardly
blame him for refusing to insult Bill Shears by
offering him corn-beef and tea with the cow-juice
missing.
“Pine Scrub”: To “Brad’s” par (B.
7/11/’O7) re “ triautelopes,” I simply remark,
Rodents ! (1) The “ tri. ” does not spin a web. He
is a frequenter of dark corners and dubious by-
ways (to wit, cracks between wall-plates and
skirting-boards) and relies for his crust on his
Squires-like rush, aided by the strategy of a
Jack O’Brien. (2) He does not appear except at
night. Now, the bee, contrariwise, is always in
camp before dark. If, by chance, a bee had hap-
pened on “Brad’s” mansion at an hour when
“ Mr. Tri.” would have been about, former would,
probably, have been rolling hiveward from the
pub and in no condition to put up a scrap.
Summed up, “Brad’s” par is a marvel of in-
accuracy, the disastrous result of an overworked
imagination.
“ Mundala ” : What a multitude of
loony citizens there are out back ! I was
escorting Matilda from “the Corner ” to Wil-
cannia, when I met a traveller with hair
all over his head and face, and no hat.
Said he never wore one, because the roof of the
sky was gradually sinking, and he couldn’t watch
it properly from under a hat. Changed my mind
about camping near him, and went on another
couple of miles. Just as I was thinking of a halt,
saw another traveller camped under a tree.
Hadn’t exchanged more than half-a-dozen words
with him when he said : “ Are you a parson or a
priest?” I said, “No. Why?” “Oh, it’s all
right then,” he answered. “I should have to kill
you if you were, you know.” Feeling that, with
my naturally holy expression and saintly cast of
countenance, my appearance was against me, I
went a bit further and camped on my own. Had
tucker, and was lying in the shade, smoking,
when I saw a third traveller approaching from
the direction I had come. I assumed he was
another lunatic, and thought he might like to
meet a fellow crank ; so when he came up. threw
down his swag, and said, “Good day, mate !” I
said, “No; it’s about five miles along the track,
but don’t you touch it! Don’t you touch it! ”
“ Strike me dead ! ” he yelled, picking up his
swag, “you’re the third mad swine I’ve struck in
three mile !”
RETIRED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At Bittern and Balnarring (Vic.) are the farms of many
old sea-dogs, whose ambition has been, like that of
Outcliffe Hyne’s Captain Kettle, to settle down to a
bucolic existence after the stress of a seafaring life.
From these pleasant uplands they can still feel them-
st'lves in touch with their old consort the sea ; for to east
and west lie the broad waters of Westernport and Port
Phillip., and southward the blue line of Bass Strait.
'The strenuous days are over,
He treads the deck no more ;
The weatherbeaten rover
Has settled down ashore.
From palm and mangrove beaches,
Prom atolls white with foam,
From lonely ocean reaches,
The skipper journeys home.
The seas have lost their glamor
For one that tramped the sea
In many an old wind-jammer
From ’Frisco to Fiji;
His sunburned visage hardens
At talk of alien lands ;
He dreams of kitchen gardens,
And crops, and fallowed lauds.
He leans to oats and clover,
To sheds, and barns, and sties }
The old sea-weary rover
In eattle-lore grows wise.
You’ll see him with his harrows,
Or clinking round his ploughs,
<Or setting traps for sparrows,
Or bedding down his cows.
No more the sea’s fierce banging
Breaks through his troubled sleep,
Nor his old tub goes clanging
Up waters green and steep.
No more his inner vision
Sees pictures in the night—
The iceberg, the collision,
The breakers roaring white.
Yet, though he’s ceased to wander,
At every day’s decline
Fondly his eyes turn yonder
To that familiar line ;
To watch far steamers trailing
Their smoke in Western skies,
And old three-masters sailing,
Bound out with merchandise.
No counterfeit presentment
Of hard-earned rest has he,
But unalloyed contentment—
A farm beside the sea !
The stormy days are over,
He treads the deck no more ;
The weather-beaten rover
Has settled down ashore 1
VIC.
C. G. A. COLLES.

“ Lumeah ” : There are medicinal virtues
in the mistletoe. When bush birds take poisoned
wheat, if they can get to a tree on which
mistletoe is growing and can pick a leaf or berry,
they recover quickly. Have boiled mistletoe
leaves for bait-afflicted dogs, and found the result
satisfactory.
SLOW SUICIDE.
GOOD SHEPHERD (to inebriate): “My man , do
you know that the cursed drink is slow death?"
INEBRIATE: “ That's all right then, boss. I’ve
been attempting suicide this last 15 years."
“ J.D.” (Waihara) : Be ants. Hang
things up by a bit of chain—one with round links
preferred. Always use hobble-chains myself.
The ants get as far as the second link, run wildly
round and round it, and then get dizzy and give
the problem up in despair.
HIS LOGIC.

BLACKFELLOW : “Ole Brown only give me 10s. a week. Got to work like the devil to make it pay!"
This is a tale of Ondit, Gippsland. The
Wyall brothers had a bit of land at Ondit, and
had built a very comfortable paling hut on it, and
started an orchard, and were doing well on the
whole. 1 n e n
Crowther put
up his water-
power saw-mill
on the creek,
and the Wyalls
sold their tim-
ber rights to
him at so much
a thousand feet.
Crowther put
his men on the
land felling, and
one day, when
the Wyall bro-
thers went home
to lunch after
p 1 oug hing a
patch for pota-
toes, they found
a man at a stick
near their hut.
Assuming that
an experienced
timber-getter
was on the job,
they entered
their humble cot
and proceeded
to victual with-
out a tremor or
an apprehensive
thought. About
11 minutes later
they heard her
crack and
squeal, and the
next second
some tons of
solid timber
came down
across the end
of their hut, cut-
ting the erection
clean in half,
and pounding
one half to
smithereens.
The escape of
the Wyall bro-
thers was little
short of miracu-
lous. They made
one wild, simul-
taneous jump
for the door and
out into the day, feeling like killing the mis-
creant who didn’t know better than to drop a
tree on unoffending settlers at meals. The feller
was standing just outside, leaning on his axe,
gazing at the battered hut with an aggrieved
expression. When he saw the Wyalls he spoke
up : “A lurid lot o’ good your runnin’ out now l ”
he said, with scorching contempt.

TASMANIAN HALTING PLAGES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When out in search of Saggy Flat
I came across some funny places,
Where old-time pioneers have sat,
And queer names left to mark their traces ;
For some are apt and some are harsh
Plain, mountain, river, hill and marsh.
On White Horse Plain, in Davey’s Port,
I’m certain that no horse has footed ;
No Cow and Calf of good report
To graze on Adamson’s have scooted ;
And Coffin Creek and Grave-yard Head
Sound reminiscent of the dead !
Of odd-named marshes what a lot!
Broad, Scrummy’s, Green’s, Long,Christian, Brushy,
Sykes, Judy’s, Beedy, and Tinpot,
Mike Howe’s, Dead Horse, Black Boy, and Bushy ;
Pat’s, Laughing Jack, White Kangaroo -
Of all the host I name but few.
Three sets of Hell’s Gates I have seen,
But only one sweet Land of Promise ;
Through Eden, Paradise I’ve been,
And tramped across the Plains of Thomas.
Who early rises at The Lawn
Will not find Nowhere Else at dawn.
The Badger Biver creeps along,
The Bapids roar—so does the Dasher ;
The Queen trills to the King a song—
The Princess is a smaller splasher.
The Savage joins the Pieman bold,
To flow with waters dark and cold.
To honor science mounts are crowned,
Their rugged peaks, star-gazing, dwindle ;
Jukes, Sedgwick, Owen group around
With Huxley, Lyell, Darwin, Tyndall;
And Murchison is of the band
Who scan the wealth ’midst which they stand.
The Parson’s Hood and Frenchman’s Cap
Lift crests apart from Collins’ Bonnet;
The Cradle beats Barn Bluff a lap—
Piled deep the virgin snow upon it.
Long-back, House-top, and Granite Tor,
Look-outs and Sugar Loaves galore.
From out the Lake Tiberias
We see the Holy Jordan flowing
Past Jericho —we leave it as
On to Jerusalem we’re going.
Who framed these names ?—we sight Bagdad-
Quaint Eastern notions Westerns had !
Plain, mountain, river, hill, and marsh,
Where e’er of old camp fires were burning,
In simple form—some apt, some harsh—
High-flown to plainer speech returning,
The pioneer has left a trace—
A name to mark each halting place.
I’AS.
J. E. PHILP.

communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MSS. No liability is under-
taken re contributions voluntarily submitted, whether
sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will be
returned unless stamps (of any State) accompany.

Old Professor Liversidge, just retired
from Sydney University, will be most remembered
by his trick in putting an “ N ” before each vowel
sound. Every Sydney undergrad, calls oxygen
“ noxygen,” and the professor’s assistant is never
called anything else than “ Nedward.”
