Bilarion Edwards —the lady who is Bill
or the gentleman who is Marion, whichever wav
you like to look at her-him —now runs a pub in
Napier-street, Fitzroy, Melbourne. He-she is
very popular there, and is said to be doing well.
Another injustice to Judaea ! The Argus
notifies that “ Mr. James Aaron, a Chinese, has
sent a six-guinea cheque to Warrnambool
Hospital.”
At Truro (S. Australia) a feeble man and
old (he was 84), Johann Christian Jaegar, lately
suicided by hanging. He might just as well have
waited and gone out by effluxion of time.
George Reid, at a Sydney school breaking-
up t’other day : “ Next year I will probably give a
prize to the boy who gets nearest to a prize with-
out getting one.” Thus does the elderly ’bus-loser
start to sow the seed of ’bus-missing in the minds
of the rising generation.
“ Bob ” Fitzsimmons has been picked by
an Italian authority as the most perfect modern
specimen of the Roman gladiator. Fitzsimmons
is accordingly to be sculptured in marble, and the
statue placed amongst those of the ancient fighters
as an essay on the physical development of this
century.
Again is Alfred Deakin’s name mentioned
as a possible High Commissioner, and again has
Deakin stated that he won’t leave Australia.
Those who know him best know him to be sincere.
And why all this fishing about among men who
are not available when an excellent man in B. R.
Wise is available?
C. C. Kingston, Australia’s great Demo-
crat, gets no better, and the chances are against
his taking any further active part in politics,
although he displays the greatest possible interest
in everything that goes on both in the State and
the Federal Legislatures. His wife is his con-
stant and devoted nurse.
Another score for Australia. A couple of
years back an Australian ex-coasting skipper,
named Forbes, invented and patented a log and
course-recording apparatus for the use of ships,
and, backed by a syndicate, submitted it to the
British Admiralty. After six months’ exhaustive
tests the Admiralty has decided to fit the inven-
tion to all British warships.
Jenkin Coles, who began to earn his ow n
crust by enrolling as a mounted policeman, has
now been continuously Speaker of the S.A.
Assembly since June 1890, and has sat for one
district without a break since LBBl. Jenkin was
once a Ministerial colleague of Kingston—it was
in the Six Fathom Ministry; every one of the
half-dozen members of it was a six-footer.
The Australian (the alleged-tired Aus-
tralian who wants to lean against a post, and
can’t do anything for himself, not even make a
clothes-peg) is in most places where pluck and
brains and adaptability are asked for. The latest
turn-up of the sort is from the Marconi wireless
station at Siasconset, Nantucket (U.S.A.). Ex-
Australian J. Cowden helped to instal and is now
working the new machines there.
James Ashton, M.L.C. (N.S.W.), has
become a “guinea pig.” He now holds the seat
on the Australian Widows’ Fund Board, rendered
vacant by the death of James Watson, M.L.C.
These Boards do not look for business capacity in
their directors—a title, a membership of either
House, the Upper for choice, or any other public
position likely to attract notice, are the qualifica-
tions desired. The manager does the work, while
the Board draws fees with surprising regularity.
Victoria’s Premier Bent is the man of
many has-beens. Judged out of his own mouth,
he has been everywhere, he has seen every-
thing, and he has been practically everything
that a man may be. He has not yet confessed
to having been to the South Pole, to having
earned his living as a grave-digger, or to
having been eaten by cannibals, but there
may still be time. The other day he told a
Brighton audience that he was a preacher
once, and preached in St. John’s Church.
Just before that he had told a lady in-
terviewer that no woman could
teach him anything about
cooking. “ I can cook cab-
bages with anyone,” he de-
clared. “I was cook onboard
ship once.” Bent is absolutely
accomplished in the matter of cab-
bages. We know him as a grower.
In England he said there was not a
better judge of cabbages in the world,
and now he stands forth as the un-
equalled cabbage cook. By the way,
Thomas talks about his family tree now-
a-days—presumably his cabbage tree.
People who wish to steal with
safety should study the career of
Frederick Watson of Victoria. Watson
was a trustee of a temperance “Tent.”
He took the key of a safe in which his
co-trustees kept their money, had a
duplicate made by a friend under the
pretence that it was for his (Watson’s)
back door, returned the original key to
its place in the bank, kept the dupli-
cate for months, and then when there
was a good haul in the safe quietly ex-
tracted £l5O. He was bowled out,
brought to trial after some difficulty,
and convicted of stealing the cash. A
number of leading temperance folk,
including Sam Mauger, Federal Post-
master-General, went along and told
a burning tale of the prisoner’s virtues
as a blue-ribboner, with the result that
Watson was released under the First
Offenders Act. It looks like a scandal,
but then Watson was “a worker in
the noble cause of temperance.”
Talk about sticking to a mate! Two
Maorilanders, Donnell an and Monkman, met at
Klondyke and agreed to battle for gold together.
They were doing all right when Donnellan
sickened and died. Monkman had the body em-
balmed and kept it in the hut for two solid years.
Then he returned to the M.L. Dominion, and
handed the remains over to relatives at Wellington.
A Taringa (Q.) man, one of these helpless
Australians named Jackson, has invented a
machine to dig potatoes. At a trial, the other
day, it turned out several rows two and a half
chains long at the rate of a row a minute. Subse-
quent digging with forks—the usual way of
breaking up Murphy’s happy home—showed that
not a potato had been left in the ground by the
machine.
Sydney University is losing Prof. Liver-
sidge, after having him for 32 years. When he
arrived, Science was scarcely hinted at in Sydney
IJni., and it wasn’t for nine years that he realised
his hopes and got a Faculty of Science estab-
lished. Gradually he moulted some of his sub-
jects, and stuck to Chemistry. Arts and Laws
courses were both in full blast when Liversidge
arrived, yet both the first Arts and the first Laws
graduates are still busy. Dr. Renwick is the one,
and Judge Rogers the other.
The name of the late Henry Rickefcson,
of N.S.W., came up in the High Court, the other
day, when barrister Canaway referred
to him as a champion litigant;
who had lost only one action
out of 80 he had brought.
The Law Reports, however,
record at least three cases
in which “Old Rick” was
badly beaten. He had a
mania for litigation, and
for over 20 years he carried
on ceaseless lawsuits
against the late Robert
Barbour (M.L.A. for the
old Murray electorate),
who, in the ’TO’s, selected
large portions of “Rick’s”
Barratta and Connalla
Stations, near .Denili-
quin. Upon one oc-
casion “ Rick ” induced
the local pastoralist or-
gan (which was under
the control of an ex-
Methodist parson) to
publish a flaming
article about Barbour.
An action for libel
followed, with a ver-
dict for £250 against
the paper. “Rick”
generously headed a
subscription list with
£lO, and left the
astonished Methodist
and the squatters of
the district to find the
balanceof the damages
and Costs. At the
time of his death,
which occurred about
10 years ago, “Rick”
was fighting the
N.S.W. Government
in the law courts over
the invasion of his
Billabong Station by
miners at the time of
the Wyalong rush.
The tin country
in and about Stan-
thorpe (Q.) has got its
magnate —Mrs. Alli-
son, of Sugar loaf. The
whole of her estate is
metalliferous, and is
mostlysublet toChinese,
who lately paid from £lO
to £l6 per ton royalty
for the tin won. Mrs.
Allison becomes the buyer,
and sends the stuff to her
mills at Stanthorpe; and she runs a store—
for the benefit, of course, of the tributors. At
Stanthorpe she has built a college, and three
miles away put up a sanatorium, so there are few
trade routes on which Mrs. Allison does not
collect the ’bus fares.
Dawe’s Point and Blue’s Point (Sydney),
which figured so largely in the Carruthers abortive
North Shore railway project, were once connected
by a rude, though efficient, ferry service. Billy
Blue, after whom one point was named, was the
ferryman of the service. Billy took out most of
the ferry profit in land in the form of Crown
grants, and with the rest he bought up job lots, so
that before he died he owned nearly all of the
North Shore. Dawe, who gave his name to the
other point, was a soldier, with a kink for
astronomy, and died poor.
John Browne, who recently died at the
alleged age of 119, in Liverpool(N.S.W.) Asylum,
may be turned to use in three ways. He smoked
till death, which is rough on the,anti-nicotine
brigade. He drank rum while he could buy or
otherwise obtain it. That should hit the Boyce
teetotal band pretty hard. Thirdly, when his
rum was cut off by hospital economies at one
period of his life, Carrington, then N.S.W.
Governor, interceded with Parkes, then Premier,
to order a breach of the common hospital rules,
and Parkes complying, the rules were broken, and
John received his rum. That shows what a
Governor can do with Premiers, officials, and a
public institution when he likes.
“ Vigilans ” about the Cattle Monarch and
his belongings
Kidman and the Georgina River (Q.) are synonymous.
Between Camooweal and Eurandangie the great cattle-
dealer owns Austral Downs and Heddingley. Between
Eurandangie and Boulia he has Carandotta. From
Boulia to Birdsville and Hergott he has Glengyle, Careon,
Rosebrook, St. Albans, Dubbo Downs, Annandale and
Kidgewarry. None of these runs is stocked with more
than 3000 or 4000 beasts, and each is in charge of a
manager or caretaker. Austral, Heddingley, and St.
Albans have been deserted, and the stock sold. Caran-
dotta, upon which the Carandotta Pastoral Co. (a Bull
concern) spent nearly £250,000 in houses, sheds, fences
and wells, was acquired by Kidman for £75,000, with
50 000 sheep and 5000 cattle. The sheep have been sold,
likewise 3000 cattle. The run is in charge of one man,
and the improvements are going fast to ruin. The home-
stead is large and commodious, built of stone and
imported woodwork. It is falling to pieces by reason of
pallid ants. Glengyle and Annandale are used as depots
for travelling stock. At one time they shore 120,000
sheep at Carandotta, but not for long ; it’s a droughty,
precarious stock-raising country.
“ S.” writes :
Mount Gambier (S.A.) boasts a female drum-major. The
lady’s name is Olive Hammer. She is a Miss, and she
leads the local brass band in an appropriate frock, with
epaulettes and military fittings, and swings ahead of the
drums whirling the usual long gaudy baton with a gold
nob and white whiskers on it. She brings her men along
in a quick-step with a spring and dash not excelled by any
band in the Commonwealth. Miss Hammer claims to be
a pioneer in a new field of woman’s work, and the papers
declare that she has “ a soldierly bearing ” and a “ dash-
ing, masterful manner.” The Mount Gambier band under
her command won a prize for marching at the South-
street (Ballarat) competitions.
“ CHIC AND CHICKER."
Arthur Henry, for nearly 36 years N.S.W.'a
Registrar in Bankruptcy, has retired at the
mature age of 69 —at least a Public Service List
fixes his birth-year as 1838—and now makes a fresh
start as a cocky-farmer. His first official employ-
ment in New South Wales was as private secretary
to Governor Belmore. When that very ordinary
potentate left and was hidden from view by the
curvature of the earth it was considered the
rectangular thing that his secretary should be
provided for, and James Martin gave him the
billet of Registrar of the Insolvency Court, as it
was then called, amid the rage of the junior Bar
and the bitter anger of Gentleman One. Mr.
Henry has outlived all the other officials who were
attached to the court when he joined it.
“ 10-2-4 ” writes :
A German Egyptologist, writing in the Berliner
Tagdische Rundschau, has made some discoveries that
are absorbing to the religious side of my nature. At the
beginning of the year this scientist unearthed Meneph-
toh’s mummy. Menephtoh was the Pharaoh who held
the fort of his ancestors what time thfe Children of
Israel were delivered from bondage. The trouble
mentioned in the Book of Exodus arose largely,
it transpires, from Pharaoh’s deplorable teeth. They
were full of cavities, and were essentially the grinders
of one who had a bad time. There is a Menephtoh eye-
tooth which was obviously for long in a very horrible
state. A black, jagged, honeycombed mass of bone
it is—the fang, in a word, to make a sovereign “harden
his heart,” as the Bible writer hath it, and treat his
subjects scurvily and with much callousness. Other facts
established re the deceased Menephtoh are that he was,
at the time of his death, plain and very fat.
He was sft. Bin. in height, bald (only a few
paltry wisps of white hair encircled his poll),
and the possessor of immense fiat feet. His nose was
long and thin, and of the extreme Semitic type. Further-
more, the wretched monarch’s blood-vessels and ribs
show unmistakable signs of chalky degeneration, from
w'hich it is deduced he suffered from uric acid. A
lamentable story, and it makes one feel quite ashamed of
the harsh words one said in one’s uninformed and prig-
gish childhood re this maligned royalty. Given gout,
rheumatism, decayed teeth, baldness, huge feet, and
revolting ugliness, and there is many an esteemed citizen
in our midst who is capable of setting all Potts Point to
work building pyramids.
GENTLEMEN, THE KING!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I fain would sing about a King who lives near London
town,
And humbly raise my song of praise unto his great re-
nown ;
And countless millions join with me, and strain their
loyal necks
In protests loud of loyalty to Albert Edward Kex,
For Edward by the grace of God (and also by descent)
Is King of England, and Chief Boss of this great continent*
He’s Suzerain and High Serang of peoples far and wide —
Their overlord and landlord and the Lord knows what
beside.
He owns the ships that float upon the ocean’s mighty
girth,
And owns or has a mortgage on a quarter of the earth.
His flag flies on a| hundred seas ; his watchful war-dogs
keep
An open space for him to pace the highways of the deep.
“ The sun is always shining o.) some part of his domains ” ;
But as it’s also setting, it is doubtful what he gains.
Throughout his vast dominions every day his praise is
sung v
By happy, faithful subjects—and there’s also someone
hung.
There’s no one discontented where he rules by Grace
Divine —
Save a few odd hungry millions, who’ve forgotten where
they dine.
The sword of battle is unsheathed, or sheathed at his
behest,
And Parliament assembles at his Majesty’s request.
With pious zeal his satraps deal with problems deep and
great,
And all agree that they should be his Ministers of State.
To them each nigger is a man, they love him as a brother,
And put their trust in everyone—excepting one another.
Throughout the greatest empire ’neath the canopy of
Heaven,
The land and all that therein is belong to Edward Seven ;
His calm, grey-whiskered countenance on every coin we
see —
For they are owned, of course, by his Most Gracious
Majesty.
In fact, there’s One thing—only One —’tis strange he
should forget
To put his “ monniker ” upon—and that’s our Public
Debt.
WESTRALIA.
TOM BEASLEY.

to stay young—and look young. No woman,
whatever her age, is older than she looks.
Valaze will keep her looking young—then why
not Yalaze ? Idle question—Yalaze is and has
been these five years the boon companion of good
looks —Yalaze the means and Beauty the end.
Are you a stranger to Yalaze ? Have you not
found the wonders of this dainty Russian Skin
Food works on your complexion—how it smooths
away the ugly, creeping wrinkles, bids crowsfeet
fly, and wards off the sunburn and freckles our
hot Australian sun is so prodigal of ? Then be
wise in time. Take Valaze by the forelock—use
it now—to-day. Your chemist has it—the gifb
of a beautiful complexion within your reach !* 2

BUCHANAN'S WHISKY,

FACILE PRINCE PS SEMPER IDEM
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, December 21, 1907.
My Dear Arint,—
Melba’s song interludes, at her Sydney matinee,
were filled with the signing of her name in
birthday books. The books were in piles,
and Melba signed for all she was worth,
and sighed at the damp heat that, curiously
enough, gave her vocal chords, as she herself
confessed, an added brilliancy. The white tulle
hat, with ostrich and osprey piumes, that she wore
with cream chiffon didn’t “go” well with her
black hair. No black-haired party looks her best
in dead white. I once knew an unfamous soprano
who used to sing with one foot in evidence, and
posed on a footstool. Her pretence was that
“ that ankle was weak.” It was a small No. 3
foot and beautifully formed (so, of course, was
its fellow), and it always ensured her as
many auditors as her singing kept away.
The object of this discursion is to intimate that
Melba’s feet are the smallest ever! If she
would wear shortish gowns she’d have all the old
Union Club gentlemen in the front seats every
time. She wears a size two shoe, which, for an
eleven stone or.'so lady, puts Cinderella’s slipper out
in the back yard to be eaten by the dog. Ola Jane
Humphrey is another Goody Two (sized) Shoes.
But the American women are recognised as being
able to wear what the boot shopmen call unsale-
able sizes.
There is yet a later hat-shriek than the one
mentioned by me last week. The ultimate
Parisian outcry in this,line, is not necessarily a
huge-brimmed lid after all. On the contrary, it
is one which produces a Botticelli effect. French
fashion journals unite in a mad paean of enthusiasm
concerning a female headpiece which is repro-
duced in a recent issue of La Vie Parisienne. It
is of the height, rotundity and general appearance
of a battered belltopper or chimney-pot or plug
headgear. The framework is swathed in green
velvet. Thus the owner seems to be wearing
a flat-brimmed, green velvet topper which has
been recently kicked to death. Attached to
the front portion of the contrivance are two
gigantic black bird’s wings. London Daily Mail
mentions this creation with awed approval; also
another, whereof an illustration is given, which
consists simply of the body—with tail, wings,
feet and head complete—of an enormous wild-
fowl bashed on to the purchaser’s head and left
there. One could fashion as chic a hat locally
from a dead emu or buzzard. However, the
essence of all these fashion-contracts is that the
deceased bird should have met its end in Paris,
or should have been taken there after death.
Science is a horrid leveller. It has no appre-
ciation of the pageantry of life, and is a ruthless
buster-up of Romance. A year ago we read awe-
somely of the King of Spain’s “ Hapsburg
mouth.” The phrase was effective. We took,
most of us, a mental retrospect adown the ages,
and pictured the youth’s mighty line of forbears
all with this sort of mouth, and grew quite
worked up on the subject. It transpires that the
“Hapsburg mouth ’’—which is, to speak sooth, a
loose, gaping and purposeless sort of cavity—
comes solely from adenoids. The mouth is
not hereditary at all-only the adenoids.
Alphonso is what is known as a “ mouth-
breather,” and will be operated on in Paris
early next year. He doesn't mind his ade-
noids himself; for one thing, they have been
so extremely long in the family that thev have
grown like old friends. But they make him
snore, and so his fair young wife has issued her
ukase, and the growth of ages must go.
Miss Victoria Lyne, daughter of the great tariff
maker, is back from rambles amongst the seats of
the mighty. She tarried long after the Great Jag
known as the Conference of Premiers had been
full stopped, and report engaged her matrimonially
to someone who had a coronet on his bluey.
Talking of engagements, Miss Bramah, one of
Edwin Geach’s new importations in the Thornton
co., became engaged on her voyage here by
the India to the commander of that vessel.
He carries the uncommon name of Vibert
and an unusual amount of popularity, and is a
vigorous, crushed-strawberry-faced gentleman.
His million or so friends say that he has re-
mained unlassoed all these years solely because he
was too kind-hearted to marry. He could please
only one woman by taking up the white man’s
burden, but he would grieve hundreds. All the
same, it is a cold job for the wife—this marrying
the Light of a Liner. She must not travel on his
craft—at least, that is the regulation on most big
steamers, I understand—and she must know 11. 1 c
all the lady moths under 40 are blinding tin-
selves circling around him, and thinking a.i i.. 0
time that they are dazzling him. Miss Bramah is
an artistic-looking young party. At least that is
the phrase used by those who know, and, there-
fore, like her. For the five-hundredth time : the
husband is a marine product.
“Steady, men, prepare to receive infantry—
and doggery too,” says Robert Chambers, ex-
military hero, in his last (charming but too yanked-
out) book, “The Younger Set.” And at the
word he is overwhelmed by an onset of
seven children, ranging in age from thirteen
to three years, and their seven dogs. Well,
for the last month Sydney has been preparing
to receive infantry. Sydney’s weapons and barri-
cades are toys, and before the infantile rush, now
at its hottest, Sydney is yielding like dry grass
before the bush fire. Nothing very new has shown
up in the toy line. There’s a revival of the Golly-
wog doll, and in David Jones’ may be found the
whole Zoo —in upholstery materials. The Teddy
Bear is still a great favorite with the young
battlers; so are velvet cats with a mouse or a
cricket ball between their front paws, and
waggish dogs. The dachshund devotes itself
to waggishness, and the young inquiring mind
is always asking why its legs are crooked,
and striving to straighten them. Mechanical
birds andreptiles, that run or walk for aspace, have
a good sale and a short life—cut short by general
paralysis of the machinery or the inquiring mind
that pulls the toy to bits to see what makes it go.
Motor-cars and steam engines are in great demand
by boys, and the mothers of the demanders say,
“ He is such a man already, and I’m sure he’ll be
Edison over again—he’s always wanting to know
about wheels and puffing and other scientific
things.” The new game is Manorama, or Hearts’
Delight—a melange of tennis, ping-pong, and
basket ball.
The day of the mother-in-law is here. A dis-
tinguished American lawyer has begun an action
in his State’s law courts to enforce his mother-in-
law’s return to his home. Since she left he is
lonely and the house mismanaged.
The red (or otherwise) slayer again! Mrs.
Melton Prior, wife of the war artist, has been
killed by a motor car. Artist Prior was in Aus-
tralia what time Coolgardie was the Mecca of gold
seekers. His clothing and arming, when he did
the coach journey from Perth to Coolgardie, sent
the teamsters en route mad with joy. Revolver
and ammunition and a knife were in evidence in
his Bond-street tailoring.
A wad of newspaper cuttings, thick enough
for a small bolster, to hand, discloses, layer
by layer, what a glorious artist is Miss Ada
Crossley, and how successful has been her just-
concluded English provincial concert tour. She
has sung at festivals and on her own, and
one of the critics mentions that the Austra-
lian has now been long enough in the
Mother Country to entitle her to the desig-
nation, “English contralto.” Percy Grainger,
who was of the party, is also pelted with the
appreciative adjective, and tenor John Harrison,
who was a pupil of Amy Sherwin, shares the
fusillade that artists love. Harrison likes to give
his audience Beethoven’s “ Adelaide.” Scarcely
anyone sings “ Adelaide ” these days. Our grand-
mothers, so one tells me, would not consider the
concert or home-circle musical evening anything
but a hard frost if “Adelaide” were not pro-
grammed.
The Right Hon. James Fran\9s Harry St.
Clair-Erskine, Earl of Rosslyn-j to give him
his full style and title—seems lb be one of
the deftest and pleasantest co-res now extant,
ihis hereditary British legislator w )s successfully
proceeded against last month by one Captain
Saunders, D.S.O.—who is the outr'alkl husband of
the frolicsome Earl’s most recent .Vfinity. Pro-
bably there never has been a case pf the kind
wherein things movedmoresmoothly. i\ r o damages
were asked. The Earl’s feelings would have been
hurt—besides he mightn’t have paid anyhow.
The most amiable letters were put in evidence.
The first Saunders knew of any awkwardness
was on October 12, 1906, when he got the follow-
ing, signed by the co-re., and dated from the Carl-
ton Club :
Dear Captain Saunders,—l have not the honor of
your acquaintance, so beg you to excuse my address-
ing you so, but I have been served with a petition for
divorce by my wife, in which your wife’s name is
mentioned It is an accusation for which, it is
■eedless to say, there is absolutely no foundation.
Yours very affectionately, Rosslyn.
Best he might annoy Captain Saunders, or cause
undue expense, Lord Rosslyn refused to defend
the suit. Anyhow, he is bored with the divorce-
court entourage, and is not socially acquainted
with Mr. Justice Bargrave Deane, who pre-
sides there (and moreover absolutely refuses
to know him). But he has taken all the
onus of everything on his shoulders, and has
promised he will pay the Costs—some day;
and he doesn’t complain an atom. Truly the man
is a preux chevalier, not to say a Bayard.
The Shackleton-David party of South Polar
explorers will need to continue the dash with
which they have begun to keep ahead of rivals.
Already three other expeditions to the South Pole
are being fitted up—one French, one Belgian, and
one American. Dr. Charcot, Dr. Cook, and
Mr. Arctowski, the leaders, are men of
repute, each greedy for polar bears and honors,
and if the expeditions come together on an ice-
floe in the vicinity of Mount Erebus, no one can
tell which will come back. Professor David has,
however, been experimenting lately with a new
chemical explosive, which is easily carried, and is
said to be capable of shifting mountains out of the
way. If he has taken a supply of this with him
the Shackleton-David party should be able to
climb over all rivals.
Mrs. O’Doherty, widow of the late Kevin Izod,
of Brisbane, was not alone a poetical revolution-
ary in her early Irish days. She was an actual
aider and abettor of rebels, as the law defined
matters at that period. When John Dillon,
fn fher of the Dillon who visited Australia a few
v trs ago, was proscribed with a price on his head,
>! O’Doherty, then Miss O’Kelly, clad him in
( .urical broadcloth and the murky hat of a celi-
bate life, and escorted him, as Father O’Kelly,
through a cordon of watching police. Dillon
played the part becomingly, and got off to
America, where he remained until the British
Government issued a general pardon.
The late Dr. Neild, of Melbourne, used to label
all women under two headings as “ the kissable”
and “the unkissable.” Miss Donaldson, of the
Thornton company, which opens to-night in “When
Knights Were Bold ” (or should it be Bowled ?)
looks like an eminently kissable girl. She’s tall
and good-figured, with light blue eyes, flaxen hair,
blush rose complexion (when she’s fatter and older
it will be cabbage rose), and the kind of
underlip that poet Suckling has immortalised
“as if a bee had stung it newly.”
She is a Manchester girl, and her first engagement
was as the ingenue, Lady Mary Carlyle’s cousin,
in “Monsieur Beaucaire.” Since then she has
been playing good young r6les and understudying
distinguished actresses. She is very fond of bread
and butter and housework and outdoor games,
and when she left London the long sleeves were
coming in. Yes, the voyage out on the India
was very pleasant, and the Australian women are
not even six weeks behind in the matter of
fashions. That’s all. Perhaps the city maid will
prove more versatile on the boards. Most actresses
are difficult under the interviewer’s stump-extrac-
tor, and facts are grubbed out of them with diffi-
culty, though many Americans and some Austra-
lians are exceptions to this rule. Anyhow, Miss
Donaldson will certainly give the Sydney young
man heart disease, just as Miss Bramah (doesn’t
that name remind of the Light of Asia and the
Brahmapootra hen?) gave it to the captain of the
India. His vessel went out to-day. Too bad that
he couldn’t wait to see his lady’s first appearance
here.
Mrs. Waldron, who as Miss May King took
“ Squaw Man ” Waldron as a mate, has cabled her
friends here that in future when they make kind
inquiries they may include a small May.
The Dearman divorce case, which has created a
mild sensation in Sydney for some weeks, ended
in a way which seems calculated to make the
petitioner-husband go and bite the corners off the
furniture. It isn’t the loss of the case which is
the trouble —that might happen to any man ;
the grief lies in the unconscious humor
of his Honor in giving judgment. Mel-
bourne Nathan Dearman—not Ballarat Abraham
Dearman; don’t make any mistake about
the name—asked for a divorce on the alleged
ground that his wife had frisked with a third
party. His Honor said, first, that he half sus-
pected that the lady had frisked, but he wasn’t
sure. He then said that the husband would
doubtless feel glad that it wasn’t positive that she
had frisked. He dismissed the case for in-
sufficiency of proof of frisk. And he ordered the
husband to pay his own Costs and his wife’s. So
these are the gentleman’s grievances: (1) He
has failed to get his freedom; (2) he has
had all the publicity for nothing ; (3) he
has to pay his own Costs ; (4) he has to pay his
wife’s Costs ; (5) he has still to support the lady ;
(6) he is told that he ought to feel comparatively
glad, not that his suspicions have, in the
Judge’s opinion, been wholly disproved, but
because the Judge has dark suspicions
himself, yet can’t make up his mind.
If the Judge had simply dismissed the case, it
mightn’t have been so bad ; but that sort of sug-
gestion that the petitioner should hasten home
with his mind full of gloomy doubts, and a huge
bill in each hand, and the lady he wanted to get
rid of on his back (metaphorically speaking), and
there skip for joy like the young lamb on the
gable of the precipice—well, why shouldn’t he
bite the furniture? Some men would bite the
ceiling.
Australia would seem to produce great new
voices with even more remarkable regularity than
it does great new bowlers, scullers, and other
phenomenaof themoment. Thusat Amy Sherwin’s
pupils’ concert at the Town Hall last Wednesday
night, one Mr. Henry Campbell, hitherto unknown
to fame, sang in a mighty tuneful tenor voice,
whose golden notes, were they but trained, would
be worth much gold, not to apeak-of-.' .rubies and
such-like portable pttipb'Pfy'—to quote the burning
words of Mr. Wemmick. Also, Miss Ruby Adams
appeared and established a sensation. She was
as a professional among amateurs. Curiously
piquant was the contrast between the tall
(very tall) and slender (extremely slender)
ingenue- faced girl and the big, passionate
voice with its under-hint of all life’s knowledge
and experience. She trilled flawlessly
on the high nightingale note at the
end of Herschel’s beautiful old “ Spring
Song ” and in the Lucia Sestet sang D
in alt. with electrifying ease. This was a
concert of home-grown voices, and it wasn’t
the less interesting on that account. The
choir, trained and conducted by Mrs. Sherwin,
gave a fresh and sweet account of itself. Mrs.
Sherwin herself put some silver point on the
programme with her own voice, and there was
a (to her unexpected) jewel scene when two pupils
presented her on behalf of the others with some-
thing gemmed and glittering and rainbowy.
After Gabriel has blown about three notes, it
is pretty certain that some calm woman will ask
him to hold on a bit while she goes and gets a cup
of tea. And it is quite possible that it will be a
woman who turned up at Rangiora (M.L.) lately.
A very busy railway porter at that station was
driven to the verge of distraction when he dis-
covered a sleeping infant in a carriage that was
being shunted off the line into a shed. In vain
he tramped the platform seeking the owner of the
luggage; nobody owned it, or wanted it. At last
he ducked into the tea-room, and found the
mother. Oh, no, she hadn’t forgotten it ; she
knew it would sleep soundly until she returned,
and she was glad the porter hadn’t wakened the
darling. The porter, of course, never said a word.
He couldn’t think of a word. He just went
behind a heap of luggage, and asked the universe
if there was a word.
A lady who signs herself “ Boh ” sends this:—
As a useless female person, I rise to denounce the
“ Come-and-bring-your-work ” afternoon tea party, which
is the only social caper possible to the lorn suburban
female, as she waits for the returning steps of her men-
folk. Was bidden to an afternoon riot of this kind lately.
Arriving late, found the room thick with scandal and the
flashing of needles. They talked not of the drama, of
art, or letters, but always of “ the other woman ” —who
wasn’t there. Their methods reminded me of the smart
man in the problem play, who gets the nasty things off
his chest as he scratches the stage business match to light
the ditto cigar. The busy ladies said their smart things
as they bent over their precious cross-stitch. I did not
bring my work, as it happened just then to be the white-
washing of the back kitchen. After an hour or so we had
tea, and the reputation-ripping commenced again. Some
day I will arise and call a pretty lady in a nice frock a
liar. Then there will be a scene, and perhaps I shall have
a paragraph in the paper. In the meantime I’ll go on
whitewashing the back kitchen. God knows it wants it 1
A Sydneyside man, who recently dropped in
upon Wellington, in M.L., found his way into
the Tourist Department, and was presently sur-
rounded by and introduced to nine of the girl
employees. To them he recounted his perils by
land and sea on the Milford Sound track. All
the brunette, mezzo and blonde she-civil ser-
vants were so sympathetic that the visitor
suggested to General Manager Donne that a team
of girls from the head office should act as guides
on the Milford track each year. “Yes,” replied
the chief, “ and have all the old sinners in Sydney
coming over here every summer.” Well, why
shouldn’t they ? Old sinners have money to spend,
as a rule.
One of “Our Own Correspondents” from
London mentions that Miss Violet Clarke,
daughter of a former Governor of Victoria, “has
decided to give up the lyric stage as a profession,
and confine herself to her literary work.” Now,
is this good for the art she has forsaken, or bad
for the art to which she has taken ? Once upon a
short while ago I mentioned to an Australian
theatrical manager that a certain Sydney girl had
evidently given up the stage, as she was bombard-
ing the editor with stories. With a sniff of
disgust, he replied: “Writing’s all she’s fit for.
She’d never be an actress.” With such contempt
do some view the poor story-spinner.
Consideration of various divorce cases reminds
one that there are many things capable of shatter-
ing Love’s young lunacy in its very first spasm, and
so staving off much subsequent misery and ex-
pense. Thus the current Yankee papers tell us
that at Windfall (Ind.) young Jarvis Scherer de-
cided to take Miss Minnie Landsade, his “fiance”
(U.S.A. French for best girl), for a drive. He
did. Two miles from home Jarvis Scherer drove
over a sleeping polecat. Without delving into
any Zolaesque details, it suffices to say that the
vehicle and horse had to be abandoned
while Jarvis and Minnie became pariahs on
the face of the earth till they got home,
fumigated themselves, burned their clothes, and
had 2f hours apiece in a Turkish bath. That
done, Minnie returned Jarvis his ring, announced
firmly that the alliance was off, and urged him
not to address her again while he lived.
Yours Affectionately,
AKENEHI.
Sandforcl goes out of the Lithgow (N.S.W.)
ironworks after 20 years’ struggling, and the
Hoskins firm comes in. The works will still go
on, and the ex-proprietor recovers at least part of
his money. All the same, it is very rough on
Sandford, who has toiled for half a lifetime in
pursuit of an honest ambition to establish the iron
industry of Australia on a sound footing, and has
to drop it when within a yard of the goal.
“ Queer Things About Persia ” is the title
of a recently-published book by Eustache de
Lorey and Douglas Sladen. Among other things,,
it states that the Persians are excellent horsemen,
and have a huge contempt for those who ride
badly. The authors tell a story of a faithful
servant who saved his European master’s reputa-
tion by explaining to the crowd, when he (the
master) fell from his saddle, that it was not due
to bad horsemanship, but that he was drunk.
Bernard Hall, Director of Melbourne
National Gallery and Art Schools, can consider
himself an exploded Art potentate. His picture
of the year—probably the picture of his life—is
“Sleep,” exhibited for the first time in Sydney a
few months ago, and since then recognised as one
of the brightest features of Melbourne’s annual
exhibition. But Smithtown, which issomewhereon
the Macleay River (N. S. W.). where they keep cows,
has a “ School of Arts,” and when the December
Lone Hand arrived the committee of the School
of Arts—mind the Arts !—solemnly met, and
resolved that “ Sleep,” therein reproduced, is an
improper picture ; wherefore “Sleep ” was cut out
before the volume was allowed to go into local
circulation. It was liable to divert the local mind
from lofty thoughts of Cow, so it was Accursed.
The long-drawn-out story of a tobacco rob-
bery was concluded in the Melbourne press reports
last week, when a shady Oollingwood storekeeper
was found guilty of having been implicated in the
theft of 12gcwt. of ’bacca from a city warehouse.
The prisoner owned up to one or two previous
convictions, but declared that the police had sworn
falsely against him in this case. Anyhow, the affair
presented an aspect suggestive of “fizgigism.” The
police were, presumably, waiting near the pre-
mises of the alleged receiver for a waggon that
duly arrived, with the tobacco and three or four
men aboard of it. One man, seemingly, escaped.
This escapee is alleged to have been the “fizgig”
who planned the robbery, and inveigled his mates
into it, and put the police in the way to effect a
capture. Two unquestionable mates were con-
victed, and sentenced to 10 years’ imprisonment,
a while ago, and they now gave evidence for the
defence. They both swore that the storekeeper
aforesaid was not with them on the night
of the robbery, and was not in the right-
of-way at the back of his shop when
the waggon arrived and the police made
a rush for it. With several years of imprison-
ment confronting them, these two men can
hardly have had a strong incentive to perjury—
conspiracy of perjury against the police—on
behalf of an alleged colleague in the robbery,
unless, indeed, they were smarting under a sense
of having been “fizgigged.” They explained
that they drove the waggon to the right-of-way
in order to get a cart from the storekeeper’s yard,
and transfer the tobacco to the lighter vehicle
before resuming their journey. The police story
was convincing in only one respect. It proved
that four detectives were grouped after midnight
in the neighborhood of the place where they
presently found a job; as to whether they or any
of them were legitimately informed of the job, or
were working in conjunction with a “ putter-up”
of felonies, there is their Word in one scale, and
the allegation of angry criminals in the other.
The jury, after deliberating for two hours on the
story of the four detectives who were keeping
one another company at midnight, decided that
it was half true. The prisoner was found guilty
of receiving the goods.
By this paper’s Oldest Inhabitant
Apropos the father of that old theatrical, Ben Leveson
(B. 19/11/’O7), we hear of Phil Solomon in the early
’thirties as a dealer in Lower George-street, in 1844 as
keeping the Elephant and Castle on the S.E. corner of
King and Pitt streets, and in 1847 as landlord of Solo-
mon’s Temple Inn on the S.W. corner of Erskine and
Clarence streets, where had stood the Crispin Arms. He
did not keep the house long, as in 1850 it was occupied by
another of the Chosen people, Abraham Joseph Levy, who
occupied it all through the ’fifties. When the house got
out of the hands of the Hebrew's it was christened the
Wynyard. It was demolished a couple of years ago
and an up-to-date building erected. This paragraph is
still another proof of the fact that it is hardly possible to
write of ancient inhabitants without also writing about
public-houses.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, December 23, 1907.
My Dear Yarrie,—
There will never be an end of the Tea Folks
memories of that Exhibition. They had a lively
time with some of the Sassiety dames. One
would carefully order a special and swagger lunch
for ten, say, in the cosy Dutch room. She
would roll up chiffonly and distractingly chatter-
ing with about 30 in tow, and oysters and turtle
soup and truffles would be expected in swift and
magic serving, with the “extra chairs ” to match.
One of these episodes ended by a Westralian lady,
of forcible tongue, invading the kitchens. There
was much fur flying, because the “ lady ” was
ordered out of the Dominion of Food by the
triumphant and dauntless manager girl. The
manager girl has departed for a well-earned rest
in some cool, ferny spot, and the lady retreats to a
land of loving slaves.
A little lady who had scored points in connec-
tion with the Woman’s Work Exhibition and who,
as a result of a few adulatory pars in the papers,
was beginning to think that the eye of the world
was focussed upon her, experienced a distressing
set-back the other day. She observed a marked
public interest as she passed down the street, and
when she entered a music-shop the curious ones
assembled at the door, and peered in with every
manifestation of excitement. When she came
forth there was quite, an assemblage. The crowd
opened before her, and necks were craned
curiously. There was a little jostling too, and
people made comments on the lady’s appearance.
Then a voice said: “Yah! That ain’t Mrs.
Wallace ! It ain’t a bit like ’er ! ” And the
crowd fell away in disappointment, while the
deserted one took up her shattered illusions and
went home.
It’s curious that until now no political wife has
attempted to turn her drawing-room into a Salon,
or the Australian equivalent of one. The differ-
ence is created easily enough—by entertaining
wise men instead of fools. But probably wise
men would abjure such attractions as Sassiety
offers them for the nonce—rich food and poor con-
versation. The political missus hasn’t tried to
remedy the discrepancies to date, however. Once
hubby has secured his seat, she eagerly
exchanges national interests for parochial
ones. For instance, what time the astute
Isaacs was a Cabinet Minister, Mrs. Isaac Isaacs
contented herself with being cultured, and taking
cookery lessons. Dame Forrest occasionally gave
a luncheon party at her hotel to a motley gather-
ing of actors and their smartest patrons, and
organised a series of subscription dances. Mrs.
Deakin lives in her quiet circle, only emerg-
ing from it to burden herself with some
official duties, or secretly do some gracious
deed of kindness or charity. Mrs. Groom’s
specialty is erudition, with side-lines in the
way of smart entertaining and tasteful frock-
ing. Mrs. Sammy Mauger once accompanied
66 Bourke-street when he drove round the
suburbs, judging a shop-window competition.
Yet the opportunities of a pleasant woman with
an intelligent interest in politics are un-
bounded. No one is more amenable to
feminine influence than the blithe politician.
And then a ringed, dimpled hand has such
an adroit and inconspicuous way of pulling
strings, and wields other people’s power so advan-
tageously. Presently some lady with a definite
ambition and a mediocre husband will inaugurate
a Salon, which will pretend to be a symposium,
and really be a show ground for big-little men’s
vanities. ' She’ll get all she wants! It’s a pity
that she isn’t anticipated by some gifted female
who lacks an ulterior motive.
It’s delightful to note the frantic endeavors of
local matrons with ordinary surnames, to disavow
the “ case.” One afternoon during the hearing of
the Wallace trouble, Mrs. Brownjohn will be casu-
ally mentioned. Within 24 hours, every Mrs.
Brownjohn for miles around will have rushed into
newspaper print with an unctuous denial that she
is even related to the Mrs. Brownjohn, now
under the eye of the Law. She usually adds,
with an inky air of virtue, that she is entirely un-
acquainted with anyone associated with the case.
Then the indignant matron makes her bow and
retires from a public gaze that otherwise might
never have seen her.
Saw the badgered “ Doctor Bob ” at the cricket
match, with his usual man clique. Strong’s a
youthful looking man, but worry forces out the
middle-aged aspect —the grey in the hair is grim-
mer, a nd there is a “ thin ” look at the back of the
neck. Melbourne medicoes keep their age well
on the whole. As an instance, consider Strong’s
double, Dr. M‘Gee. He has owned that same
sprightly youngster look for so long that the
memory of man can’t recall when he looked other-
wise. Auto-racing Davenport has no calendar
to thank for his youthfulness; mebbe he manages
it by the devil-may-care way he drops patients,
work and worry in his sporting holidays. In his
work-a-dav time there’s not a cooler, keener hand
at an operation.
Folk watching the exciting haul of names daily
dragged into the Wallace case marvel at the dead
silence about the plump tenor and the warm-
haired Algy of chiffon-counter fame. The duo
used to hang to the lady’s train like adoring
puppies. Concerts, theatres, suppers, balls—
always the Fat One or the Warm-Haired wedged
snugly between the fair lady and chum “Mrs.
Mac.” Algy keeps meekly to his counter nowa-
days. The tenor is in London.
Another yachtsman, ’tis said, contemplates a
visit to the Court of Liberation. But it may not
come off. He and his wife have been long prone
to regretful re-cooings after angry separations.
Little Mrs. Lee, drawn into the maelstrom of
the Great Divorce Case, might bleat a lesson to
young wives about obeying the wishes of their lords
and masters. Her particular owner tried many
methods to draw his wife from the atmosphere of
Novar. Mrs. Lee is the daughter of politician
Allan McLean. Hubby was Government expert
of some “culture” or other in N.S.W., and was
put in charge of the beet-sugar experiments at
Maffra. For the future, seemingly, the matron
will be a missionary with one text: “Wives,
hearken to the wise man who payeth the rent.”
How annoying is the spectacle of other people’s
enjoyments. And how vapid it seems ! I know
nothing more intolerable, when one is in a serious
strenuous mood, than watching others slaughter
common-sense, and their reputations for “ naice-
ness ” to make themselves a holiday. Even
the prospect of a holiday is demoralising.
In anticipation, and with regard to their
probable locations, lads and lasses re-arrange
their own flirtations, and disorganise those
of other folks, with the most callous dis-
regard for any buds of romance that may be
opening. In holiday time girls let their
souls go on the “ bust ” with creatures
they wouldn’t even bow to at any_ other
season. And men make vows which include
eternity, and aren’t fulfilled even for an hour.
Yet, I dare say, environment is more to blame
than the individual. It’s very difficult for a maid
to preserve an afternoon-tea attitude when
she’s sitting hand-in-hand with a brown, blithe
chum, hidden by a bosky curtain from all observ-
ant eyes. And it’s practically impossible
to be flippant when she and he stroll
through the moonlight, uninterrupted save by
the chirp of some belated bird (though it’s
quite easy to be frivolous under such circum-
stances !). It must be environment that makes
Cupid work so hard while his accomplices are on
furlough. For, after all, a woman always appears
less alluring in holiday season than at any other
time. She doesn’t mind looking for whole days
through as dishevelled as she appears only in the
early morning hours of other periods. She
becomes careless of her toilette, and wears the
shape that Fate inflicted on her as an original
punishment, and puts the improvements that have
been made thereon in the bottom of her dress-
basket. She offers little encouragement for
admiration —gives no reason for rhapsodies ; and
really doesn’t seem to desire sentimentality. Yet
she’s inundated with all these. Yes, indeed, it
must be environment!
Miladi Northcote paid a gracious attention to
some Wimmen’s Exhibition supernumeraries at
the last executive meeting. She had the firemen
who had done duty there called in, and gave them
a silver matchbox apiece in memory of the fact
that there hadn’t been any big fire.
Mrs. Percy Chirnside gave a children’s fancy-
dress party at Menzies’ this week, and most of
the youngsters made a joyful reappearance in the
charming frocks they had worn previously at the
Northcote spreelet. Mrs. MacDonald’s cherubs
were about the daintiest episode of the afternoon.
The most amusing aspect of these bibbed and
socked festivities is the embryo dignity and
sturdy self-possession of the babes-in-clover. The
attitude is very stolid and wonderfully consistent.
Yet the humor and good-temper of the brat
who has never owned a pair of whole boots except
by accident are really a lot more pleasing and
more natural.
King O’Malley, the airy legislator who once
wanted a permit to camp out and lead the Simple
Life in the Parliament House Reserve, is reputed
to be building a terrace of eight houses, A terrace
is a terribly ordinary thing. A wild original legis-
lator with a wild original hat, who wants to cook
his own buffalo steak in a tent, should build
pyramids, or something out of the common.
Mrs. F. S. Grim wade and her daughters and
husband go to the Respectable Country for a long
spell. They depart on Christmas Eve. What an
awful essay—to eat Christmas fare during the first
days of a tempestuous sea-voyage. But pro-
bably the medicine-chest of the chemical family
is capable of overcoming both horrors. More pro-
bably the family knows too much about dyspepsia
to eat any pudding.
Miss Beatrice Day, looking wonderfully well,
and more mondaine than ever, says with a wide
smile that it’s just delightful to be back again in
sunshine and clear air. She reveals frocks that
are indicative of what less audacious people
will be trying to wear next Cup-time. She’s
going to air them within the month. And she
says one pleasant thing worth noting—that
any woman can be as well dressed as she need
wish to be by local Madams, provided she doesn’t
permit herself to be gowned like anyone else.
Otherwise she tells of a holiday spent in working
hard at play-getting, frock-buying, Society and
study—and says that she’s relieved to start real
labor again, it’s so much less exhausting.
On Friday there was a function at the Catholic
Ladies’ College, when her Federal Excellency
visited the school to invest some students of honor
with the college badge. The Lady, having been
duly received by the Archbishop, Rev. Mother
Lawrence, and Prime Minister Deakin, was taken
off to the students’ hall. It was a delightful
scene—the big room filled with girls and flowers,
all virginal and white, with here and there a silent
nun with sombre gown and smiling face. The
star pupils were presented with jewelled, golden
badges emblematic of their virtues. Miladi
was quite tender in her kindly speech to the
honored ones, and the eloquent Alfred affected
them deeply, or seemed to.
The second Arral concert showed the lady in
her best vocal style, and her least becoming dress.
As usual, she sang like a bird, and, owing
to her curious coiffure, looked rather like an
unclassified one. But oh, how she sang !
It made one sigh for sentimental zephyrs,
and the sobs of a love-stricken nightingale,
and several other things unobtainable in Mel-
bourne Town Hall. It even made one forget
that influenza would surely be the penalty of ven-
turing to that infamously draughty place. The
other soloists were not enlivening—nor even
deadening. They were neither good nor bad
enough to distract a half-dressed woman from her
grievances against the Town Hall authorities.
The audience was undistinguished for the most
part. There were a lot of people in shirt-blouses,
and a large number in ball-dresses, but few were
notable. Mrs. MacGillicuddy was there in white
silk, looking rather haggard after her afternoon’s
appearance in the witness-box. Mrs. Gaston
Mervale, also in white with a head dress that
made her look like a tinted Greek statue, was
under the wing of a theatrical young man. And
Mr. John Wren and his missus, in a blue confec-
tion, came in late. Mr. Wren’s demeanor —full
of modest pride and unobtrusive self-content —
was most edifying. He might have been one of
the humble public, instead of the director of what
the programme described as a “ concert de luxe”
—whatever that may mean.
The old “ floral offering ” or vegetable tribute
practice was tolerable as long as it was confined
to the stage or platform, where the recipient or
recipients earned or didn’t earn the obtrusive
compliment. In its latest development of out-
door demonstration for the purpose of special
newspaper report it is a blatantly boomsome prac-
tice, that amounts to a public nuisance. The Age
published a postscript to the musical critic’s
notice of Mrs. Arral’s reappearance at Melbourne
Town Hall, under the clap-trap direction of John
Wren: —“A great proportion of the audience
waited around the main entrance, and as Mrs.
Arral emerged, wreathed in smiles and flowers,
she was ruthlessly set upon, and unceremoniously
robbed of her flowers and covered with fresh ones.
Many of those who could not get near enough to
present their floral offerings hung them on the
harness of the horses. A way out was cleared by
the police, and Mrs. Arral finally got away,
followed by & dropping fire of floral bunches.”
Funny little tales are told of the Conserva-
torium students’ performance at the Princess’s.
For instance, there was the ballet of children in
the “Faust” scene—eight dainty damsels, pro-
vided by the Green Dancing Academy. The
kiddies, accustomed to distinction in their Green
sphere of entertainment, naturally regarded them-
selves as the salt of the show. But in the “Faust”
scene, of course, they found that they amounted
to a mere passing incident. Having danced their
little bit, they were de trop. The chorus —coached
by Miss Maud Harrington—wanted all the avail-
able stage room, so the youthful ballerinas were
quite rudely hustled out of the way by the
grown-ups. Whereupon, one or two of them
burst into weeps, and behind the scenes the whole
ballet held an indignation meeting. If this was
real life on the stage they wanted no more of it.
They wished they were dead, and they were quite
sure their mammas wouldn’t let them appear in
opera any more. By the way, the top-scorer in the
amateur show was undoubtedly the fine chesty
tenor, Sydney Monk, who has a fortune in his
voice if he chooses to work for it. To the surprise
of everybody who saw him sidle on to the platform
a while ago, when he was singing between the
acts of a Taits’ biograph, the melodious Sydney
has already developed a good stage presence and
a graceful bearing—in repose, at any rate.
One of the week’s sprees had a formidable label
—“The Annual Dinner of the Prelectors of
Trinity College Dialectic Society.’’ But the feed
itself was a joyous episode in a “Savoy” private
room, to which all the wise men who have pre-
lected from year to year, were bidden by Trinity
Warden Beeper. One who was there confides to
me that Mitchell, K. 0., and Ernest Hughes sat
side by side, and, instead of talking science,
almost"fought over the exact number of runs
made in a long-forgotten match by some mutual
chum who, perhaps, at this moment may be
knocking solar systems around space in dim
meadows desolate, where his soul dwelleth. And
all the other prelectors chatted and giggled over
undergraduate memories of “shop” misdemeanors,
and no one was either wise or particularly witty.
Which suggests that a good many academic
gatherings impress us under false pretences.
On Thursday,- at Cliveden, a performance of
“ The Tyranny of Tears,” was given. The mere
acting was unexceptionable—but the audience was
funny. It was quite a large one, and every soul
wore an evening frock of some old sort, or some
new sort. There were very few men to be seen ;
but women just crowded in—coming on foot, in
cabs, in trams, and by the cheap train service that
stops in the Cliveden vicinity. In the car which
conveyed my humble self, there were 37 women.
Some were dressed in tulle and diamonds, some
were in nun’s-veiling with cheap beads. One or two
looked like unclassified articles of drawing-room
furniture in steaming plush and best Genoa—
and all were on their way to the house where the
Dowager holds her suburban court. And many
more cars came laden with the same motley
burden. The acquaintances of Janet Missus sat
as close as they could to her, and some were asked
to supper afterwards. And at last the show was
over, and the crowd in its respective gala gar-
ments faded out of the distinguished portals into
the very cold night.
In connection with the Waitara (N.S.W.)
Foundling Home appeal, there is one pleasing
feature. A man interested in the welfare of the
accidental infant sent along an invitation to
local men and women to contribute speci-
mens of their arts and crafts, if they
felt inclined, to be included in the pro-
posed exhibition and auction. Already he
has received promises, pictures, poems, and other
encouraging indications that Melbourne folk are
possessed of a fine spirit of charity, and some idea
of an all-Australian spirit. It’s about the first
time that a charity in one State has received so
much support from another. It’s to be hoped
that it will establish a happy precedent.
“ Bosna ” sends news of the mad frivolities of
Adelaide: —
Before leaving this crow-haunted State, the Butt-Rum-
fords persuaded their Adelaide friend, Mrs. Jack Fox
(whose engagement to singer Frank Robertson was lately
announced), to fix the date of the surrender of her liberty
for April so that they can be present to assist at the
sacrifice. Mr. Rumford will give the bride away, and Joy
is to be bridesmaid or train-bearer or something pic-
turesque like that.
Singer Ethel Hantke arrived from Britain by the Mon-
golia after four years of study and the gathering of many
laurels under the shadow of the throne of Edward VII.
She was formerly star pupil at our little Conservatoire,
and was chosen to warble before the Yorks at the State
concert, and upon other jewelled occasions. Then she
fled to Britain to have her voice soled and heeled at the
Roval College of Music. She will stay with us till some-
where about September, when sordid considerations of
engagements and money again called her Bull wards.
Bv persuasion, or by being smitten with a club, or by
having the dog started after them, or something, the
’Varsitv undergraduates were induced to come down oft
their indignant perch and decorate the Commemoration
this year, as of old. Last year, owing to the Counci
making certain remarks about their hilarities, they
staved away in a haughty body, had their larks else-
where, and took most of the usual audience with them.
This time, although they were present to hear the Chan-
cellor make the usual touching observations about the
authority committed to him, there were none of the
aforetime revels. The students sat in a nicely-behaved
little Sunday-school row, and preserved an ominous
silence. They looked too good to be real, and there is a
feeling that they have done something elaborate, which
hasn’t yet transpired, or are going to do something
which will break all records.
Great are the preparations going forward for the
annual Commemoration Day luncheon at Glenelg, on the
Patawalonga Creek, beside Holdfast Bay. The thing
happens every year, on December 28, the day South
Oss was proclaimed a province, somewhere hack in the
dark ages, when Hannibal still rang furiously at the front
door bell of Rome. Don’t forget that I said “ province,
please. If you attempt to call us a colony we shall get
up and have an uncivil war with you. This time, the old
women who arrived here before 1840 are to be included,
and his Ex. will come out of his summer haunt to help in
the speech-making:, and deliver a special message from
Edward VII. to the pioneers and pioneeresses.
Mr. Harry Dalton of the shekels, whose desire to slay
time has sene him on a wild motor chase across the con-
tinent from Adelaide to Port Darwin, has been heard of
beyond Alice Springs, fleeing into the wilds where white
men are not. The most sportive obstacles he has en-
countered so far are the sandhills, about 59 of which
sprang up in a distance of two miles or so. The motor
had to be towed up them by donkeys. What Mr.
Dalton is going to do with Port Darwin, if he gets it, no-
body seems to know. But the chances are that he won’t
get it, and that we’ll see him walking into Adelaide with
the remains of the motor in a carpet bag before very long.
Yours affectionately,
JOHANNA.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Judging by the cable reports the Russian
Imperial Government has just discovered that it
has lost Port Arthur and Moukden. Stoessel is
to be tried for surrendering the former, and
Baron Kaulbars is invited to attend at St. Peters-
burg and explain what he did with Moukden, and
where it is now, anyhow.
“ Snakebite ” writes from the spot :
I saw in a recent
Bulletin (17/10/’O7) a
paragraph re the
Kilmore (Vic.) railway-
accident, which stated
that the guard of the
train was killed. This
is incorrect, He was
uninjured. Anyone
seeing the train smash-
ed to matchwood in
the creek, and only the
guard’s van standing
on the rails would
come to the conclusion
that that officer was
meant to die of old age.
Took a walk last week
from Pvalong to Too-
borac along the rail-
way line, and it is
really in a shocking
condition. Some of
the sleepers are so old
and brittle that you
could smash them with
a blunt hammer into
splinters, and in a
couple of places I found
a sleeper broken where
the rail passed over it,
so that the end was
tilted up in the air.
Also, many of the fish-
plates are quite loose,
with a bolt missing in
some instances. Three
men are supposed to
keep nearly eight miles
of line in good repair.
By the majority this
long length is con-
sidered too much, even
when it is a new line
that is to be kept in
order, but when the
line is old and rotten
the idea is absurd, and
the public is only in-
viting sudden death
by putting up with it.
Meanwhile, Tait shows
a surplus.
The Tory Adelaide Register, which has
been squealing for financial honesty for a long
time, had its own honesty tried the other day, anj|
the result was only moderate. Labor Premier-
Price, though he isn’t too strenuous a no-borrower,
did put down £92,000 in the last Appropriation
Bill for works usually paid for with loan money ;
whereupon the Register howled that it was a
most improper proceeding, that taxation was
something terrible, and that the Register’s
friends, Fat and the Landlord, who own the
Legislative Council, ought to have a chance of
throwing the item out on its nose. As the
Government had tacked it on to an Appro-
priation Bill, Fat couldn’t very well get at it,
wherefore the Register cried in great pain that
the whole thing was “ unconstitooshnal.”
Arthur Morgan, a son of ex-Premier
Morgan, is opposing Grayson for Cunningham
(Q.). Grayson jumped from Philp’s vessel to
Kidston’s; but now he is back on the old derelict.
He represents a farming constituency, and is a
hard man to shift: but all over that district
Morgan is a name to conjure with.
Compulsory arbitration will soon be the
means of settling labor disputes all over the
civilised world. If Lloyd-George had not been
able to avert the threatened English railway strike
by inducing both parties to submit to an arbi-
tration court, the Brit’ f C-vernment intended
to force them into miission by Act of
Parliament.
The Victorian Bible n-schools crowd is
left again. It almost persuaded Premier Bent to
let it have another referendum on the subject,
but when it came to deciding on the form of the
question to be put to the people, Bent was almost
torn in six pieces by conflicting religionists, and
he dropped the Bill to save his life and what re-
mained of his clothes.
A Broken Hill storekeeper the other day
opened a case of imported condensed milk, and
found this note inside:—
X wish the finder of this would remember an old Aus-
tralian and send me a copy of Tiib Bulletin.
If Australia had had high enough Protection that
Australian wouldn’t be putting up foreign milk in
foreign packages to send back to his own country.
With enough Protection Australia would have
supplied itself with its own milk, and the Aus-
tralian would have been able to buy his Bulletin
as he went to work on Thursday morning. The
Broken Hill storekeeper sent on a paper to the
name and address given.
The “ Manufacturers’ Protective Asso-
ciation” at Adelaide and its employees have
held meetings to claim that the duty on agricul-
tural machinery passed by the House of Reps,
under the new tariff is inadequate to allow the
wages ordered by the New Protection to be paid.
If the manufacturers can make out that case,
Parliament should promptly remedy the defect.
Australia wants fair-wage industries. It wants
them alive, not dead, and an industry can’t keep
alive on an unprofitable basis. Some of the agri-
cultural machinery people have in the past rather
spoilt their case by blundering tactics, but that
provides no reason why they shouldn’t have a fair
deal now.
The wiping-out of the duty on kerosene in
tins means a dead loss to Australian workmen of
some £85,000 per annum, that sum representing
the wages that would have to be paid yearly if the
packing and tinning were done in Australia.
Now, £85,000 means the support of over 400
workmen at £4 a week each. With their families
and dependents they would represent, probably,
1200 people. They would support more than
another 1200 in subsidiary trades ; and these 2400
people would supply something like £24,000 a
year to the revenue. And all this population,
wages, and revenue are sacrificed for the fetich of
Cobden and the glory and profit of the bald-headed
Rockefeller.
HER UNFAIR SHARE.
MR. G. SQUID: “I see, my dear , that the men
still preponderate in Australia. There is one
man and a spare portion over for every
woman.”
MRS. S. : “ Yes. Wasn’t it just my wretched
luck to get the spare portion? ”
How Parliamentary methods change !
Nowadays, in S. Aus. especially, the under-secre-
taries and other permanent officials compile most
of the Ministers’ set speeches; the Ministers
merely bring in the typewritten screeds and
stumble through them anyhow. A few years ago
a dozen members would have jumped up to call
the Speaker’s attention to the horrible fact that
the shameless person was reading his speech ; now
nobody takes the smallest notice.
1ST SMALL BOY : “ Bet you a boh I do it.” 2ND SMALL BOY : “Bet you a bob you don’t.”
1ST SMALL BOY : “ But you ain’t got a bob.” 2ND SMALL BOY : “ And you ain’t going to do it.”
The maddest man at the meeting of Philp’s
party in Brisbane, after it was known that there
would have to be a general election, was old John
McMaster. He only just crawled in last time
through the Labor-Kidston split, and now, fearing
there will be no Labor-Kidston split in his
electorate, the ancient feels cold already. All the
warmth he had left he used up in telling Bhilp
what he thought of him for not resigning when
Parliament gave him its emphatic boot.
N.S.W. Arbitration Court award in the
boot trade is a powerful argument for the
Federal control of labor conditions. The Court
considered £2 14s. a fair minimum wage, but
could not award it, as the Victorian rate was
only £2 Bs.; and under inter-State Freetrade con-
ditions the Victorian manufacturer, with cheaper
labor, would be able to unfairly compete with the
N.S. Wales man. So £2 8s was decreed, though
confessedly not an adequate wage.
The Commonwealth Auditor-General has
no proper regard for the feelings of the defrauded,
insulted Ma State. At the tail of his report,
handed to Parliament at the close of the session,
he callously gave particulars of the defaulting
public servants-; but, alas ! instead of those “Vic-
torian wolves ” showing up as the rascals and
robbers that Ma knows they are, it seems that
dear old Ma herself is the worst offender of the
lot. She managed to raise 16 defaulters, who
annexed £lBlB os. 7d. altogether, while the wolf
only provided four, who collared a beggarly £62
17s. Bd.
A new naval power is on its way to the
Pacific. The great Yankee Armada of 18 first-
class battleships and 35 smaller vessels, with 850
big guns and 16,000 men, has left Hampton Roads
to struggle round the Horn and give a broad
hint to Japan. It is a very different Armada from
the weary herd of ships under Rozhdestvensky
which plodded miserably into the same ocean a
while ago; but both Armadas teach Australia the
same lesson—that its isolation is ended, and the
once illimitable distances are no longer a defence.
The doom of N.S.W.’s Great Zig-zag
approaches. It figures in all the pamphlets of
the N.S.W. Tourist Department as one of the
State’s “ great natural resources,” and it is also a
“permanent and reproductive work.” But it is
a costly stumbling block to traffic, and £lOO,OOO of
N.S.W.’s hard-borrowed money is to be spent on
a deviation. The Zig-zag itself was built with
borrowed money, and when it is abandoned, the
loan will still survive. It is a habit that loans
have. The oldest inhabitant isn’t a patch on the
loan for survival.
British War Minister Haldane spoke with
common-sense—and yet, considering the ostrich
policy of Great Britain in this matter, with con-
siderable audacity—when, the other day, he
warned his country that the “ two-Power standard
of naval strength could not be always main-
tained.” That is obvious to the poorest in-
telligence ; 60,000,000 Germans and 80,000,000
Americans (140,000,000 in all), when they are once
roused to a fury of naval ambition, can build a lot
more ships than 44,000,000 British folk can afford
to do. The alternative is the abandonment of
Freetrade and the breaking up of the great
estates, so that Britain can grow its own food and
won’t require a navy twice as big as that of
its next rival to protect its imported food
supplies; and universal military training, so'that
the defeat of the fleet won’t necessarily mean
the end of England. But the Campbell-Banner-
man Government isn’t the crowd to face either
emergency.
“ McClosky ” : And another thing about
the King’s head on the new Australian stamps.
The Southern Cross never dies, nor will the out-
line of our matchless Australia; and the kangaroo
and emu would be good for a hundred years. I’m
Scotch, and I want something that will last—not
a perishable king.
Westralia seems to grow madder as the
years progress, The proposed loan expenditure
this year is well Over £2,000,000 —a trifle of
about £8 for every man, woman, and infant in the
State. And already they owe about £7O each. Yet
the Treasurer says that “ the finances are quite
sound.”
Five times lately has William Wilks,
M.H.R., the infuriated Sydney Freetrader, voted
and hollered for High Protection, and each time
it has been for some industry that has its head-
quarters in his own electorate out Balmain way.
Wilks has an ideal. He would like a tariff wall
100 miles high round his own electorate, and Free-
trade for all the rest of the world. Then the
wealth of the globe would gradually concentrate
in Balmain and Annandale, and their houses
would be malachite, and their streets would be
marble, and in the centre of all this glory would
be a statue of Wilks, 100 ft. high, with the tariff in
one hand and a thunderbolt in the other.
The N.S.W. Assembly, late in the session,
passed almost unanimously a Bill providing for
the construction of a railway from Cooma to
Bombala. But the Upper House had an idea in
its aged and infirm noddle that the railway might
be a convenience to the proposed Australian
capital at Dalgety, and it was all in vain that the
Government representative explained that the
line had been carefully fixed so as to avoid
Dalgety, and that it would do no good to that
hated spot at all, and that the Ministry had been
just as nasty to the Australian Government in
this matter as it knew how. The Cooma-Bombala
Railway Bill went out on its ear by 17 votes to
eight.
“ Vulcan ” : Looks as if a “ Black Thurs-
day” is ahead of N.S.W. Education Department.
The school attendance invariably coincides with
the birth-rate six years previously. Beginning
in ’97, the birth-rate fell steadily and regularly
until ’O3, when the school attendances, reflecting
the beginning of the drop in ’97, began to fall.
This decrease has continued since, and will cul-
minate in ’O9, when, reflecting the birth-rate
minimum of ’O2, the Department expects an at-
tendance of 12,000 less than in ’O4! Just what
will happen the present teachers of the 12,000
who won’t be there in ’O9 is a matter for anxious
speculation.
“ Copper ” : I have stood on the jetty at
a S. Aus. seaport watching copper ingots (pro-
duced at the local smelting works) being shipped
to Europe, India, China, and other countries.
The ingots have been going into one hatch, whilst
sheet copper, telegraph wire, electric cable, and
general copper goods, made in America and
Europe, have been coming out of another. And
this useless dragging of things on and off a ship,
so that foreign shipowners may get the freight
money, and foreign workmen may get the wages,
and foreign manufacturers may get the profit, is
the commerce disease which our Foreign-traders
regard as the source of all true greatness.
THE BUGLES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
"What are the bugles calling for?” said Someone on
Parade.
“To turn you out; to make you men ! ” —’twas Alfred
Deakin said.
“ Why are these folks a-turnin’ white ? ” said Someone on
Parade.
“ Because they want to shirk a fight ” a passing soldier
said;
"Because they seem to scent at last a deadly day has
come
When ev’ry man, from gilded toff to poorest city scum,
Must hear the call and heed the call of yonder rolling
drum
That tells us we’re a nation in the mornin’.”
“Why is the flag a-wavin’ there?” said Someone on
Parade.
"To show you you must do and dare!” ’twas Alfred
Deakin said.
“ And who are they who murmur low ? ” said Someone on
Parade.
" The men who never face a foe ” a voice in answer said ;
“ The men who think another man should guard them
with a gun ;
The men who raise a cheap ‘Hooray!’ and think their
duty’s done;
The men who only qualify to lift a leg and run
Whene’er the bugle calls them in the mornin’.”
•‘What means this pile of warlike gear?’’said Someone
on Parade.
“It means ‘we’ve got a nation here!”’ a crowd in
answer said.
“Of that what is your guarantee?” said Someone on
Parade.
“Our sons in arms on land and sea” the noble answer
said.
"The fact we’ve dropped our baby talk of help from
other land;
The fact we’ve put our boys afloat to guard their mother
strand ;
The fact we put our single trust in native grit and sand
When the bugles call to battle in the mornin’.”

The Test match cost the Wolfe’s Schnapps Co.
£ll 18s. 6d., yet the firm, as a firm, does not bet. Simply
the Wolfe’s people decided to give 6d. to each Australian
batsman for every run over 50 scored by him ; also £5 to
the bowler who got the first five wickets, and 10s. to who-
ever caught an Englishman out. Thus Noble scored
catches to the tune of 30s. ; Hazlitt and Armstrong snared
a sovereign’s worth apiece, and Trumper, Carter and
McAlister caught 10s. each—one catch per man. Hill hit
up £llBs. 6d. worth of runs, and Cotter pocketed five
wickets—at one golden quid per wicket. Wolfe’s scheme
mayn’t be cricket, but it’s an excellent one for cricketers,
all the same.*
