NOTIONS
“Trumper is the champagne of cricket”
(Limerick): —
But a great wine can go flat,
And the keenest eye grow dull-edged.
Trumper fizz is mostly good ;
Last week’s lot was badly ullaged.
N.S.W. 10-2-4.
* * * *
The rain which compelled adjournment during
the afternoon of the fourth day of the Austral-English
test cricket match, was even more pronounced during
the forenoon of Wednesday. Umpires and rival captains
several times walked gravely over the sloppy turf to the
oval’s centre, looked serious, and pondered heavily, and
came back. Then word passed round that a resumption
could not be yet, and Association members lubricated
still further and told of mighty things they had seen and
done in cricket. The 77th test was continued on Thurs-
day, and a big majority of 3500 spectators paid £l2O
just to see the finish, as success for England was
as near certain as anything could be. Cricket-
ing cranks who stayed at home have been kick-
ing themselves hard ever since. Test cricket never
knew a closer finish as far as wickets in hand were
concerned, excepting in London five years ago, when
England won. The question whether the sun would
come out strong and “cake” the wicket, and thus give
the home side completely over to the enemy, worried a
considerable section of Sydney ; but the sun 'kept accom-
modatingly hidden, and the pitch dried slowly and picked
up remarkably. It was either side’s game right to the
moment the winning runs were notched, for anything
might have happened at the eleventh hour. Cotter and
Hazlitt —the last two together—carried all the responsi-
bility, and never staggered under the load once. Cotter’s
tight rein on himself was a revelation, and colt Hazlitt
shaped like a veteran.
Noble’s was an unlucky “out.” At 27 he let.
Barnes’ first delivery just shave the outer edge of the
wicket and the bail’s fall was a second-thought act, as the
ball had gone a noticeable time before. Armstrong’s dis-
missal came when he had 44, huilt up in two hours.
Though there were five fours in the compilation, the bats-
man lacked force. Half the side had gone, leaving the
tail to cope with the situation. Ransford ran up 13, and
then scooped the easiest of catches into Blythe’s hands.
The “ off theory ” was to McAlister as the light
to the moth, despite forcible urgings from the crowd
to “ let ’em alone,” and Crawford’s second ball
displaced the off stump. There were six fours and three
threesin his 41, accumulated in 1 hour 26min. It came
at a ticklish time, through really good play. Little of a
genuinely sticking-up nature could be detected in the
bowling; on the contrary, it was soft, though the
wicket’s condition allowed of possibilities. Trundlers
Blythe, Barnes, and Rhodes failed to do anything near
what might reasonably have been looked for, and what
they did bag was easily gathered. Six had strutted
and fretted for 124, and a seventh saw 185 as he
retraced his steps. Carter supplied the champagne
of the match as far as his innings went; he tacked fours
to the score immediately, and played magnificent cricket
all through. Two hundred appeared in 2hrs. 56min.
Carter had run up 40 in an hour, and had added another
thirteen eleven minutes later. Six minutes further on
he was with the majority of the side—caught by Young,
standing back from Fielder. Carter’s contribution
reached £>l, inclusive of eight fours, and the crowd felt
better. Hazlitt and Cotter had to face the stupendous
(in the circumstances) task of making 56, and proved
equal to it. Each had 26 when, at the tea adjournment,
the total was 260. For long spasms of excitement were fre-
quent, and thousands swayed involuntarily with every
move of the batsmen, and vented roars of relief as each
extra tricky one failed in its mission. The winning hit was
Hazhtt’s. He sent Fielder to leg fence for four ; and the
match, which will be food for pavilion talk and newspaper
reference for many years, ended. Australia’s advantage
was two wickets and two runs. Evidence of the thorough-
ness of Cotter’s stonewalling is that he was there lhr. 6m.
for 33 runs, and only one four appeared in the list. In
39 minutes Hazlitt made 34 runs, of which six were fours.
* * * *
Hazlitt, who, with Cotter, was the hero of Aus-
tralia’s cricket victory last week—-the first time for many
years that Australia has won an opening test match in
Sydney—is only 19 years of age, and yet he is not the
youngest Australian player that has figured in a similar
engagement. When T. W. Garrett fought for his country
against the invading Lilywhite combination, 30 years
ago, he was just ISJ.
* * * *
George Gunn, who figured so prominently in
recent test match, must not be confused with William
Gunn, one of a British team in Australia years ago. The
Gunn that played such havoc with Australia’s forces last
week is a nephew of the obsolete Gunn.
* * « *
“Ancient”: Old-time cricket team manager
Charlie Beale says the finish of last week’s test recalled
that in England in 1882, when Australia won by seven
runs.. The struggle was so intensely exciting that a man
dropped dead, scoring card and pencil in hand, and a bet
of £lOO to 1 was laid against Australia. The Britishers
were at the wickets, and seven of them had to get 19, but
they only made 12.
* * * *
“ 0.K.” : The batting of poor Gunn, the
invalid, in the First Test, suggests that the next
Australian Eleven to go to the country that
lends us money should be selected by a com-
mittee of medical men on account of their delicate
health. The English team included 14 robust members
and Gunn, who came out for the voyage and the rest, and
was understood to be chiefly interested in taking care of
himself. As soon as they got to Australia the able-
bodied men started to play themselves into “form,”
whilst Gunn seemingly continued to play for safety, with
brilliant success. By the time the Test match came due,
the English captain was lying in the hospital at Brisbane,
and two professionals—Hobbes and Hayes—were so
obviously out of “ form ” that the deputy-captain brought
the sick Gunn into action. His batting average for the
two innings was 96, the second best average being
Hardstaff’s 37. If Gunn retains his weak state of health
until the next Test, and Captain Jones crawls out of
hospital to take part in it, and the others put themselves
in the doctors’ hands, the Englishmen should be favorites
at 6 to 4 on.
* * * *
Fane, the acting captain for the Englishmen,
was unlucky in his selection of amateur Young in prefer-
ence to professional Humphries for the wicket-keeping
billet. According to the English averages for last season,
Young is a vastly better batsman than Humphries, but in
the Test he failed very badly. The other three amateurs
failed less badly.
Judged by the figures to their credit in the Cold
Country, the most dangerous men in the visiting team
are Hobbes and Hayes with the bat, and Blythe and
Rhodes with the hall. Australian facts have belied Eng-
lish figures thus far. Figures, in fact, are not “ form.”
* * * *
After all the talk about the fine influence of
athletic sport in knocking out the stupid tuft-hunting
business, note how we grovel to anything that can be
even remotely suspected of owning a title. First there
was “Prince” Ranji, who was no more “prince ” than
Joe Darling is—he was merely the adopted heir of a petty
Indian rajah, who is again merely a big landowner, like
Joe Darling’s father was. Now there come the Fijians,
and it is “ Prince liadavu will be captain,” and “Prince”
Somebody did something at silly point; and, after all,
they are at most “ Ratu,” which means chief of a tribe,
all the same as our own “ King ” Billy of Bungaroo.
Frjis braves encountered N.S.W.’s military-
cricket champions last week on their own battle ground
at Victoria Barracks, and completely routed the enemy
in short order (140 to 72). The result was mainly
through Ratu Pope’s bowling ; eight wickets fell to him
for 24, and he also topped all scores of the match with 47
—including a remarkable “sixer.” At Wollongong, a
day or two previously, Ratu accounted for a ' similar
number of batsmen.
* * * *
“Stumps”: Apropos bowling averages. How’s
this for a record? In the Western Suburbs (Sydney)
Church Association matches, left-handed Duncan Middle-
ton. for St. Peter’s, opposed to Lewisham, the other day,
scattered nine wickets, all for “ducks,” in the initial
innings, and got five down in the next innings for 14. As
neither of the first two in the second innings produced a
run, Middleton had. 11 consecutive wickets to his credit
for nil. How is that for muscular Christianity?
***■*'
The decision of the Victorian State school cricket
premierships, last week, saw some remarkable bowling.
For St, Hilda against Prahran, Annisch bagged seven
wickets for nine and five for four. Eicke got two for two
and five for three, and Prahran trundler Turner got five
for 19. North v. South Williamstown match witnessed
something almost as remarkable. For North, Box’s record
was six for seven and three for six ; and Mitchell’s four
for five and five for three. In South’s interests Glad-
stone secured five for 11 and three for three, and Arnold
five for nine and six for 15. South Yarra’s defeat of
Elsternwick by an innings and 14 runs was brought about
mainly through Bishop’s nine for 12 and Sloggett’s eight
for nine. Sloggett is a good namefor the fellow who puts
up such a performance.
* * * *
Sydney Prince Alfred Hospital match last
week supplied something extra in bowling. Dr. C. V.
Bowker, opposed to a good batting combination, gathered
six wickets for 13.
Sydney Grammar School boys defeated Mel-
bourne 0. of E. Grammar School in the annual inter-
state schools’ match, after a vigorous game. The Sydney’
boy’s scored only 148 in the fii'st innings, to which Mel-
bourne answered with 225 (Lowers 106). Then the Sydney
boys waded in, and piled up 464 (Gregg 180, and Green
75), and Melbourne, with 388 to win, put up a big effort
with 359. Melbourne’s crack schoolboy’ (Lewers) scored
106 and 98 ; and the Sydney boy’s are principally thankful
to Barbour for a fine bowling performance of six wickets
for 42 runs.
W. Whittv, chosen in the second-rate N.S.W.
team v. Queensland, is a left-hand bowler of good style
and promise, and seems likely to make a mark in bigger
cricket. It is stated that he is in a list of promising
Australian players who are likely to be offered a trip with
the returning Englishmen to qualify for an English
county team.
* * * •
The A. J.C. meeting at Kandwick on Saturday
supplied no great contribution to the gaiety of nations.
The Trial Handicap was won by the favorite Rose Linnet,
on whom the lavish Christian Moses laid 3 to 1
-—and there were 14 starters. This lavishness on
the part of Moses will yet be his undoing. About
here the luck of the punters left off." In the
Villiers Stakes 14 also started, and the public planked its
scanty winnings on Garches. Patrick Aarons, broken-
hearted at his paying out, only offered 6 to 4
against Garches. All the public got for its money
was an enquiry into the running of the animal,
and even that was explained satisfactorily.
The reason why the 6 to 4 beast wasn’t placed, and
perished miserably away at the back part of events, was
simply, it seems, that the other horses ran faster than
it did. Kyeadgerie, a friendless moke on which
12 or 15 or 20 to 1 was going begging, came in
first; the 10 to 1 Luciana was second, and
Banzai (8 to 1) was third. For the Nursery Handicap (14
starters again) two animals (Safrano and Vervain) went
out at 3 to I—any honest tote would probably have made
them 10 to I—and the fact that Safrano was an indifferent
third was the only reward the punters got. Little Lad, a 10
to 1 show, after running well all the way, gathered in the
prize with comparative ease. For the Grand Stand
Stakes things were a shade better. Gaby (5 to 2)
only got third place; but Artful, the winner, had at
least the credit of being second favorite at 3 to
1. For the Holiday Handicap there were once
more 14 starters, and Alexander Solomons, the bookie,
had the front to reckon 5 to 4 against Royal Star a fair
thing. That constellation came in a poor second, and
the 6 to 1 Electron won easily. Finally there were a
dozen starters in the High-Weight Handicap, and
Karanaud, which didn’t even get a place, went out at 2
to 1.. Goldlock, which made a worse attempt than
Karanaud at getting a place, left the post on a 3 to 1
basis.' Neith, asto 1 show, took the prize. As an essay
on the folly of trying to pick winners it was a great day.
* * * *
There is some trouble between Victorian and
N.S.W. bowlers over the length of a bowling green. In
N.S.W. the turf is 120 ft. long, while in Victoria it runs
to 165 ft. N.S.W. says: “Make your greens shorter.”
Victoria says : “ Get a little more muscle and plug your
bowls a bit further.” N.S.W. says there is no scientific
play in long heads, and Victoria says there is too much
fireworks in short heads. Once before, on a very hot
night, delegates from N.S.W. met delegates from Victoria
to discuss this question, but one of the Victorian repre-
sentatives talked so long and rang the bell so often that
after an hour or two nobod}’ knew whether it was long
sleevers or long greens or short heads or short whiskies
and sodas they were discussing.
* * *
The latest billiard foolishness in London is an
eight-sided table. Weiss, who seems to pick up any new
idea quicker than his rivals, promptly beat Peall on it,
easily and twice, as he beat Roberts on the oval table.
After an interval for rest and reflection, pro-
moters of the slogging game pulled off another match in
Melbourne last week, when Ruenalf, the Maori showman,
and Alf Russell, the Sydneyside heavyweight, faced each
other in the Athletic Club’s room. It was little more
than a stoushing match, in which Ruenalf was the un-
grateful recipient or beneficiary. The Maori used to
look something like a fighter, but on this oc-
casion he resembled the has-been who never was,
and showed up very poorly indeed against Russell,
who, although a big, strong, hefty lad, is as
slow as a god with feet of clay, and couldn’t get
out after a ton of lard on a cold day. The scrap went
eight rounds ; then the much-damaged Ruenalf knocked
off suddenly, having recollected something to say.
Ruenalf, with the true showman’s instinct, would stop in
the middle of a burning house to make a speech. His
oration was something to the effect that Russell had
beaten him fairly, but it wouldn’t have happened so if
things had been entirely different, or if something had
been otherwise.
* * * *
N.S.W.’s quest for a pug heavyweight to fill
the space left by the passing of Peter Jackson, Bob Fitz-
simmons, Paddy Slavin, and Joe Goddard has attracted
considerable good material, but there’s no one—no Peter
Jackson or Larry Foley (the latter is apparently out of the
teaching business)—to mould it into shape. At least
eight men, physically and in other essentials thoroughly
equipped but lamentably short of skill, are offering-, and
will be tried out by promoters, and the fun enjoyed by
their patrons. Nothing excites or amuses so much as a
mill between grimly determined big ’prentice men.
• * * *
Manilla (N.S.W.) plate-layer, Jack Ross, and
Cobar (N.S.W.) shearer, George Stirling, figured in a wild
whirl at Sydney Gaiety A.O. Tuesday last week. More
clash and bump occurred during their three rounds than
is seen in some allegedly stirring 20-rounds battles. Each
of two strong gritty men did all he could to out-thump the
other. Blows, fiercely hurled, made big holes in the
atmosphere, and frequently brought the deliverer into
collision with posts and ropes, when great entangle-
ments occurred. While cool, Stirling had a decided
advantage ; but when something extra vimful from the
other side fired him up, one was as good as the other, and
the affair then became a mad skurry. By-and-bye Stirling
completely outforced the man from Manilla, and a couple
blows placed him beyond rising (try how he
mi Sht;, till too late. Stirling- now aspires to fight Felix
or Sid Russell, a jump to the top—though it is a poor
top—that caterers do not approve. They want Stirling
to step over the bodies of the other novice heavies first—
if he can.

* * * •*
Curl’ : Disagree with “Billy” Jordan, who
says there are only four punches any real good to a fight-
ing-man (B. 5/12/’O7). Of course the straight left is not an
actual knock-out blow, but a well-delivered one of that
sort has helped to settle many an argument, professional
and amateur. I should put it second on the list.
* * * *
A dreadful, dreary thing is to begin to happen
at Sydney on December 30. On that date, at Sydney
Tivoli, Tom Burrows, the champion club-swinger of the
world, commences an attempt to wave two pieces of
wood, without stopping, for 61 hours and 20 minutes.
And when he leaves off he won’t have even torn the
atmosphere ; the places where he split it with his imple-
ments will have healed up.
* * * *
A one-legged cycle championship figures on the
programme of the Christchurch (M.L.) cycling club’s
Anniversary Day meeting. Somehow the one-legged
seem to take particular delight in riding the wobbly
wheel. For two years past a Sydney High School boy
of this variety has shone in the wheel-races at the annual
school sports. At an Adelaide meeting, too, a while ago,
three one-leg riders foregathered, and Broken Hill has a
seasoned one-legged cycling competitor.
* * * *
The latest development of the Dunlop Pneu-
matic Co. is the detachable motor rim, which will do away
with a whole sea of trouble for the wandering motorist.
The new perfected detachable rim is simplicity itself,
and is so easily manipulated that a complete cover tube
and rim have been taken off and put on seven times in
one minute. This means that the spare tyres will be
carried ready inflated on their rims, and when the
puncture occurs, the tyre in use can be replaced by a
spare one in much less than half a minute. The device is
so simple as to be almost fool-proof, and so perfectly
secure under all conditions that it is practically safe
to hazard the prophecy that in 12 months’ time there
won’t be a car running without it.
* * * *
The saucer cycling track at Prince’s Court
(Melb.) has smashed all records thereabouts in quick
construction ; three weeks from the beginning of the
work saw wheelists taking the side-of-a-house-like bank.
As a saucer it has been misnamed; in the way of
crockery it resembles a gigantic pie-dish with the long
sides depressed. The real saucer was presented by the
Lottos, whom Geo. Musgrove imported years ago. The
pie-disb is a sample of what the Australian workman can
do, and is voted by the knowing ones as ahead of any-
thing else on earth. If the dish doesn’t knock ’em—well,
novelties are a drug in the Australian market, or the
smell of cycling schemes has overpowered the public.
* * * *
“0.K.”: After long years I renewed my old
acquaintance with Melbourne Cricket Club ground as a
show place for bike-racing— i.e., a “pitch” for the
Austral Meeting. Came to the conclusion that the place
is all right, and the show is even weaker than the times
when Bill Martin and Co. had something of the bloom of
novelty upon them. The stage management Was ama-
teurish then. It is amateurish now. One pays 6d. for a
racebook to get an idea of what is going on, but the
book is of little assistance to a casual reader, for the reason
that the riders, or rather their bikes, are not numbered on
a reasonable plan. You find that, peril ips, the scratch
man in the first heat of a handicap race ' - numbered 999,
and the limit man is No. 3, and when t o final heat is
arrived at the scoring, which is the only source of infor-
mation re the identity of the starters, presents a jumbled
array of numbers something like thi5—9925617893420, the
punctuation being fitful and bewildering. The village
idiot at an English rural cricket match could
rig up a better scoring board. Moreover, the
board is not even readable, as a rule, by the
people in the M.C.G. members’ stand. The members
are “deadheads,” and the M.B.C. naturally orders the
board’s broadsides to be turned almost always towards
the paying public in the grand stand ; but this arrange-
ment comes rough upon a number of shilling people who
face the smokers’ pavilion from (he opposite side of the
ground. Apart from the crudity and two-sided policy of
the “revolving” scoring board, the old conservative
system of numbering the competitors before the heats
are drawn is a positive grievance. Competitors for each
handicap should be numbered in rotation. If 100 riders
are portioned off into 10 heats of 10, the scratch
man of the first heat should be No. 1, and the
limit man of the 10th heat should carry No. 100
at the rear of his machine. And in the case
of the Austral where all the heats are run
off on the first day, and only the final is left for the last
day, the competitors should be numbered from 1 to 14 on
the programme, so that the audience could tell at a
glance how the men were going, according to their han-
dicap terms. In short, a bicycle race card should be as
intelligible as a horse-race card, and the cards fastened to
the riders or their vehicles should be given to them
before every race, as numbered saddle-cloths are given to
jockeys. After 15 years’ experience of the show business,
the biking managers have not yet learned the simplest
method of numbering the competitors! And in the
matter of engaging a number of little girls to release a
lot of little pigeons from hampers, the M.B.C. has a lot
to learn.
* * * *
Sculler Dick Arnst has left for his home in
Maoriland in a very bad mood. Less than 12 months ago
Arnst was a raw sculler, with a stout heart and big inten-
tions of some day becoming a world’s champion. The
Maorilander has beaten the best Australians in handicaps,
and thereby completely scared away all chances of a side-
wager contest, and is about the most respected and the
most unemployed athlete in the country. However, Arnst
intends to be on hand to challenge the winner of the race
between Webb and Tresidder, and he deserves a match
for his persistency.
* * * *
When woman’s back is up she gives no quarter.
The recently-formed N.S.W. Ladies’ Amateur Swimming
Association came down heavily on four of its members and
disqualified them for twelve months because, though
completely costumed, they showed before men. Said
men were the bath-keeper, a journalist, and a photo-
grapher—the last engaged in taking pictures for a Syd-
ney weekly. Sinful as this “ offence ” was rated, Thf.
Bulletin is curious to know how much more heinous
is that of the large number of the disqualified ones’ sister-
members, who go down to beaches and bathe among
and before hundreds of the other sex. The woman,
surf swimmer is often far more scantily clad than thP
bath performer, whose upper attire covers all that the
theatre lady leaves exposed without a blush.
*** * -
The 220 yd. breast swimming championship
of N.S.W., at Pyrmont Club Carnival Wednesday night
last week, provided a high-strung finish. Ten yards from
the end the young South Sea Islander, Alick Wickham,
seemed to own the title, but there G. Snell (Mosman) and
1. Cantor (East Sydney) brought every ounce into service.
Keck and neck the trio travelled, and, just as Cantor was
stretching to touch, Wickham and Snell made the
necessary contact. The judges placed Snell 1, Wickham
2, and Cantor 3. As the winner's time was 3min.
44 2-ssec. possibly all three got within standard—3.4s.
* * * *
The surprise of N.S.W. swimming so far this
season was Dick Cavill’s successful cut at the world’s
hundred” professional record (61 l-ssec.) in Pyrmont
(Sydney) baths Wednesday night last. Curiosity as to
how much of his once high-class form Dick had retained
after years of compulsory (through becoming a pro-
fessional) abstention from racing, prompted consider-
able interest; but only those who knew were prepared
for what happened. Swimming strongly all the way,
Cavill accomplished his task, beating the figures assailed
by J sec *» and, but for faulty turning, he would un-
doubtedly have broken the minute, which no other swim-
mer knovn to the world—bar record-holder E. M. Daniels
(America and his great rival Cecil Healy (N.S.W.)—
be relied upon to do to-day. Amateurism deplores
Dick Cavill s “ going over ” ; so fine an all-round swimmer
is badly needed in Australia just now.
* * ■* *
“ Hootan,” writing with the last sniff of the de-
parting motor in his nostrils: Motorists Dutton and
Aunger, who are trying to get through to Port Darwin
from Adelaide, will have a pretty easy go as far as Ma-
cumba (one of Kidman’s stations, 35 miles out from
Oodnadatta), but just there they will strike their first pint
of trouble. They will have five miles of sand to go on
with. Then almost to Old Grown Point station, 35
miles north of Charlotte Waters, the going is pretty
good, except for gibbers. The worst patch is
between Plamilton borejon the Stevenson) and Blood’s
Creek, with a further bad three miles on the Adminga
River. To get off the beaten track would be to court
disaster, as the surface is gibber-strewn for miles, while
to stick to the track, which consists of two parallel gut-
ters, spoke-deep, may mean wrecking the under gear.
Nearing Old Crown Point station the motor will have to
face a mile of sand in the Finke River. At Horse Shoe
Bend the Finke has to be re-crossed. There is a mile of
sand again, and an awful mile it is. Then, after two miles,
the Depbt Sand-hills are reached, and they furnish 12
miles of the worst track in Australia. The sand is of the
hour-glass variety, and the wheels of coach or waggon
sink nearly nave-deep into it. The undergear
will plough this and, I believe, it will effectually
bog the chariot. The northern face of these ridges
is invariably precipitous, and so sudden is the
dip that the gear must bog itself in the crest. This
sand on a still day floats buggy-high. Leaving Depot
Well, the road crosses the Hugh River six times in 12
miles—moresand. Then, after a few mixed patches, comes
a villainous stretch; from the crest of each successive ridge
of sand (the ridges are about a mile and a half apart)
the heart-sick traveller can sight another crest,
and over these hills of horror the line of telegraph
poles stretches without a turn for more than 40 miles.
Then comes a good run of 12 miles to near Heavitree Gap
(Todd River), and you can rest in Alice Springs gaol, two
miles south of the township (Stuart); two miles further
north again is Alice Springs Telegraph Station. The sand
here is as fine as flour, and lies knee deep at most times.
The Mac Donnell Ranges follow—gibberv, but not im-
passable ; and after that—if there is any after that—a
comparatively easy task is before the motormen.

Died t’other day the once famous Maoriland
footballer, Alf Bayley, who skippered a team in Australia
during the late ’nineties. ’Twas said that poor Baylev
never really recovered from the shock of Barney Armit’s
death, the result of a palpably unfortunate accident
caused through Bayley tackling. Bayley was one of a
M. family rich in brilliant exponents of Rugby. He
had seen 41 years.
* * * *
Died, last week, aged only 21, John L. Ground-
water, one of the best Rugby footballers N.S.W. has
turned out for many years. Groundwater, who was a
medical student at Sydney University, represented
N. against the Maoriland team, and also against
Queensland, last season.

When pondering’ over the presents you are going to buy, and while thinking and wondering how
much money you are going to spend on perishable Christmas presents, stop and realise NATURE’S
GREATEST GIFT OF ALL—“ THE SENSE OF SOUND ’’—the sense that binds us with hooks of steel
to our fellows —the sense that means EVERYTHING to us.
In many instances the loss of hearing is a gradual and painless process. No pronounced warning of
the awful affliction is given you. At other times the evidences are extremely acute. Shooting pains,
dizziness, buzzing, ringing, and noises or discharge in the ears all testify to the dangerous condition of
your ears. Do you suffer this way ?
Think what it means to be deaf. To live for ever in a tomb of silence is an awful affliction. The
voices of friends, the melody, the intercourse of life will be yours no longer. Why not accept our offer
to be cured—permanently cured right at your own home without any interference to daily work ?
It makes no difference what stage you are in or the cause of your affliction. There is a sure hope
for every unfortunate sufferer.

“Wusslean”: Isn’t it ever the 24th of
December —and Christmas Eve—anywhere but in
England ? That exiled child of the Family
Hebald, the Austbalian Jouenal, publishes a
Christmas number, and almost all its fearsome
yarns begin with the eve of Christmas, and the
bitter north wind beats its load of sleet and snow
mercilessly down upon the brave old manor, while
inside the Yule log burns merrily, etc. Can’t it
be for once in an Australian Journal the “eve
of Christmas” or at least the 24th December
somewhere else, and can’t it be 104° in the shade,
and very little shade; and can’t the north wind
pile its dust mercilessly against the brave old
wire-fence on the road to God-forgotten which
lies near the Devil on the by-way to Dry-bones
and Insolvency ?
It is seriously proposed to disturb the
bones of long-dead Governor Phillip, and bring
them to Australia to be planted under his statue
in Sydney Botanic Gardens. If there were any
hope of the proceedings involving the utter de-
struction of the statue, the otherwise senseless
proposal would deserve support. The Phillip
monument was among Henry Parkes’ jobs; it
was perpetrated by the late Simonetti, and is one
of the ugliest bits of statuary planted on one of
the fairest spots in Australia.

FULL CONCERT GRAND, recently exhibited at Federal Parliament House. Price 275 Guineas.
THis Piano was. like all BEALE PIANOS, made ENTIRELY IN AUSTRALIA
WITH THE EXCEPTION OF ONLY ONE PART.
'HE whole CABINET WORK, every detail of it, was made in our own factories in
Australia.
The whole of the IRON FRAME and all other IRON CASTINGS were made
from Australian (Lithgow) iron in our own factories in Australia.
The SCALE was calculated and drawn out on plan entirely in our own factories in Aus-
tralia. This is the most difficult and technical item in piano manufacture.
The whole SOUNDING-BOARD was made entirely in our own factories in Australia.
All the BRONZE, GUN-METAL, BRASS and ALUMINIUM CASTINGS were
made entirely in our own factories in Australia.
The SCREW STUDS, ACTION BOLTS and NUTS were made entirely in our own
factories in Australia.
The PEDALS were made entirely in our own factories in Australia.
The BALL-BEARING CASTORS were made entirely in our own factories in Aus-
tralia out of Australian metals.
The KEYBOARD was made, according to the SCALE, entirely in our own factories in
Australia. ...
The CARVINGS were designed and executed in our own factories in Australia.
The SOLID IRON WREST-PLANK was invented by us in Australia, and patented
throughout the world.
The patented WREST-PINS for the patent tuning-system were made on an automatic
machine entirely in oar own factories in Australia.
The AUTOMATIC MACHINES for turning, squaring and drilling the patented wrest-
pins were designed and made entirely in our own factories in Australia.
The SPINNING MACHINES, for winding the bass strings with copper and iron wire,
were designed and made by us in our own factories in Australia.
The AGRAFFE STUDS (which in horizontal pianos take the place of the pressure-bars
in uprights) were made entirely in our own factories in Australia.
The AUTOMATIC MACHINE, which made the agraffe studs, was designed and made
in our own factories in Australia. This machine can also be adapted to a variety of
other work.
The PRESSES used in % making of the TOP and SIDES, including the bending of the
sides, were designed and made entirely in our own factories.
The Improved HINGES for the top (whereby a moulding may be had on all four sides,
an impossibility with imported horizontal pianos) were made entirely in our own factories in
Australia. .;
Even the LOCK AND KEY for the piano were made entirely in our own factories in
Australia.
The only part of this instrument which was not made entirely in our own factories is the Action (the mechanism which causes the hammers
to strike the strings when the keys are played on). The action was made by the action-maker admitted to be the best in the world, whose
actions are used throughout the world only in the most expensive instruments. The style used by us is the very latest double-repetition.
No other Piano Manufacturer in the World accomplishes such a wide range of work.
INASMUCH as wood-screws and steel and copper-wire are not made in Australia,
Nettlefold’s screws were used by us, and the wire was imported; but even then the
copper-wire, as above stated, was wound on to the steel in our own factories, on
machines designed and made by us.
This instrument is of the highest possible grade, unequalled in respect of the tuning-
system —whereby the durability of the instrument is greatly extended —and it is not surpassed
in any other direction by any instrument in the world.
A remarkable evenness of tone is obtained by the unique construction of the frame. We
have retained the usual resistance-bars (the trusses which are intended to give rigidity to the
frame under strain of the wires), but they are so constructed as to obviate the necessity of
cutting the fibres of the wood in the sounding-board bridge. This interference with the
bridge has been the cause of the dulness of some nates observable in many
pianos, both upright and horizontal. The result ob our improvement, coupled with a scale
which has been calculated cowling to the ascertained laws of harmoni'cs, is that our piano
has a magnificent tone which has been greatly admired.
Placed sidg by side in Parliament House with a foreign-made horizontal grand, the latter
was so hopelessly outclassed, although of a leading Germaji make, that the importers
removed it next day. The construction, finish and tone of the foreign instrument were
plainly inferior. To place our pianos side by side with those of any good maker is the
opportunity we always desire.
TWENTY-FIVE YEARS’ GUARANTEE
BEALE COMPANY LIMITED

% Steamshin Lines
HEN the tourist on
any of the liners of
the Five Companies
approaches Sydney
Heads his first view
is a gigantic gate-
way of grey, worn rock. The passage is not
much more than half a mile across, and from
the ocean side all that is visible is the towering
lighthouse, high on the cliffs overhead, a flagstaff
set in the midst of a patch of green, a yellow-roofed
house or two, and—Desolation.
Inside the Heads the scene is vastly different.
On the right lies the pleasant watering village of
Manly ; on the left the suburb of Watson’s Bay.
The Harbor widens out, and the liner glides past a
red lightship, and on the right in the distance can be
seen a long stretch of white sand known as Balmoral
Beach, beyond which lies the entrance to one of the
most picturesque pieces of water in the world, the
Middle Harbor, where the water winds in and out
between the ridges of the hills that dip down sheer
hundreds of feet into the mirror-like water. But of
Middle Harbor more anon. Port Jackson has a coast
line of 225 miles, and flowing into it are the Parra-
matta and Lane Cove Rivers, one of which is a scrap
of Elysium and the other a fragment of Paradise.
But they lie off the track of our liner. Although we
have practically entered the oldest city in Australasia,
the long succession of heads and capes are all verdure
clad. Great masses of red-tiled houses surmount the
heights, and it is not till Cremorne is passed that the
houses begin to abut on to the foreshores. And
then there arises from the water a forest of masts
and a wilderness of funnels. The Imperial Naval
Station at Garden Island, the beautiful wilderness
around Lady Macquarie’s Chair, and the castle known
as Commonwealth Government House, with its
square keep of grey old stone. Then the liner glides
round Dawes Point, and finds a berth at one of the
many wharves belonging to the Five Companies.
<52?
Once in Sydney the question is not what to do,
but how to get through the programme of sight-seeing
in the time. A man or party might spend six months
wandering round the beauty spots of this lovely
place and then go away without having seen them
all. However, we will start the excursion by a
motor trip round the Harbor. Motors are
here in hundreds, and the keenness of the
competition makes it possible for a
party to secure one for either the day
or the week very
reasonably. Leav-
ing Fort Mac-
quarie Steps
the launch
will run
round Farm
Cove where
the warships
lie anchored
“ like paint-
ed ships up-
on a painted
sea,” with
the magnifi-
cent Palace
and Botanic
Gardens and Government House Demesne all
in view. Past Lady Macquarie’s Chair, across
the entrance of Woolloomooloo Bay, full of
great cargo liners, gigantic unwieldly looking
monsters, to Potts Point. Here the scene changes
immediately. The Homes of the Mighty look down
from terraced gardens—great castle-like structures,
till Elizabath Bay, where even more palatial estab-
lishments overlook the water. Rushcutters Bay is
seen full of dancing yachts and rocking motor boats,
whose owners are toiling in the city. Up on the
ridge above there, in the dark days of the p.ast, a
party of convict rush-cutters mutinied, overcame the
soldiers, and captured their overseer, who was a par-
ticularly unlovable gentleman. The soldiers they tied to
trees, knowing they would be released before long,
and did them no further injury. The overseer they
took off into the bush with them. He was never seen
again, but 40 years afterwards some boys, exploring
what was still bush, found a skeleton pegged out on
an abandoned anthill. His buttons suggested the
skeleton had held a position of some authority.
The mutineers rowed out to a schooner in the
Harbor, boarded her, and bolted. A corvette
went after the runaway, but it was hull-down
at nightfall, and was never seen again. Leaving
the scene of this rather grisly romance, the launch
will pass a succession of bays without any
special interest. All of them are overlooked by beau-
tiful homes, and everywhere the inevitable motor
launch, canvased over, rocks on the wavelets. Bay
and cape follow in rapid succession till the launch
strikes across the Harbor and he ids for the white sand
of Balmoral Beach, but leaves it well on the left, and
glides away to what looks like the head of a cul de
sac. But there’s a channel, across which a punt
takes passengers and vehicle's. That is The Spit, and
when we round it we are in Middle Harbor. There
the bell bird calls, the jackass laughs, and the
blue hills float dreamily in the sleepy sunlit
water. But Middle Harbor is beyond description.
Then a day can be put in exploring the Parramatta
and Lane Cove Rivers. Cook and Sons send out
Touring Motor Cars, which make splendid runs over
50 miles of Sydney environment for a modest ss. per
head. Eighteen miles away on the South Coast line lies
National Park, on Port Hacking River, where more
motor boats can be obtained and visits made to the
hatcheries at Worumbla, where the art and
practice of breeding fish can be seen in full
blast, and is a most interesting sight. The Port
Hacking River is dammed, and above the weir one
can only travel in a rowing-boat, and a couple of de-
lightful days can be spent paddling up and down.
All birds and animals are protected in the Park, and
are fairlv tame. Kangaroos, wallabies, and emus
make friendly advances, and in the evenings’possums
stare in a half-timid half-Pd-like-to-trust-you-way
from the branches. An Accommodation House in
the Park provides hampers and accommodation.
The other National Park, known as Kuringai
Chase, lies on the Northern coast. It is on
the sea coast, and there are great caves and
mighty rock arches through which the rising
tide booms magnificently. And among the hills and
valleys of Kuringai Chase are to be found the
finest samples of the beautiful Australian wild
flowers. While exploring Kuringai, the best plan is
to stay at Hornsby, a suburb eight miles north of
Sydney, a place which is inordinately proud of
having an extinct volcano. Then there are well-
known places like Pittwater, Narrabeen, Rocklily
outside of Manly ; Lady Robinson’s Beach, Kurnell,
La Perouse, and others along the shores of Botany
Bay, and a score of others too numerous to mention
in a brief sketch like this.
The finest sight of all is, of course, the far-fanied
Jenolan Caves at Mt. Victoria. A delightful trip in
a motor-car service will land the tourist at Cave
House in the evening, and he will wake, curiously
braced by the exhilarating mountain air, for the ex-
ploration of these wonderful caves —admittedly the
most wonderful and diversified in the world. Of
course a guide is necessary. The tourist is
taken from cave to cave. Some represent
vast cathedrals all in glistening white, in
others the lights are flashed back with an infernal
glory. Over winding staircases, and through wilder-
nesses of stalagmite and stalactite the wanderer
gropes on till he comes to the Infernal River, which
will scald his hands, and Bom which the guide will
net a few of the fish with which it teems. They are
just like other fish, excepting that they have no eyes
—they are useless in the awful darkness of that
boiling flood.
(SS?
Having exhausted the wonders of the caves, there
remains the panorama of the Blue Mountains—pic-
tures of vast distances seen from dizzy heights;
awful precipices dipping down sheer thousands of
feet; tumbling waterfalls, where the water sprays
out to the thin and beautiful texture of a veil waving
in the wind ; deep, cool recesses where ferns luxuriate
in everlasting moisture. Weeks can Vie spent in the
Blue Mountains entertainingly and inexpensively.
Less than 24 hours’ journey (12 of which can be
spent in the sleeping car) will take the tourist to
Mt. Kosciusko, the highest mountain in Australia,
and the oldest in the world. The next oldest is Mt.
Ruwenzori, in British Imperial East Africa. This
towering mass of grey granite is quite inaccessible, to
ordinary tourists in the winter, although huge
crowds go there to take part in the skiing carnivals
around its base. There is also a Government Accom-
modation House there, under the same excellent
management as that of the House at National Park,
Sydney. In summer time the air is wonderfully
bracing.
These are but a few of the pleasant places in which
the tourist to Sydney may have a delight-
ful time. The whole of the steamers of
the Five Companies concentrate on
Sydney, and to the man in Tas-
mania, North Queensland,
Westralia, South Australia,
and Victoria, there is no
better place than Syd-
ney in which to spend
a holiday. And in
the most part it is
cheapness itself,
and the fares by
any of the Five
Companies are
fixed at suchalevel
that one really
pays only for first-
class hotel accom-
modation, and gets
his travelling
thrown in for
nothing.

CONSTIPATION
is the most common and fruitful cause of suffering, especially
among women. It may arise from a variety of causes, but is
most frequently due to a torpid liver. When the liver becomes
torpid and inactive the bowels become sluggish and constipated,
foul and noxiops gases are absorbed into the system, poisoning
the blood a.nd nerve centres, and producing headaches, languor,
difficulty in breathing, sleeplessness, and general disorder of
the system. Drastic purgatives don’t cure constipation
may give temporary relief, but do not remove the cause*-

A CURED WOMAN
Relates her Intense Sufferings—how Doctors
Failed to do her any Good. She Finally Tried
Clements Tonic
Which Makes Permanent Cure.
(BY A SYDNEY REPORTER.)
Here is a narrative which will assuredly put many a reader on the right track—supplied to us by
Mrs. Annie Young, of Myrtle-street, Leichhardt. It was at Mrs. Young’s present address* where
she has been living for the past eleven years, that our special correspondent was granted the inter-
view which appears hereunder :
“It is thirteen years since I had the illness you refer to. It doesn’t seem half so long ago, for
everything is as fresh in my memory as though it was only yesterday.”
“Were you experiencing any physical pain ? ”
“Oh ! don’t talk about it. Have you ever had neuralgia in the head ? I hope not, for it was
something dreadful. Right across the forehead, through from one temple to the other, and up to the
top of my head, the most acute pain imaginable harassed me. The agony was so great that often I
imagined something was pressing on my brain, and I really used to think I would go mad with it.
It had such an effect on me, indeed, that even my sight went bad, and, as for the feeling in my eyes
—well, they felt as if they were double the size they should have been. Ah ! what desolate, melan-
choly days they were ! I’m sure I felt myself to be about the most unhappy woman in the world,
and I think I had occasion to be, in view of the fact that the outlook was so black,”
“Do you mean that your prospects of getting strong again seemed remote?”
“ They did, indeed, for I had been attended to by several doctors, and the most they could do for
me was to give just a little relief now and then; but there was nothing lasting about it, and if I felt a
trifle better one day, I was sure to feel ten times worse the next, and the medicine, besides being so
ineffective, was always disagreeable to take. A strange thing was that I felt always so worn-out,
drowsy and exhausted —as if I was only fit to go somewhere and lie down and die—yet, when I made
an effort to sleep, do you think I could ? Not a bit of it. I’d twist and turn nearly the whole night
through, and when at last I dosed off the dreams I had were fearful.”
“ And you couldn’t get permanent relief from these symptoms through the medicine prescribed
for you?”
“No, unfortunately. But finally I got something to do me good. Clements Tonic it was, and
the best remedy I ever had in my life, too. Goodness, what a show it made of everything
else I had taken, for instead of only easing me a little occasionally, it went right to the base of my
infirmities, and lifted them out by the roots ! In addition to what I have already told you, I must
state that I was highly nervous—nobody could be more so, for I was actually afraid to stay in my own
house by myself, and was continually worrying myself about things that I imagined were going to
happen. Trouble whs always hanging over me, and I couldn’t get the idea out of my head —not until
I had been taking Clements Tonic for a space of time, when, quite mysteriously, I ceased brooding
over such silly things, and my mind became as happy as the day. Oh ! it was wonderful what an
altered woman Clements Tonic made me. Instead of being anxious and timid all day, I got as happy
and cheerful as possible—nothing frightened me, and my nerves were steady. It is because I know
so many nervous women that I deem it my duty to say what a splendid remedy Clements Tonic is for
that complaint, though, for that matter, it was equally beneficial to me in other ways. I used to get
pains down the small of my back and about my shoulders, also palpitation and pains in the region of
my heart, which made me think that something very serious was wrong with me there; but Clements
Tonic showed itself to be such a pain-removing medium that I can faithfully recommend it tb all
sufferers. Its regulating influences on the heart were extraordinary, and for making one’s sleep sound
and peaceful the same remedy was the only one to meet with my requirements. I wish I had used it
months before, for plenty of people told me what a grand medicine Clements Tonic was. The least I
can do is to assist my neighbors to throw off their afflictions, so you may publish my experiences in
any manner you like.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, ANNIE YOUNG, of Myrtle-street, Leichhardt, in the State of New South Wales, Commonwealth of
Australia, do solemnly and sincerely declare that I have carefully read the annexed document, and that it contains
and is a true and faithful account of my illness and cure by Clements Tonic, and also contains my full permission
to publish in any wa&'tw statements—which I give voluntarily, without receiving any payment; and I make
this solemn declarati&ftf conscientiously belieying the same to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of the Oaths
Act, 1900. ■' (Sgd.) ANNIE YOUNG.
Subscribed and declared at Petersham this fifth day of September, one thousand nine hundred and four,
before me, H. EVANS, J.P.
The Inky Way.
Even Brisbane Sun has now been “ enticed ” over to
Philp, and poet-politician Kenna has wiped his feet on the
mat and left the office in disgust.
* * * *
Adelaide Critic has again changed hands. Its new
editor and proprietor is Alexis Holtze, whose father is
Director of the Adelaide Botanical Gardens, while his
brother runs the Port Darwin Gardens and does other
odd jobs in the Northern Territory. The paper has
hitherto been an echo of the bray of the National Ass,.
but it is understood that the Ass will now have its echo
amputated.
» * * *
A bright new weekly gleams forth in Melbourne on
February 2 next. Comments it will b« called, and it pro-
poses to raise ructions wherever it permeates. As a pre-
liminary, it will refuse absolutely to make the general
error of mistaking Tummas Bent for Cincinnatus, Caligula,
J. Carruthers, or any other bygone notoriety of the sort.
In fact, the journal intends to treat the Yic. Premier as
just plain Bent when he gets into his anti-Federal and
parish-minded mood. Comments will be strongly Federal-
ist, and will denounce State Frighters and all their works,
which is a sufficient reason why good Australians should
support it.
* * * *
“ Philo ” : A novel little publication is the organ of the
Fort-street (Sydney) Public School— The Fortian. The
paper was established eight years ago by myself and a
few other pupils, who set up and printed the six small
pages of pica type at the school, stoutly refusing assist-
ance from anyone but the schoolboys themselves. It has
since increased to about three times its original size, and
has now a consistent circulation of 900 copies per month.
The uncontrollable tendency on the part of the boys to
accelerate the disposal of “ pie” by depositing it by the
handful in the cases, in preference to the slower method
of “ dissing,” has forced the editor to get the setting
done away from the school, but the boys still do the
printing. The pupils are encouraged to write stories and
articles for the paper, and are paid for accepted matter.
Altogether our baby-reptile contemporary, The Fortian,
is a highly creditable sheet.
* * * *
“C.”: “Plain English” par. (B. 28/11/’O7) says that
£2OO a year as the wage of a senior reporter is rapidly
coming into sight. Coming? Why, I know a metro-
politan daily, largely owned by a very wealthy absentee,
which has been paying its “gallery” writer 10s. a day
for an original column summarising the Parlia-
mentary proceedings. He takes his seat in the gallery at
3.30 p.m. and remains to the close of each sitting—which
sometimes means ior 5 a.m. He chucked it when they
asked him to fill in his “ spare ” time in the mornings
“ doing ” the police courts.
* * * *
“ Snell ” : Talking of the great expectation of adver-
tisers, how is this? I was part owner and section editor
of The Ant, also assistant publisher, head stapler, and
supernumerary gas engine and canvasser. One day an
angry man came in and wanted his money back. He
said he was broke, and must have the pecuniary con-
sideration. He was prepared to break the office if he
didn’t get it. The staff did not recollect him. (I was
the staff.) He explained. Thirty years earlier in
Ireland he lost a brother, with red hair and a notch in
his left eyelid. He had paid ten shillings for four inch
advertisements in The Ant, calling upon that brother to
return and all would be forgiven. Being adjacent, and
short, he had dropped in to recover his ten shillings.
The staff explained that money was never returned.
“What?” yelled the advertiser, “not give me me
money ? I don’t get me money back ? Why, ye dirty
imposthor, the man I got wasn’t me brother at all l”
* * * *
“Isla ” : Brisbane Daily Mail, which was established
some five years ago for the purpose of lifting Philp, Leahy
and party back to power, isn’t feeling too well, and orders
have been issued to cut down expenses—which means
that the reporting staff is to consist mainly of youngsters
at about £1 per week. The paper’s first editor was the
ancient Buzacott. He lasted two years ; then they made
him consulting editor and leader writer, and sub-editor
Stumm stepped up. Stumm worried through with the
directors until early this year, when Asher, of the S.M.
Herald’s Parliamentary staff, was hurried north. Asher,
however, quickly found himself up against the directorate,
and promptly stepped out. Now F. O. Woosley, previously
leader of the reporting staff, goes to the chair, on which
he has long kept an eye. If he can’t “ hit it” with the
directors, they will probably call up the boy who pastes
the wrappers. The Waily Tale is a depressed, grave-
digging sort of paper to work for.
THE LAND OF OAN’T-BE-DONE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Most unnecessarily the maker of pianos has to pay duty
on his raw material. ... No one would suggest for a
moment that Australia can produce sxich materials.
Why, then, handicap the industry by making them duti-
able ?—Argus (3/12/’O7).
Pore' gran’ma’s bones are brittle, and her back is bowed,
and she
Sees nothing: now beyond her nose—and, Lor’ 1 what
does she see?
Yes, though the world roars in her ear
The message of the age,
She’s gone stone-deaf—she’ll never hear,
But, in her crazy rage,
Though blind and deaf, she mumbles shreds of sour and
mildewed speech,
Nor ceases, day by day, her tattered gospel-truths to
preach.
The voice of young Australia says, “ We can do this, and
will 1
And it is done, or will be soon, while, all unknowing, still
Pore gran’ma, cackling, shakes her head,
Her agfed mouth a-grin,
And mutters how she’s always said
Sech doin’s was a sin !
And, maudlin-mad, she quakes and moans in imitation
fear :
“ Grow things to make things from ? O, no, it can’t be
done—not ’ere ! ”
With starving strangers hanging round our tinkered-up
back fence,
Here is a voice that’s crowded with uncommon common-
sense :
Pore gran’ma lifts her voice to speak
Her mind. Just hark to this,
In quavering tones that pipe and squeak
With senile emphasis :
“ We must ’ave population, but imported food’s too dear
For white kids ! Let the Hindu in—we can’t grow people
’ere! ”
Pore gran’ma’s bones are turned to chalk with utter age,
and they
Creak as she moves—her false, blonde hair is braided
Over grey ;
But still her voice-box, unimpaired
By years of preaching woe,
Turns out the sounds with which she scared
The foolish, long
Ah, since pore gran’ma’s blind and deaf, as all her mum-
blings tell,
Great pity is she hasn’t yet been stricken dumb as well!
VIC.

LIMERICKITIS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
On the tram and the train, on the ’bus and the boat,
You will hear it, both going’ and coming ;
In the pub and the club and ashore and afloat—
A perpetual humming and strumming !.
From the ponderous personage, proud in his prime,
To the little kids grouped in the gutter,
They are muttering over a doggerel rhyme,
And this is the matter they mutter:
“ There was a young man of Junee,
Who went, in a basin, to sea ;
The basin was broken,
And, by the same token ...
Ta-rumtiddy, dumtiddy, dee !
N.B.W.
W. T. GOODGE.

FROOTOIDS
For Headache, Indigestion,
Constipation, and Biliousness.
OOO
The immense number of orders for
Frootoids, sent by post direct to the Pro-
prietor, is convincing proof that the
Public appreciate their splendid curing
power over the above-named complaints.
They are elegant in appearance, pleasant
to take, and, what is of the utmost
importance, are thoroughly reliable in
affording quick relief.
Frootoids are immensely more valu-
able than an ordinary aperient, in so far
that they not only act as an aperient,
but do remove from the blood, tissues, and
internal organs, waste poisonous matter
that is clogging them and choking the
channels that lead to and from them.
The beneficial effects of Frootoids are
evident at once by the disappearance of
headache, the head becoming clear, and
a bright, cheery sense of perfect health
taking the place of sluggish, depressed
feelings, by the liver acting properly, and
by the food being properly digested.
Frootoids are the proper aperient
medicine to take when any Congestion
or Blood Poison is present, or when Con-
gestion of the Brain or Apoplexy is pre-
sent or threatening. They have been
tested, and have been proved to afford
quick relief in such cases when other
aperients have not done any good at all.
It is of the utmost importance that this
should be borne in mind, for in such
cases to take an ordinary aperient is to
waste time and permit of a serious ill-
ness becoming fatal.
Frootoids act splendidly on the liver,
and quickly cure bilious attacks that
“antibilious pills” make worse. Many
people have been made sick and ill by
“antibilious pills” who could have been
cured at once by Frootoids. People
should not allow themselves to be duped
into contracting a medicine-taking habit
by being persuaded to take daily doses
with,each meal of so-called indigestion
cures that do NOT cure. Frootoids
have been subjected to extensive tests,
and have in every case proved success-
ful in Completely curing the complaints
named.
The ordinary adult dose of Frootoids,
of which there are 72 in a bottle, is 2 to
4 —more or less as required—taken, pre-
ferably at bedtime, when constipated,
or at the commencement of any other
disease requiring an aperient, as an
auxilliary with the special medicine
necessary for the case. A constipated
habit of body will be completely cured
if the patient will on each occasion,
when suffering, take a dose of Frootoids,
instead of an ordinary aperient; making
the interval between the taking of each
dose longer and the dose smaller. The
patient thus gradually becomes indepen-
dent of Aperient Medicines.

TO KEEP YOU SICK!

THE' first impulse of persons who find that they are ailing in any way is to
plunge into a chemist’s and purchase a box of pills or a bottle of some patent
medicine. . . Failing to get more than temporary relief from this they then
consult a doctor, and he writes a prescription which more than likely calls for the
same medicine, only in a different form from what the patient has already taken.
The same result, generally. Next, the patient comes to the conclusion that this
doctor didn’t diagnose the case right, and then goes to another, with the same
result. And so on.
Our modern medical system is a foolish system. We practically pay
our doctors to keep us ill. Their prosperity depends not on our health,
BUT ON OUR SICKNESS! . . . little is known of medicine
and its use. The wisest doctor can do but little more than clear out
the system or accelerate the action of the heart. When he has done these
things he has reached the end. MEDICINE CANNOT MAKE STRENGTH.
Now, just study yourself for a moment. The failure or breaking down of any
organic function means that it has not strength to do its work. If there was any
nourishment in medicine it might bring about a healthy change, but you know there
is not. Drugs, medicines, stimulants, narcotics and antidotes are POISONS, not
food. Food is needed to build up new energy. Electricity is nerve food, nerve life.
No. pain or debility can exist in a body charged with Electric Fife; you can have
no Weakness, no Inactive Parts, no Rheumatism, Indi-
gestion, Constipation or Kidney Trouble, if you fill your
body with it, because the life generated by this grand force gives health and
strength to every organ, and
Dr. MCLAUGHLIN’S ELECTRIC BELT
is the newest and most successful method of applying it. It is a series of small batteries which you wear about your waist.
It worhs while you sleep* and generates its power constantly. It is worn as comfortably as your clothing,
adjusted in a minute, and made as strong or mild as you like by the pressure of a finger upon the regujator. With it
you can carry the current direct to the spot affected. You can get the full current at your ankles, feet, arms; shoulders,
head or any other part of your bodyf’ It has a Special Attachment for men. We have fixed a price for it which puts it
within the reach of everyone. Its price is as low as £1 in many cases.

The Review, a quaint Sydney paper,
came out lately with an article stating
that “New Zealand” is a clumsy, inconvenient
name and should be replaced with something
better and handier. Then it offered a series of
suggestions that were dreadful in their banality.
They included Zealandia, which is just the foolish
old name with a slight variation; Nelsonia
(presumably after Nelson—and there are already
22 Nelsons in the office gazetteer); Hobsonia (after
Hobson’s Choice or Hobson’s Bay or something);
Alberta (a plain steal.from Canada); and Egmont
(there are four places bearing this Dutch name
already and the Review would fain make
five). And all the time the country has
already been re-named, though the belated Review
perhaps doesn’t know it. The Bulletin re-
christened it more than 15 years ago by the
musical name of Maoriland, which is its own
name—not a,left-off, worn-out, used-up, irrelevant
Dutch cognomen whereof the repetition would
make confusion more dizzy and confounded than
it is already. And so well has the name caught
on that you could address a letter to Maoriland
and post it in England, and it would reach its
destination, whereas that same letter wouldn’t
find Hobsonia between now and the general
resurrection of the dead. . Could anything be
more bathetic than Hobsonia—the capital of which
would naturally be Jqbsonville on the Dobbs
River? Why not call the place Hobnail right off?
“ J.A.C.” : Seven is a remarkable number.
There are the Seven Champions of Christendom,
the Seven Wise Men of Greece, the Seven
Wonders of the World, the Seven Ages of Man,
the Seven Mortal Sins, the Seven Sleepers, the
Seven Sorrows of the Virgin, the Seven Stars, the
Seven Years’ War, and the Seven Years’
“stretch.” Now a Dr. Williams, recently called
as a witness at Marylebone (London) Police
Court, insists that there are seven stages of
drunkenness. He defines them as follows: (1)
The communicative; (2) the irritable; (3) the
beatific ; (4) the bellicose; (5) the affectionate;
(6) the lachrymose; and (7) the comatose. You
can have the course for about Is. 9d.
A powerful religionist, or a wild humorist,
wrote recently to Melbourne Herald, signing
himself “ Atnen ”
As a Christiiin of the old school, I believe in the old
methods of dealing- with heretics like spiritualists and
their mediums. I say enforce not only the penal laws
against them, but burn, hang, flog, dragonade, and use
every conceivable form of torture against them.
I would also use the thumb-screw, pull out their nails,
and rub pepper on their eyelids, or any other of the good
old methods likely to prove efficacious.
As a Christian man, it is pleasing to me to know that even
death will not release them of their tortures, for they are
doomed to Hell fire for ever more.
Good old Hell! How would the world get on
without, it?


MICK SIMMONS
HaymarKet, Sydney, N.5.W.

The Monk and the Faun.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IT ceased, the urgent matin bell.
Fra Angelo, with downward glance,
Thrice crossed himself, then left his cell
(It happened at the Renaissance)
To grub the garden round the well.
His girdle hempen, sandals thin,
And tonsured pate, alas, entangled
With thoughts of death and mortal sin ;
A mattock through his arm, where dangled,
Down past his knees, a discipline.
Birds warbled in the trembling air
That blessed the land with lilac breath ;
Ah God ! this world was wondrous fair.
But brooding deep on sin and death
Not God’s joy moved him unaware.
Telling black beads he paced the lawn
Till, near the holy well, ’mid herbs,
Mint, thyme, and stocks, there lurked, withdrawn
From shovelled soil his foot disturbs
With sly grimace, a marble faun.
A wee wood fay half-turned aside,
Greek, flawless—by an artist carved—
Whose eyes and rude lips opened wide
To see and taste life, nor soul starved,
Nor healthy body mortified.
For once he danced with brows rose-crowned
In that old wicked world where Pan
Piped in the wild wood, limbs embrowned,
Naked, with wave and wind—and man
Rejoiced with satyrs gathered round.
Why should he fast—he mused—askance
At joy, stew Latin, bark weak shins
With clumsy mattock ? Song and dance,
Flutes, nymphs and roses, pricked wine skins —
That faun’s religion meant romance.
But muttering Aves, head bent low,
Perturbed to face Temptation’s tricks,
To save his soul Fra Angelo
Clasped tight his wooden crucifix ;
Yet one doubt gnawed him, even so.
Nymphs yet might lurk in stream or tree;
From sculptured fountain faun or Pan
Look out, tell Bacchus’ rosary
Petal by petal, luring man
To love earth, life, flesh, vanity.
For grace and strength he prayed to God,
His jealous Hebrew God, whose ire
Was not appeased till, like some clod,
That brown faun, which was earth’s desire,
He buried safe beneath the sod.
Yet, oft at dusk, day’s duty done,
He capers, faun like, hails the worth
Of fleshly things, remembering one
Who loved the earth, the ripe, sweet earth,
And loved the sun, the laughing sun.
VIC.
LOUIS ESSON.
The Shepherd Kings of
Canterbury.
THE ROUT OF THE KINGS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
BY the irony of fate the rout of the Kings
fell most heavily upon the best family of
the caste, the Leighs of Rangihape. In
the early days the family had made much money
by the simple process of removing wool from
where Nature had placed it into bales, which
were shipped to England. But when the hustling
Roland returned home from a trip round the
world, he began to exploit the forest which, till
then, had remained practically unexplored, be-
cause the only time the old man tried to penetrate
it, it took him two hours to get into it and two
days to get out again. Being a wise man, he
reckoned that, where his superior intelligence got
bushed for two days, anyone else would remain till
the morning of tfye Resurrection. With the return
of the heir a new era began. He pierced the
depths of the forest with roads, and made wooden
tramways through its gloomy recesses, and erected
a sawmill. A township sprang up on the out-
skirts of the bush, and the roads that radiated
from it north, south, and east were covered with
waggons of timber and carts piled high with fire-
wood.
One day foreman Raikes, spying out the land
for new supplies of timber, blundered through a
belt of ribbonwood, and found himself standing
on the edge of a rolling expanse of beautiful land,
covered with the short green native grass, with
never a sign of a tussock in it. Through the
midst of this plain of Beulah flowed a stream that
rippled over shingle bars, through miniature
forests of golden-blossomed kowai, and wilder-
nesses of lovely giant moss. Beyond the creek
the grass land swept away to the line of a second
forest, four miles off, the dark purple tint showing
it to be mostly of black pine. Through it gleamed
thin veins of red, due to the presence of the rata
blossoms ; and the red-lined expanse swept over
the first slope of the Southern Alps, and surged
right away to where the snow gleamed like silver
in the, sunlight.
As it was all in the Leigh grant, Roland Leigh
rewarded Raikes with a cheque for a year’s wages.
The grass land was very valuable, but the explora-
tion of the new forest could stand over till
the old one showed signs of being worked
out. The only people interested in it were the
twins, Arthur and Harold. They decided to
explore it thoroughly at midsummer vacation.
* * * *
At the end of an exciting three weeks they
returned home with tattered clothing, bronzed
skins, and several inches of increased chest girth.
It was twilight when they clattered into the
kitchen and put out of sight an amazing meal, and
then burst into their father’s den.
“Oh, we had a great time, sir,” said Arthur,
who always acted as spokesman. “Plenty of
shooting j but we never looked at anything but
pigs, and bagged over a dozen of them. Murray
has the tusks. We cut tracks all over the place ;
there’s millions of black pines, heaps of totara- —a
lot of white pine too. The second week we
found a big clearing about three miles across.
And in the middle there was a cone about
90 feet high, looking for all the world like a
model of Vesuvius. We scrambled to the top of
it and found it was an extinct volcano right
enough, for the chimney was still open. And we
brought back some samples of clay—Harold
swears the brown one is hematite.”
“ So it is ! ” said Harold, fiercely.
“We’ve made our fortunes right enough,” con-
tinued Arthur. “Just look at these; rubies,
sapphires, and nuggets. Got the stones in the
black shingle, and Harold jabbed the gold out
from between some rocks with his jack-knife. ”
He emptied out of a small bag on to the writing
table four thin water-worn nuggets, half-a-dozen
small red stones, and a few others that showed a
deep blue tinge. Roland picked them up and
examined them carefully. He seemed interested,
and told Arthur to hand him down a book on
“ Precious Stones, and How to Recognise Them,”
from the bookcase. The Twins and his father
watched him making elementary tests.
“The rubies are indisputable, but I don’t know
about these blue fellows, though I think they will
polish beautifully, and look something like aqua-
marines.”
As Arthur swept them back into the bag,
Harold pulled out of his inside pockets a handful
of curious white stones, which he laid before
bis brother reverently.
“Do you think these would polish, Rol ? I
thought they’d make a nice necklace for the
mater. Being off her own estate they’d be a
curio, you know,” he said apologetically.
Arthur scoffed.
“He thinks they’re diamonds—as though
diamonds were ever that size,” he laughed scorn-
fully, picking up a stone about the size of a
pigeon’s egg.
Harold blushed, but waited doggedly.
“No, Harold; this isn’t a diamondiferous
country, my boy. If you’d brought home that
number of rubies, now ! ”
“ But will they polish—if they will I’ll get ’em
made into a necklace for mother out of my own
money.”
‘ ‘ Of course they will—almost any stone of that
density and hardness will polish,” said Roland,
after testing one.
“He went fairly mad on those,” laughed
Arthur. “And I was dotty on the rubies and
blue ones. We went down the flue of the volcano
to see if we could get any more, but ”
“ Went down the chimney!” said Roland in
amazement.
“ Yes,” continued Arthur. “We tossed up a
shilling to see who’d go, and, of course, with his
beastly luck, Harold won. So I lowered him
down with a rata vine that we had brought from
the bush to measure the width of the chimney.”
The squatter shivered.
“ Do you mean to say you lowered your brother
down the chimney on a rotten rata and had him
dangliag over eternity ? ” he gasped.
“ Oh no, sir ; we could see the bottom, or what
we thought was the bottom. But it turned out to
be only a ledge about eight feet wide, and he
chucked stones over it to see how far the next step
was, for there was nothing there but basalt and
obsidian. He lay on his stummick, counting the
seconds on his watch till he should hear them
flop ; but they didn’t. So I threw him down
some brush, and, after tying it into a bundle with
his braces, he lit it and chucked it over. Lord
knows how deep it is, as it went on burning till it
seemed to strike the wall and disappear round a
corner or something ; and then ”
“My Creator ! ” gasped the squatter, rising
suddenly and, with savage energy, reaching down
his wife’s riding whip.
“That’s the sort of capers you get up to, is it ?
Take that—and that —and that, you young devils.
Stop them, Roland ! ”
But Roland was too slow, and the howling
youngsters vanished into the passage and roused
the house in their agony.
Had there been only half-a-dozen of the white
pebbles, Roland, who had spent a few months on
some diamond workings in Brazil, might have
examined them carefully. But that startling heap,
which would have declared a handsome dividend
on a capital of a quarter of a million, he did not
regard seriously. Still, after the Twins had gone
howling down the passage he found himself
strangely attracted by them. He examined them
carefully, referred to the book repeatedly, then
applied a number of tests.
“ They’re diamonds right enough, sir.”
“ Diamonds, eh ! ” said the old gentleman,
pushing his spectacles back on his forehead and
regarding his son fixedly.
“Yes, sir, diamonds. If there are any more,
we’re made. This lot alone is worth almost as
much as the station. By Jove, we’ll make this
country yet, sir ! I can see Rangihape diamond
mine in full blast; swarms of men working in the
open cuts, hear the machinery whirring. I can
see a Leigh ”
“Not so fast, Roland. They may be dia-
monds —I believe they are when you say so ; but
there may be no more there, or there may be
millions, as Arthur says, and you unable to strike
the spot. Ring for supper, my son. I’m getting
too old for that sort of thing, and I don’t think I
want to see any sort of a mine on my property.
It would disturb the sheep.”
All the same, Roland went straight to his
brothers’ room, and, informing them they had
really struck a diamond mine, promised to send a
parcel off the very next day to Amsterdam to be
cut, polished, and set in a necklace.
* * * *
The scattered squatters rallied their forces at
Rangihape ' homestead. Down in the ravine of
the Rangitata the triumphant Hori Grey waited to
gather the dead and wounded before pressing on
to deliver the final blow. As troop after troop
galloped up the wide carriage drive the first query
was as to the whereabouts of Roland Leigh.
“He’s dead,” said Colonel Mortimer. “I
saw him fall. ,He swore he wouldn’t retreat
before even Grey himself, and, damme, he didn’t.
I stayed till they prepared to give us the steel.
Their sharpshooters picked us off like rabbits.
