The line broke. I begged him to come away and
make another stand in the forest ; but he
wouldn’t. And then a bullet took him in the
throat and broke his neck. I saw' he was dead,
and left. Now, what are W'e going to do ?”
“ Whatever it is, it must be done quickly, for
unless we look for Grey he’ll seek us damned
suddenly. He doesn’t let the grass grow,” said
the master of Magnolia Grange.
“ What does Rangihape say ? ” inquired several,
referring to old Mr. Leigh.
They gathered in the billiard-room, and the
bent form of old Mr. Leigh w'as carried in and
placed in an easy chair at the end of the room.
Pie tried to speak, but could not. His son was
dead—a rebel and an outlaw, slain by the
Queen’s troops. (And he had cut his beard to a
point because the Prince of Wales wore his like
that.) His daughter, his favorite Helen, was in the
hands of the victorious Grey to be tried for slaying
her husband. He was crushed. He whispered
that they were to consider themselves and not
him. He was beyond even the terrible Hori
Grey—he was going to the Head Station. And
he spoke truly. It was a thousand times worse
than having his sheep disturbed, and that night
the old Shepherd King gave up the ghost amidst
the general ruin.
The squatters fired the tussock to hold Grey in
check while they decided what to do. The nor’-
west wind came down and drove the fire straight for
the Pro-Consul’s force, and that hardy fighter had
something else than the squatters to think of for
the rest of the morning.
Meanwhile the squatters wrangled fiercely. Old
Mortimer, who, in spite of Grey’s withering con-
tempt, really did possess some horse-sense, pro-
posed to retire into the forest and invite Grey ‘ ‘ to
poke them out.”
The master of Magnolia Grange was aggres-
sive. Why not follow' up the tussock fire and
treat Grey to a taste of his own medicine ! Pie
had surprised them that morning ; why not sur-
prise him that afternoon ? He had bluffed them
out of Christchurch a year ago ; why not see now
what his hand amounted to? They were on
their own plains, amidst their own shingle ridges,
and it would be easy enough to slip past him and
fall on his rear. If Grey was defeated Parlia-
ment would give good terms.
Burning with the memory of their former
humiliation and their rout in the Rangitata gorge,
the squatters cheered the idea lustily.
“Be damned !” snorted Mortimer. “He’s a
better soldier than all of us rolled into one, and
if you think you can surprise him, I don’t. I
think we ought to retire into the bush—he can’t
burn it, and he’d lose every man he has if he tried
to poke us out. Just keep him hung up, and
then w'e can make terms. Damme ! you don’t
suppose you can fight him, do you ?”
“ Why not ? ” chorussed a dozen voices.
“ Well, —just because he has the whole force
of the Empire behind him. That’s all—but it’s
enough. What we have to do is fight for the
best terms now. If we tackle him again, there’s
no hope of mercy. Confound it, I can feel the
rope round my neck already.”
“ How many sheep are there on Raikes’ Flat?”
asked Greville of The Wilderness, turning to the
Twins.
“Twelve thousand !” said Arthur.
“ That’s our commissariat,” said Greville, de-
cisively. “I vote with Mortimer. Tackling
him means a fight to the death, and I’ve seen
enough of that this morning. Leigh’s dead, his
sister’s captured, our old friend here is at the last
gasp —what the Hell more do you want ! There’s
nothing but our lives and property left to fight
for ; and we can put up a better fight in the bush
than outside.”
“ What do you say to that, Magnolia ? ” asked
somebody.
“ It’s safer than my plan, which depends upon
surprising him fighting a tussock fire. What
would happen if we wiped him out, or captured
him ? ”
“Aye, that’s it ! What would happen ? Why,
every man in the colony would be called out
against us, and, if necessary, Imperial troops
brought out. We’d be smashed first by sheer
force of numbers, and hung by dozens after-
wards,” howled Mortimer. “Are you going to
talk all day ? Let’s take a vote whether we retire
into the bush or attack Grey ; strikes me he will
attack us while we’re arguing ! ”
“ Which is exactly what he has done,” said
Mort, of Ocean Caves, as through the roar of the
wind came the rattle of rifle fire.
“ Keep cool,” said Mortimer. “ Hands up in
favor of attacking the man who’s tackling us out-
side now ? ”
None were lifted.
“ Hands up in favor of retiring into the bush,
and sticking out for the best terms ? ”
The room was filled with hands.
Mortimer dashed out, and in five minutes the
retreat into the bush had begun.
So fast did Grey advance through the smoke of
miles of burning tussock that the retreat be-
came a flight. He had advanced his men in two
lines, and they were enfilading the squatters’ men.
The raging wind made marksmanship impossible,
and although there was much noise there was
little damage. The last of the yeomanry entered
the forest in safety. Grey rushed the house
expecting to find it defended, but it was not. The
Twins, who had stayed to secrete their stock of
diamonds, escaped with difficulty.
* * * *
When he. had wasted a week trying to “ poke
them out,” Grey’s opinion of Mortimer rose some-
what. The old fellow did know something about
defending a position, if he knew nothing about
other branches of military business. Grey had
hauled out, and spent a day gazing yearningly at
the mountains, but there was no hope that way.
His one success w'as that he had captured the poor
little Twins, who had been trying to gain informa-
tion of their father.
Strangely enough this depressed Mortimer very
much. He asked for an armistice and a con-
ference. The Governor consented, and under safe
conduct pledges, Mortimer, Greville and Monk
entered the house.
Mortimer gazed interestedly at the long aristo-
cratic face, the picture of which he had carried in
his memory since that night in Christchurch when
he and his force were stampeded out of the city by
the smiling, cynical Pro-Consul.
He sat at the end of the dining table with his
secretary.
“ I think we have met before,” he said to
Mortimer, with an affable smile. “ Please intro-
duce me to your friends.”
Mortimer growled that they would sooner not
have their names mentioned till after the amnesty
was declared, at any rate. He opined that his
Excellency understood the position they were in.
His Excellency did, but he doubted very much
if the gentlemen themselves did. Did they repre-
sent the whole force outside ?
They did—they had stuck together so far ; they
would stick together to the end.
“ What are your grievances—why are you in
arms ? ” asked the Pro-Consul severely.
“We have no grievances, your Excellency ; we
are in auras solely out of loyalty to Roland
Leigh ! ”
“And his sister!” added Greville.
“ But Mr. Roland Leigh is dead. So is his
father. The lady is in Christchurch waiting her
trial. The other two sons are prisoners in this
house. Why are you still in arms ? ”
“ For the best terms, your Excellency. We
want to save our lives, but they are useless to us
without our property. Grant us these and we
march out and surrender. If we’re to be beggared
we might as well die ; and if we’re to die—-well,
better in the forest than in poverty. ”
And Mortimer stuck his hands into his pockets,
leaned back, and stared at the ceiling. Hori
Grey looked at Greville.
“I can only add, your Excellency, that we are
still a force in being, and are determined to play
expediency for all it is worth. We recognise that
the cause of the Leigh family is lost. All we ask
is to be allowed our lives and properties. We are
suing for mercy, sir.”
The Pro-Consul smiled, and turned his keen
but pleasant eye on Monk. Monk was a man of
telegraphic brevity, and he addressed the Pro-
Consul as “ Yer Ex.”
“That’s it, yer Ex. Cost £i oo,ooo and half
yer force to smash us right up. Cost yer nothing
to be merciful. Why not ? Can’t hang us all.
Can’t gaol us all. Not worth fighting to gaol a
score, hang a dozen, and beggar the whole
damned lot. Now, you tell us what you want us
to do, an’ we’ll do it, yer Ex. ! ”
“ Yer Ex. ” was equally brief. He was glad
to have met them, the leading spirits of the
most remarkable insurrection he had ever had to
deal with or had read of. He presumed that they
called it chivalry to litter the countryside with
innocent dead to prevent an aristocratic lady
standing her trial. He disagreed with that view',
and regarded it as treason and murder. However,
the question was what were they going to do. It
was impossible for the Crown to make terms with
them. He w'as there to smash them, and if they
remained in arms would do it. As he once before
remarked to his friend Colonel Mortimer—and he
was sure that Mr. Greville, of The Wilderness, and
Mr. Monk, ofßangitata, would agree with him —it
was a good general who knew when to get out.
They had a strong position, but with another 200
men he would undertake to capture or kill every
one of them. As time was valuable he did not
propose to bring up any more men, and would
assault their position with what troops he had the
following day, when everyone caught in arms
would be arraigned in accordance with the terms
of his proclamation. He had noticed the roads
to the north were better than those to the south,
and opined that it was due to the more regular
traffic to the north. He was glad to have met
Colonel Mortimer —as that gentleman had a good
grasp of one branch of warfare, and, ifhe remem-
bered rightly, he had promised him another audi-
ence. He W'ished them good afternoon. When
they had dined the guard would conduct them to
their own outpost.
Finding that the squatter force had taken the
hint and dispersed in haste along these good roads
that led to the north, Hori Grey hurried av*.y.
There w’as trouble with the Maoris in the other
island, and for the time he left the Shepherd
Kings to lie low and hide as they pleased. Before
he left, the Pro-Consul promised the Twins to use
his influence on their behalf, but warned them
that his“reign was nearing its end. It terminated
almost immediately, and the Twins found them-
selves at the mercy of a General Assembly that
was not in a generous mood. It had its hands
too full of native problems to be vindictive to the
squatters of Canterbury, but it was merciless to
the family that had caused the trouble. Rangihape
was confiscated, and sold piecemeal. Burning
with rage, and scared to death, the squatters
placed a sum of money at the disposal of the
Twins to repurchase at least a portion of it. The
boys insisted that the purchase should consist of
the homestead and that patch of barren land on
which the diamond mine was situated. Then
they waited anxiously for the arrival of the cut
gems from Amsterdam, and the news of illimit-
able wealth. That was the hope on which they
had gambled. They filled in the time gathering
as many of the diamonds as they could.
At last the package arrived. The stones had
been set as a necklace for the mother, who had
died at sea, and the Twins regarded the glorious
thing with tear-laden eyes. Their sister was in
Christchurch awaiting her third trial, and they sat
in their father’s office watching the scintillating
fire with the blue tinge in it that was to them the
dawning light of fresh fortune. Both fell into a
reverie, dreaming of the house of Leigh restored
to wealth and power. Through Arthur’s imagina-
tive brain rushed visions of the homecoming of
the scattered hands. He saw the long line of
carts w'inding between the sycamores and pines to
the cottages that had been done up and re-painted
for the occasion, and saw himself and brother
riding round to welcome them back, the old men
taking off their hats, the children cheering. He
saw his sister presiding over the feast in the home-
stead to celebrate a new era. Then the methodical
Harold broke the spell by opening a letter that
accompanied the necklace.
The letter was brief. Its burden was that the
Amsterdam firm had never met with zircons just
like these before.
And the possessions of the once great family of
the Leighs were a patch of shingle that had once
been called a diamond mine, an extinct volcano,
and a mansion so great that all the other assets of
the Twins would hardly buy it one coat of paint.
C. A. JEFFRIES.

ONCE upon a time there was a contractor who had a good job, but do what he could he found at the week’s end he was only just making expenses, with nothing for him-
self. He cudgelled his brains many times, trying to think out the cause of the trouble, but without success. All his men were good workers, for he paid good
wages, and so far as he could see there was nothing wrong with the work. An old friend who was also a contractor came along and stopped to have a chat one day
when he was feeling very despondent and miserable, as with all his care he had. been unable to give his wife any money for housekeeping for some weeks past. Or
course, the question of cost, etc., came up, and the friend said, “ Well, Bill, I can’t see what is wrong, you have a good job at a good price, and there is not mg wrong wi h
your men or the way in which you are doing your work. In mining and contracting the two chief items of expense are wages and explosives now, your wages aie a> rig it,
you have good men, and good men always earn their money ; what explosives are you using ? ” The contractor told him, and his friend immediately.clapped him on the shoulder
and said, “ Why, Bill, there’s where your trouble is, old man ; you go right away and tell your storekeeper to send you some ‘ Hervey s Rackarock, as in tins kind or rock you
will find it will do double the work. Well, old man, I must be going, but I expect you will have more cheerful news next time lam around. By the way, lie said, le
sure that ‘ Hervey’s Rackarock ’is stamped on every plug.” The contractor bid his friend good day, and, with a smile on his face, he hitched up his pants, whistled lor Ins
dog, and off to the store went he. Next day a supply of “ Hervey’s Rackarock ” was on the job. Some time after the contract was finished, the contiactoi met Ins friend
again, and Said, “ You solved my trouble, Tom, when you recommended ‘ Hervey’s Rackarock,’ as everything went Well after I started using it. At the week-end 1 found
I could give my wife all the money she wanted, and, after the job was finished, I was able to buy a terrace of houses. Come on, old man, it smy turn.
The moral of this little story may be obtained at any time at the corner of Clarence and King Streets, Sydney, N.S.W*
(Est. 1884. Tel. 618).
